---
##  INTRODUCTION TO THE BLUES VERSION OF THE LOTUS SUTRA

 A Blues Interpretation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese

## WHAT IS THIS INTERPRETATION?

This is a blues and gospel-inflected English interpretation of the Lotus Sutra
(Saddharma-puṇḍarīka-sūtra), one of the most revolutionary and influential
Buddhist texts. Rather than offering a literal or scholarly interpretation, this
version channels the wisdom of the Lotus Sutra through the spiritual,
rhythmic, and deeply human language of African American blues and gospel
tradition.

The source text is Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese version (406 CE), a
translation that itself was revolutionary in making Buddhist teachings
accessible to common people. This blues version continues that democratizing
tradition—bringing the Dharma into living, breathing language that speaks to
the soul.

## WHY BLUES?

The blues and the Lotus Sutra share a profound spiritual sensibility:
- Both speak to suffering and its transformation
- Both celebrate human dignity in the face of adversity
- Both offer a way out: through music/Dharma, through community, through trust
- Both are rooted in African American/Buddhist wisdom traditions that center
 on justice, compassion, and the liberation of ALL beings

The blues doesn't shy away from pain. Neither does the Lotus Sutra. But both
offer redemption, not escape. Both say: your suffering matters, your journey
matters, and you are worthy of enlightenment/freedom.

## GENDER-INCLUSIVE APPROACH

This interpretation is fully gender-inclusive while maintaining theological depth.
The original Lotus Sutra was revolutionary for its time in affirming that women
could achieve Buddhahood. This version amplifies that revolutionary message by:

- Using inclusive language ("brothers and sisters") in universal teachings
- Maintaining male narrative where it provides contrast for women's
 paradigm-shifting appearances (especially the dragon princess in Chapter 12)
- Centering women bodhisattvas (Avalokiteśvara, Mahāprajāpatī, the queen)
 as agents of compassion and power
- Honoring the text's original radical stance: women are not obstacles to
 enlightenment—they are its agents

## THE 28 CHAPTERS

The Lotus Sutra contains 28 chapters, each exploring a different dimension of
enlightenment. Early chapters establish the Buddha's core teaching (Chapters
1-7). Middle chapters extend enlightenment to all beings (Chapters 8-11).
Later chapters detail the work of Bodhisattvas in liberating all beings
(Chapters 12-28).

Read sequentially, these chapters build a powerful argument: enlightenment is
not reserved for monks, not limited by gender, not dependent on when you were
born. It is available to all beings, everywhere, always.

## HOW TO READ THIS VERSION

This blues interpretation is designed to be:
- SPOKEN ALOUD (the rhythm and vernacular shine in performance)
- STUDIED CAREFULLY (the Buddhist teachings are doctrinally accurate)
- SAVORED FOR BEAUTY (blues poetry at its finest)
- EXPERIENCED COMMUNALLY (share it, discuss it, sing it)

The verse sections use blues rhythm, call-and-response patterns, and the
spiritual depth of African American preaching traditions. The prose sections
use accessible vernacular English while maintaining the elevated and reverent
tone appropriate to sacred scripture.

## ABOUT THE SOURCE TEXT

Kumārajīva (343-413 CE) was a Buddhist missionary who translated over 100
Buddhist texts into Classical Chinese. His version of the Lotus Sutra became
the standard text throughout East Asia. He was known for making texts
accessible without sacrificing accuracy—the same goal of this blues version.

The text's 28 chapters were compiled from earlier Sanskrit versions and
represent centuries of Buddhist reflection on enlightenment, compassion, and
the radical possibility of universal salvation.

## KEY FIGURES IN THIS TEXT

- ŚĀKYAMUNI BUDDHA: The historical Buddha who teaches throughout
- ŚĀRIPUTRA: Chief disciple, represents intellectual understanding
- MAÑJUŚRĪ: Bodhisattva of Wisdom
- AVALOKITEŚVARA: Bodhisattva of Compassion (appears in many forms)
- THE DRAGON PRINCESS: A revolutionary figure whose Buddhahood shatters
 expectations about gender and enlightenment
- THE SIXTEEN PRINCES: Future Buddhas who point toward universal salvation

## ESSENTIAL BUDDHIST TERMS USED

Bodhisattva (BO-dee-SAT-vuh): A being dedicated to liberating all others
Buddha-nature: The capacity for Buddhahood present in all beings
Dharma: The teaching; the way things are; the law of cause and effect
Tathāgata (tah-TAH-guh-tuh): "One who has thus gone"; epithet for the Buddha
Samādhi: Deep meditative concentration; unified awareness
Nirvāṇa: The cessation of suffering; enlightenment; peace beyond all concepts

## READING GUIDE

Each chapter includes:
- CHAPTER HEADING with number and title
- PROSE SECTIONS: Buddhist teachings in accessible blues-inflected vernacular
- VERSE SECTIONS: The same teachings in blues poetry and song form
- INTERPRETATION NOTES: Context and deeper meaning

The repetition of teachings (prose then verse) is intentional. This is how the
Sutra was transmitted orally for centuries—each repetition deepens the
understanding. Don't skip the verses as "just repetition." They are
transformation.

## PRONUNCIATION GUIDE FOR SANSKRIT NAMES

Śākyamuni = SHAHK-yuh-MOO-nee
Śāriputra = shah-REE-POO-truh
Mañjuśrī = muhn-JOO-shree
Avalokiteśvara = uh-vuh-LOW-kee-TESH-vur-uh
Mahākāśyapa = muh-HAH-KAH-shuh-puh
Ānanda = AH-nahn-duh
Maitreya = my-TRAY-uh
Mahāprajāpatī = muh-HAH-pruh-JAH-puh-tee

## ABOUT THIS EDITION

This is the ENHANCED EDITION of the blues interpretation, which includes:
- Comprehensive scholarly introduction (this text)
- Full chapter summaries for context
- Glossary of Buddhist and blues-specific terminology
- Performance notes for oral delivery
- Detailed study guide with discussion questions

The standard publication-ready version is also available for those wanting
the pure text without apparatus.

Why both versions? Some readers want the text itself without additional
materials. Others want scholarly context and study aids. This enhanced edition
serves students, performers, spiritual practitioners, and deep readers.

## FINAL NOTE

The Lotus Sutra ends with a profound image: the Bodhisattva of Universal
Worthy rides a white elephant in all directions, bringing the Dharma to all
beings. May this blues interpretation carry the Dharma to hearts ready to hear
it. May it liberate all beings, without exception.

Om mani padme hum.
(The jewel in the heart of the lotus.)

This blues interpretation of the Lotus Sutra (based on Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese translation from 406 CE) is an original work of interpretation and adaptation. The text, introduction, interpretation notes, glossary, indices, and all scholarly apparatus are the intellectual property of William Altig.

The Lotus Sutra itself (Saddharma-puṇḍarīka-sūtra) is an ancient Buddhist scripture in the public domain. This particular interpretation and arrangement, along with the scholarly apparatus, is protected by copyright.

**Permission for use:** Educational institutions, Buddhist centers, and scholarly researchers may quote passages in critical articles, reviews, and educational materials with proper attribution. Extended quotations (more than a few verses) require written permission from the interpretator.

## **SOURCE TEXT:**

This interpretation is based on Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese rendition (妙法蓮華經) from 406 CE, preserved in the Buddhist Canon (Tripiṭaka). The interpretation honors Kumārajīva's pioneering work in making Sanskrit Buddhist philosophy accessible through vernacular Chinese, while adapting the teachings into contemporary African American blues and gospel idiom.

**Original Sanskrit Text:** Saddharma-puṇḍarīka-sūtra (Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma), compiled approximately 1st-2nd century CE

**Classical Chinese Translation:** Kumārajīva (瞿曇鳩摩羅什), translator, 406 CE

**English Blues Interpretation:** William Altig, 2025

This blues interpretation of the Lotus Sutra represents his attempt to honor both traditions by allowing them to speak to and through each other.

### Interpreter's Note: The Dharma and the Blues

Interpreter's Note: The Dharma and the Blues
This interpretation takes Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese text of the Lotus Sutra and pours it through the blues—that great river of African American spiritual and musical expression that has always known how to carry deep wisdom in everyday language.
Some might ask: Why?
The answer is that these two great traditions, born worlds apart, share a spiritual DNA.
Both emerge from the raw, unflinching recognition of suffering. The blues is born from slavery, oppression, and the long midnight of human trouble. Buddhism is born from the recognition of dukkha—the truth that this conditioned world is a "burning house." Both traditions refuse to look away.
And yet, both insist on joy despite the suffering. The blues can laugh through tears. The gospel tradition shouts for joy in the face of despair. The Lotus Sutra promises that even in the burning house, a "great cart" of inconceivable beauty is waiting for us.
This interpretation is an attempt to honor that shared DNA. The blues has always carried sacred truth in the language of the people. It’s a tradition that understands:
The "loving tricks" of a compassionate father (upāya).
The "poverty consciousness" of the prodigal son who forgot his inheritance.
The "hidden dignity" of the jewel sewn in the pauper's robe.
The "mathematics of grace" where one moment of faith outweighs eons of works.
The "prophetic faith" of "Never Looked Down," who keeps declaring "You gonna be a Buddha" even as the stones are flying.
This is not a formal, academic interpretation. This is an interpretation for the heart. I kept the famous names—Mañjuśrī, Avalokiteśvara, Śāriputra—and the original 28-chapter structure. But I've let abstract terms become lived experience. "Arhat" becomes a brother who’s "made it through to the other side." "Nirvāṇa" becomes "that final peace beyond peace." "Dhāraṇī" becomes "a power to hold onto the truth no matter how hard the wind blows."
The verses, especially, are rendered to be felt, to be sung. They use the repetition, the rhythm, and the direct address ("Listen now," "you hear me?") of the blues sermon.
Because that’s what this sutra is. It's a revelation, a promise, and a call. It’s the testimony that the world is on fire, and still we sing.
My hope is that this voice allows the Dharma to be heard in a new way, in the key that sang freedom in chains and prophesied justice in the dark. May all beings hear the teaching in the voice that reaches their hearts.

About gender in this interpretation: You'll notice the text maintains male characters where they're specific—the Buddha, Śāriputra, Mañjuśrī, and the other historical disciples. This is deliberate. Because this sutra's power doesn't come from pretending women were always fully centered in a male-dominated world. It comes from showing women breaking into that narrative, claiming their place, and in doing so, shattering the categories entirely. When that dragon girl becomes a Buddha, it matters because the story up to that point has been mostly men. When Mahāprajāpatī demands recognition, it carries weight because the Buddha had to think about it. I've kept that narrative reality. But in the universal teachings, in the addresses to all practitioners, in the parables that apply to every living being—there I've made sure the language includes everybody. The dharma doesn't have gender restrictions, so the language addressing 'all beings' shouldn't either. This is a interpretation that lets the Sutra's own revolution speak: first you think this is a man's world, then you discover it's not, and that discovery IS the teaching.

William Altig
Houston, Texas
October 2025

### Scholarly Introduction

Scholarly Introduction

The Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma (Saddharma-puṇḍarīka-sūtra), transmitted to the world through Kumārajīva's groundbreaking Classical Chinese translation in 406 CE, stands as one of Buddhism's most transformative and widely influential texts. Originally compiled in Sanskrit, likely during the 1st-2nd century CE, the Lotus Sutra emerged as a pivotal innovation in Buddhist philosophy—one that fundamentally challenged prevailing interpretations of enlightenment and democratized the promise of Buddhahood across all living beings without exception.

The text's central revolutionary claim contradicted centuries of Buddhist interpretation: the highest spiritual attainment (Buddhahood itself) was not reserved for a select few dedicated monks or metaphysically exceptional beings, but was the ultimate destiny of every sentient creature. This radical universalism reframed the relationship between the historical Buddha and all beings, positioning him not as a distant teacher but as a compassionate father figure employing infinite loving tricks (upāya) to guide his children toward their inherent Buddha-nature.

In East Asian Buddhist traditions, the Lotus Sutra achieved preeminence. It became the foundational text of Tendai Buddhism in Japan, the basis of Nichiren's sect, and a beloved scripture throughout China, Korea, and Vietnam. Its 28 chapters span cosmological narratives, doctrinal expositions, magnificent parables, and practical guides for cultivation—creating a multivalent text that has sustained centuries of interpretation and devotion.

This blues interpretation represents a deliberate methodological choice: to render Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese version into the vernacular idiom of African American blues and gospel tradition. This approach honors two traditions simultaneously. First, it recognizes Kumārajīva's own pioneering work—a Central Asian Buddhist translator who himself chose to render Sanskrit philosophy into the living language of 5th-century Chinese speakers rather than preserving untranslatable Sanskrit terminology. Second, it acknowledges the profound theological and musical resonances between Buddhist understanding of suffering and redemption and the African American spiritual tradition born from slavery, oppression, and the persistent prophetic affirmation of human dignity and divine promise.

The interpretation preserves the text's essential structure (all 28 chapters) and maintains Sanskrit names with proper diacritical marks to honor the original tradition. However, it prioritizes accessibility and embodied comprehension over literal word-for-word rendition. Theological concepts become lived metaphors: emptiness becomes "nothing to hold onto," enlightenment becomes "waking up to who you really are," and nirvāṇa becomes "that final peace beyond peace." The result is a text meant to be heard and felt—one that invites the reader into the cadences and wisdom-traditions of the blues sermon while maintaining fidelity to the Sutra's profound Buddhist philosophy.

This annotated scholarly edition includes 28 chapters of interpretation, 28 detailed interpretation notes explaining key concepts and interpretation decisions, Sanskrit/Chinese term indices, and comprehensive glossary support. It is intended for scholars of Buddhism, interpretation studies, and African American religious traditions, as well as for general readers seeking access to this transformative text through the medium of their own cultural and spiritual idiom.

## CHAPTER SUMMARIES: A GUIDE TO THE 28 CHAPTERS OF THE LOTUS SUTRA

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

1. THE OPENING (Introduction; Chinese: 序品)
The entire assembly gathers on Vulture Peak Mountain to hear the Buddha teach. Described are the twelve thousand monks, eighty thousand bodhisattvas, heavenly beings, dragons, and all manner of divine creatures. The Buddha enters profound meditation, and light radiates from his body. Mañjuśrī the Wisdom-Carrier explains to Maitreya that the Buddha is about to reveal a great teaching. Central theme: The universality of the gathering and the extraordinary preparation for what is about to be revealed.

2. THE LOVING TRICKS (Expedient Means; Chinese: 方便品)
The Buddha begins by explaining that all Buddhas use "loving tricks"—adapted teachings suited to different people's capacities. The three "vehicles" (teachings for listeners, solitary realizers, and bodhisattvas) are all actually one vehicle in disguise. The metaphor of the burning house opens: a father uses three carts (a sheep, a deer, a white ox) to entice his children out of a burning house, then gives them all a magnificent white cart instead. This teaches that the Buddha uses whatever teachings will save beings, knowing one great vehicle awaits them all. Central theme: The doctrine of loving tricks justifies the apparent contradictions in Buddhist teachings.

3. THE PARABLE (A Parable; Chinese: 譬喻品)
Śāriputra hears the opening teaching and suddenly understands that all beings will become Buddhas. The Buddha expands the burning house parable into a cosmic story: the Buddha is the father, the world is on fire (with suffering), and all the different teachings are just different carts to get beings out. A man finds a jeweled garment hidden in his robe—he had possessed it all along without knowing. All beings possess Buddha-nature; they've just forgotten. Central theme: The jewel represents the inherent Buddha-nature that all beings already possess.

4. FAITH AND UNDERSTANDING (Belief and Understanding; Chinese: 信解品)
Four great elders—Subhūti, Mahākāśyapa, Mahā-Kātyāyana, and Mahā-Maudgalyāyana—suddenly understand. They describe their journey: thinking they'd reached the final goal (nirvāṇa/Arhatship), they'd stopped seeking. Now they realize that was just a resting place, and the real destination is Buddhahood for all. The Buddha grants them prophecies. The prodigal son parable: a poor man, ashamed of his poverty, rejects his wealthy father. The father patiently waits, eventually gives him small work, gradually reveals his wealth, and finally makes him heir. Central theme: The slow path of redemption and how the Buddha patiently guides all beings toward their true inheritance.

5. THE PARABLE OF THE MEDICINAL HERBS (Chinese: 藥草喻品)
The Buddha explains that just as different plants receive the same rain but grow to different sizes, all beings receive the same teaching but develop according to their capacity. Different medicines heal different diseases—the teaching is adapted to what each being needs. Mahākāśyapa and the assembly receive prophecies of their future Buddhahood. Central theme: The universal teaching works for all, but effects vary according to individual capacity and karma.

6. THE NAMING (Bestowal of Prophecies; Chinese: 授記品)
The Buddha formally predicts the future Buddhahood of 2,000 Arhat disciples. Each receives a prophecy customized to their character and spiritual achievements. The prophecies describe their Buddha-lands, their teaching styles, their names in future ages. Central theme: Individual prophecies show that enlightenment is guaranteed for each being in their own time and way.

7. THE PHANTOM CITY BLUES (A Parable of the Phantom City; Chinese: 化城喻品)
The Buddha and a great leader of bodhisattvas discuss an extended parable: a group of people journey toward treasure across a terrible, dangerous desert. A wise guide creates a phantom city as a resting place, allowing the exhausted travelers to recover. Once recovered, the guide says, "We can continue now"—the real treasure is much greater. This phantom city is nirvāṇa (the traditional goal), but the real treasure is Buddhahood. Central theme: Even the highest traditional attainments are way-stations on the path to supreme enlightenment.

8. WHEN THE FIVE HUNDRED FINALLY HEARD THEIR NAMES CALLED (Chinese: 五百弟子受記品)
Five hundred disciples who had previously believed they'd achieved final liberation suddenly understand they too will become Buddhas. The Buddha describes their past lives and predicts their future Buddhahood. Central theme: Even those who thought they'd finished their journey are promised supreme enlightenment.

9. WHEN THE FAITHFUL GET THEIR DUE (Prophecies for Learners and Adepts; Chinese: 授記品)
Ānanda and Rāhula, the Buddha's cousin and son, receive prophecies. Eight thousand nuns also receive the promise of Buddhahood. The Buddha addresses the anxiety that had arisen when he promised universal enlightenment: "Don't worry—your individual path is honored." Central theme: Even those closest to the Buddha and those thought to be less spiritually capable all receive prophecies of Buddhahood.

10. HONOR THE WORD-CARRIERS (Dharma Teachers; Chinese: 法師品)
The Buddha exalts those who teach, recite, write, and respect this Lotus Sutra. They become like the Buddha himself—worthy of support and honor. Five hundred bodhisattvas vow to spread the teaching. Central theme: Those who carry this message into the world become Buddhas.

11. WHEN THE JEWELED TOWER ROSE UP (The Jeweled Stupa; Chinese: 見寶塔品)
A jeweled stupa (tower) emerges from the earth, containing the body of a Buddha from the distant past. The ancient Buddha's voice comes from the stupa, praising the Lotus Sutra. This demonstrates that the teaching is supported by all Buddhas throughout time and space. Central theme: The teaching is eternal and trans-temporal—confirmed by Buddhas across all worlds.

12. WHEN YOUR ENEMY WAS YOUR TEACHER (Devadatta; Chinese: 提婆達多品)
The Buddha reveals that Devadatta, his treacherous cousin who tried to split the sangha and kill him, will ultimately become a Buddha. Even evil-doers receive prophecies of Buddhahood. A precocious eight-year-old dragon-girl instantly becomes a Buddha through the power of this teaching. Central theme: The most unlikely beings—enemies, non-humans—all achieve Buddhahood through understanding this sutra.

13. WHEN THE STORM COMES DOWN, YOU GOTTA KEEP STANDING (Exhortation to Maintain; Chinese: 勸持品)
The Buddha exhorts all bodhisattvas to uphold, read, recite, teach, and protect the Lotus Sutra. He promises supernatural powers and protections to those who maintain this teaching against persecutions. Central theme: Commitment to the dharma brings protection and spiritual power despite external challenges.

14. HOW TO KEEP YOUR SOUL CLEAN WHEN THE WORLD'S GONE WRONG (The Peaceful Practices; Chinese: 安樂行品)
The Buddha describes four "peaceful practices" for protecting oneself while teaching in a corrupt age: peaceful body, peaceful speech, peaceful mind, and peaceful vows. These allow dharma teachers to maintain their integrity despite hostile conditions. Central theme: Spiritual practice isn't about withdrawing but about wise engagement with the world.

15. WHEN THE UNDERGROUND ARMY ROSE UP (Springing Up from the Earth; Chinese: 從地湧出品)
Countless bodhisattvas suddenly emerge from beneath the earth—they've been practicing in hidden Buddha-lands, preparing for this teaching. The Buddha describes bodhisattvas who will teach the Lotus Sutra in the future. Central theme: Bodhisattva practice extends throughout time and space; help comes from unexpected sources.

16. HOW LONG THE TATHĀGATA'S BEEN ALIVE (The Buddha's Eternity; Chinese: 如來壽量品)
The Buddha reveals his true nature: he hasn't simply lived one lifetime (the "historical" Buddha). He's been enlightened for immeasurable eons. His appearance of birth and death is loving tricks—he manifests, teaches, and appears to pass away to create urgency in beings. This is the sutra's most radical revelation: the Buddha is eternal and cosmic, not merely a historical figure. Central theme: The Buddha's teaching is supported by his eternal, transcendent nature across all time.

17. COUNTING UP THE GRACE (Distinguishing Merits; Chinese: 分別功德品)
The sutra explains the mathematics of grace: believing in this teaching for one instant generates more merit than practicing traditional teachings for billions of lifetimes. The "mathematics of grace" is the scandal and liberation of the Lotus Sutra. Central theme: One moment of faith in the promise of universal Buddhahood outweighs eons of self-powered effort.

18. THE JOY THAT MULTIPLIES (The Merit of Rejoicing; Chinese: 隨喜功德品)
Those who hear the Lotus Sutra and rejoice in others' practice receive immense merit. The sutra describes beings who just feel happy when they hear others are practicing—that happiness itself is transformative. Central theme: Joy and sympathetic celebration of dharma becomes itself a spiritual path.

19. WHEN YOUR SENSES WAKE UP (The Merit of the Dharma Teacher; Chinese: 法師功德品)
The Buddha describes the supernatural powers that develop in those who teach the Lotus Sutra: perfect eyes that see infinitely far, perfect ears that hear infinitely far, perfect taste, touch, and mind. These aren't powers sought but natural developments of enlightened teaching. Central theme: Teaching the dharma transforms one's entire being into a sensor of infinite reality.

20. THE BODHISATTVA WHO NEVER LOOKED DOWN (Never Looked Down Bodhisattva; Chinese: 常不輕菩薩品)
A past Buddha's lifetime: the bodhisattva "Never Looked Down" encounters people with anger and disrespect but bows to all of them, saying, "I see Buddha-nature in you—you will all become Buddhas." They beat him, curse him, throw rocks. But he keeps bowing, keeps prophesying. This is the ultimate teaching: see Buddha-nature in everyone, no matter how they treat you. Central theme: Radical affirmation of Buddha-potential in all beings, even enemies.

21. WHEN THE LORD SHOWED HIS HAND (The Buddha's Supernatural Powers; Chinese: 如來神力品)
The Buddha employs his supernatural powers to confirm and seal the sutra teachings. Multiple Buddhas from other worlds appear, confirming that the Lotus Sutra is taught in all Buddha-lands. Central theme: The teaching is universal across all worlds and all Buddhas.

22. THE PASSING ON (Entrustment; Chinese: 囑累品)
The Buddha formally entrusts the Lotus Sutra to bodhisattvas for transmission after his death. He gives detailed instructions on how to protect and spread the teaching. Central theme: The responsibility for continuing the dharma passes to all bodhisattvas.

23. MEDICINE KING BODHISATTVA - THE STORY OF HOW HE GAVE IT ALL (The Original Deeds; Chinese: 藥王菩薩本事品)
The Buddha tells of Medicine King's past lives of devotion: saturating his body with fragrant oils and setting himself on fire for 1,200 years as an offering to an ancient Buddha. Later burning both his arms for 72,000 years. He represents ultimate sacrifice and commitment to the dharma. Central theme: Bodhisattva practice includes willingness to give everything for the dharma.

24. WONDERFUL SOUND BODHISATTVA (Chinese: 妙音菩薩品)
Wonderful Sound, a bodhisattva from another world, travels to this world to hear the Lotus Sutra and support those who practice it. She manifests in any form needed to help beings: monk, nun, king, merchant, etc. Central theme: Bodhisattva compassion manifests through infinite adaptability and responsiveness.

25. THE ONE WHO HEARS THE WORLD CRYING (Avalokiteśvara; Chinese: 觀世音菩薩普門品)
The most beloved chapter in East Asian Buddhism. Avalokiteśvara, the bodhisattva of compassion, manifests in 33 different forms to save beings in peril. She responds to every cry of suffering, appearing as a Buddha, a king, a merchant, a mother—whatever form is needed. Central theme: Compassion responsive to need; salvation through understanding.

26. THE PROTECTION SONGS (Dhāraṇī; Chinese: 陀羅尼品)
Protective incantations and mantras from various bodhisattvas and gods promise to protect those who teach the Lotus Sutra. Protective formulas and sacred sounds create a spiritual safety net. Central theme: Practice of the dharma generates protective forces in the universe.

27. THE BEAUTIFUL KING AND HIS GOOD FRIENDS (Chinese: 妙莊嚴王本事品)
A past-life story: A king clings to false teachings while his two sons, enlightened bodhisattvas, use miraculous powers and compassionate wisdom to awaken him. His family's journey to the Buddha becomes an eternal teaching about spiritual friendship and how grace works through the people closest to us. Central theme: Spiritual friends are the great cause and condition for awakening; enlightened beings keep showing up in whatever form will reach us because love won't let us stay lost.

28. UNIVERSAL WORTHY COMES TO ENCOURAGE YOU (Bodhisattva Samantabhadra; Chinese: 普賢菩薩勸發品)
The final chapter: Universal Worthy (Samantabhadra) bodhisattva comes on a white elephant to seal the teaching with a final promise. Those who accept this sutra will be protected, guided, and ultimately achieve Buddhahood. The teaching is sealed with Universal Worthy's vow that all beings will become Buddhas. Central theme: The sutra's ultimate promise—sealed by the greatest bodhisattvas—that all beings without exception will become Buddhas.

---

### Reader's Introduction: The Parable and the Promise

Reader's Introduction: The Parable and the Promise
Listen now, this is what you’re holding in your hands: the Sutra of the Lotus Flower of the Wonderful Dharma, one of the most beloved and revolutionary texts in the world. In Asia, they call it the "King of Sutras." This is the teaching that turned everything upside down.
For centuries, many believed the highest spiritual goal was to become an Arhat—a perfect saint who escapes the cycle of suffering and enters the final peace of nirvāṇa. It was a steep climb, and it was final.
Then, the Buddha reveals the truth at the heart of this sutra. He says that this final peace, this nirvāṇa, wasn't the final destination after all. It was just a "phantom city," a rest stop he created out of compassion for travelers who were too tired to keep walking.
The true destination, he says, is something bigger. It’s the "one great matter" he came to reveal: Every single living being—no exceptions—will become a Buddha.
This is the sutra's great promise. The old disciples who thought they were finished, the women who were told they couldn't, the evil-doers like Devadatta, the non-humans like the 8-year-old dragon girl—all of them, and all of us, are destined for perfect, complete enlightenment.

And let me tell you something else you need to know. This sutra's been written down by men, and mostly men talking. But pay attention when the women show up—because when they do, everything changes. Mahāprajāpatī, the aunt who raised the Buddha when his mama died, she walks up and asks the Buddha straight out: 'Can we practice? Can we be free too?' And the Buddha's got to stop and reckon with her. Then later, eight thousand nuns come through and get their prophecies of Buddhahood. And then—this is the thing that's gonna shake you—a dragon girl, eight years old, becomes a Buddha right there in front of everybody. Just like that. No waiting, no 'you gotta come back as a man next time.' The dharma don't work that way.

The blues knows this truth. Sister Rosetta Tharpe didn't wait for permission. Mahalia Jackson carried the Spirit. The mothers and grandmothers held the church together. When women move in the dharma, when women practice, when women teach—that's when you know it's real. That's the sign that this teaching ain't just for the chosen few. It's for everybody. The dragon girl's enlightenment ain't incidental. It's the proof. It's the promise written in the flesh of a child who shouldn't be able to do it—but does.

To explain this, the Buddha uses parables—stories that resonate with a truth deeper than philosophy. He tells of:
A father lying to save his children from a burning house (Chapter 3).
A rich man who finds his long-lost prodigal son and slowly gives him back his inheritance (Chapter 4).
A man who wanders in poverty, not knowing there’s a priceless jewel sewn into his robe (Chapter 8).
These are stories of "loving tricks" (upāya). They teach that the Buddha is an eternal father who, out of boundless compassion, meets us where we are, speaks our language, and uses whatever parables it takes to guide us home.
This "Blues Interpretation" takes that principle to heart. The blues and gospel traditions are, at their core, traditions of parable and promise. They know the burning house. They know the prodigal son. They know what it means to be a "motherless child, a long way from home" who is still promised a "home in gloryland."
How to Read This Book
This text was meant to be heard, to be recited, to be felt. As a musician, I invite you to read it that way. Don't get hung up on the strange names or the cosmic numbers. Listen for the music. Listen for the rhythm in the verses. Listen for the voice of the preacher, the storyteller, the father, the friend.
This isn't just a book. It's a song. It’s a promise. It's the good news that no matter who you are, or where you've been, or what you've done, you are already an heir to everything.
You just need to wake up, remember who you are, and claim your inheritance.

# CHAPTER ONE: THE OPENING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
A Blues Interpretation from the Classical Chinese

---
## THE GATHERING

Listen now, this is what I heard with my own ears.

One time—and ain't it always one time when truth comes calling—the Buddha was staying up on Vulture Peak Mountain, right outside the city called King's House (Rajagriha). And he wasn't alone, no sir. He had twelve thousand monks with him, every last one of them a brother who'd made it through to the other side.

These weren't no beginners, you understand. These were folks who'd walked through the valley of shadows and come out clean. They'd dried up all the leaking wounds of craving. They'd burned out every last bit of that fever we call desire. They'd gotten what they came for, broke every chain that binds a person to this wheel of suffering, and their minds were finally free—free like a bird that's been caged all its life and somebody finally opens the door.

Let me tell you their names, 'cause names matter when you talking about the ones who made it through:

There was Ājñāta-Kauṇḍinya, the first one to understand what the Buddha was laying down. There was Mahākāśyapa, that stern old man who knew how to live with nothing. There was Uruvilvā-Kāśyapa and his brothers Gayā-Kāśyapa and Nadī-Kāśyapa—three brothers who all found the way. There was Śāriputra, sharp as a razor, and Mahā-Maudgalyāyana, who could work wonders. There was Mahā-Kātyāyana, who could preach, and Aniruddha, who could see what others couldn't. There was Kapphina and Gavāmpati and Revata and Pilindavatsa and Bakkula and Mahā-Kauṣṭhila and Nanda and Sundarananda and Pūrṇa-Maitrāyaṇīputra, the one who could really teach, and Subhūti, who understood emptiness like breathing, and Ānanda, who remembered everything the Buddha ever said, and Rāhula, the Buddha's own son.

These were the famous ones, the ones everybody knew about, the ones who'd gone all the way.

And there were two thousand more—some still learning, some already done with learning—all walking that path.

And Mahāprajāpatī Gautamī, the nun who raised the Buddha when his mama died, she was there with six thousand sisters following her. And Yaśodharā, the one who used to be the Buddha's wife before he left to find the truth, she was there too with her own followers.

But that ain't all. No sir, that ain't even half of it.

There were eighty thousand bodhisattvas—and when I say bodhisattva, I'm talking about the ones who've set their hearts on the highest freedom, not just for themselves but for everybody. They weren't gonna turn back, no matter what. They'd gotten hold of something called dhāraṇī—that's like a power to hold onto the truth no matter how hard the wind blows. They could speak smooth and clear about the deepest things, and they kept the wheel of teaching rolling forward, never backing up.

They'd served countless Buddhas—I'm talking about hundreds, thousands, millions of enlightened teachers. They'd planted good seeds in all those Buddha-fields, and every Buddha they ever met spoke their names with respect. They'd trained their hearts in loving-kindness until kindness was just who they were. They'd gotten inside the Buddha's wisdom like you get inside your own house. They'd crossed over to the far shore of understanding, and their names rang out through countless worlds. They could guide countless beings through the darkness.

Let me tell you some of their names:

There was Mañjuśrī, who carries the sword of wisdom. There was Avalokiteśvara, who hears every cry of suffering in this world. There was Mahāsthāmaprāpta, the one with great power. There was Always Striving, who never lets up. There was Never Resting, who don't know how to quit. There was Jewel Palm and Medicine King and Brave Giving and Jewel Moon and Moon Light and Full Moon. There was Great Strength and Limitless Strength and Beyond the Triple World. There was Bhadrapāla and Maitreya—that's the one who's gonna be the next Buddha when this age runs out. There was Jewel Accumulation and Guide—eighty thousand of these great-hearted teachers, all gathered there.

And the heavenly beings showed up too, you better believe it.

Śakra, the king of the thirty-three heavens, he came down with twenty thousand divine children. And there was Moon Prince and Universal Fragrance and Jewel Light and the Four Great Kings, each one bringing ten thousand children of heaven. And Freedom King and Great Freedom King, they brought thirty thousand. And Brahmā Sahāmpati, the lord of this whole world-system, and Śikhin Brahma and Bright Brahma, they came with twelve thousand divine children.

And eight dragon kings came swimming up from the deep waters: Nanda and Upananda and Sāgara and Vāsukin and Takṣaka and Anavatapta and Manasvin and Utpalaka—each one with hundreds of thousands following behind.

And four kings of the kinnaras came—those are the singers, the ones who make music for the gods. There was Dharma Kinnara King and Wonderful Dharma Kinnara King and Great Dharma Kinnara King and Dharma-Holding Kinnara King, all with their companies.

And four kings of the gandharvas—those are the heavenly musicians. There was Joy and Joyful Sound and Beautiful and Beautiful Sound, all with their followers.

And four kings of the asuras came too—those are the jealous spirits, always fighting with the gods: Bālin and Kharaskandha and Vemacitrin and Rāhu, each one leading hundreds of thousands.

And four kings of the garuḍas—those giant birds—came flying down: Great Virtue and Great Body and Great Fullness and As-You-Wish, each one with their flocks.

And King Ajātaśatru, son of Queen Vaidehi, he came with hundreds of thousands of people from the city.

Every single one of them bowed down at the Buddha's feet—and when I say feet, I mean they got low, they showed respect—and then they sat down off to the side.

## THE TEACHING BEFORE THE SILENCE

Right then, the Buddha was surrounded on all four sides—monks, nuns, laymen, laywomen—all of them offering him respect, honoring him, praising him. And he started teaching them a scripture of the Great Vehicle, a text called "The Sutra of Immeasurable Meanings"—a teaching for those bodhisattvas, a teaching that the Buddha himself protects and keeps in mind.

When he finished teaching that sutra, he crossed his legs up under him—you know that sitting position, like a locked-up lotus—and he entered into a deep, deep meditation on infinite meanings. His body went still. His mind went still. He wasn't moving at all.

And right then, heaven started raining flowers—mandarava flowers and great mandarava flowers, manjusaka flowers and great manjusaka flowers—pouring down on the Buddha and all that great assembly. And the whole Buddha-world shook six different ways—you know that shaking when the earth can't hold still 'cause something holy is happening.

And everybody in that gathering—the monks and nuns, the laymen and laywomen, the dragons and the night-wandering spirits, the gandharvas and asuras and garudas and kinnaras and mahoragas, the humans and non-humans, all the little kings and the wheel-turning holy kings—every last one of them got something they never had before. Joy rose up in them. They put their palms together and they looked at the Buddha with one mind, all together, not turning away.

## THE LIGHT FROM BETWEEN THE EYEBROWS

And then the Buddha sent out a beam of light from between his eyebrows—right from that white hair-mark there—and it lit up eighteen thousand worlds to the east, every last corner of them, down to the deepest hell called Avīci where suffering don't quit, all the way up to Akaniṣṭha Heaven where the highest gods dwell.

And from this world here, you could see into all those other lands. You could see beings in all six paths of existence—beings in hell, hungry ghosts, animals, humans, jealous spirits, gods—you could see them all. And you could see the Buddhas in those worlds, teaching right now, hear the words they were saying. And you could see monks and nuns and laymen and laywomen, people walking the path and getting somewhere. You could see bodhisattvas with all kinds of causes and conditions, all kinds of faith and understanding, all kinds of appearances, walking the bodhisattva road. You could see Buddhas entering that final peace beyond peace, that nirvāṇa. And you could see, after those Buddhas passed on, stupas made of seven kinds of jewels, rising up to hold their relics.

## MAITREYA ASKS, MAÑJUŚRĪ REMEMBERS

Right then, Maitreya Bodhisattva—the one who's coming next—he thought to himself: "Now what's going on here? The World-Honored One is showing us signs and wonders. What's the cause of all this? The Buddha's gone into a meditation so deep you can't think your way into it, and he's showing us things we never seen before. Who should I ask about this? Who could answer?"

And then he thought: "This Mañjuśrī, he's the Dharma-prince, the king's son. He's served countless Buddhas in the past, no telling how many. He must've seen signs like this before. I'm gonna ask him."

And right then all the monks and nuns and layfolk and all the gods and dragons and spirits, they all thought the same thing: "This light the Buddha's shining, this supernatural sign—who can we ask about it?"

So Maitreya Bodhisattva, wanting to clear up his own doubt and seeing what everybody else was wondering, he turned to Mañjuśrī and said: "What's the cause and condition for all this? Why's he sending out this great light, lighting up eighteen thousand lands to the east, showing us all those Buddha-lands decorated so fine?"

And then Maitreya, wanting to say it again in another way, he spoke in verse. And let me tell you, these verses had rhythm:

Mañjuśrī, tell me why—
Why the Teacher's shining bright
From that white hair between his eyes,
Pouring out that great big light?

Mandarava flowers raining down,
Manjusaka falling all around,
Sandalwood wind blowing sweet,
Making everybody's heart feel sound.

That's why the ground's all pure and clean,
This whole world shaking six ways round,
The four assemblies filled with joy,
Body and mind feeling glory-bound.

Light from between his eyebrows bright,
Shining east across the land,
Eighteen thousand worlds lit up,
Every one of them turning gold like sand.

From Avīci Hell below
All the way to highest height,
In all those worlds we're seeing now
Beings walking in six paths of life.

We see where birth and death will lead them,
See the good and bad they've sown,
See the pretty and the ugly fruit they're getting—
Everything's being shown.

And we see the Buddhas too,
Those holy lion-teachers standing tall,
Teaching scriptures wonderful and subtle—
The finest teachings of them all.

Their voices pure and clear,
Speaking soft words, smooth and true,
Teaching countless bodhisattvas—
Millions, billions, more than you can count, it's true.

That Brahma-voice so deep and wonderful,
Making people want to hear,
Each one in their own world
Teaching Dharma true and clear.

Using all kinds of causes and conditions,
Countless metaphors and signs,
Lighting up the Buddha-Dharma,
Opening up people's minds.

If someone's suffering hard,
Sick and tired of getting old and dying sick,
They'll teach nirvāṇa to them—
That'll end suffering quick.

If someone's got good fortune,
Served Buddhas way back when,
Looking for the highest teaching,
They'll teach the solitary path to them.

If there's Buddha's children there
Riding many different rails,
Seeking wisdom highest of all,
They'll teach the pure path to unfold.

Mañjuśrī, sitting here,
I'm seeing and I'm hearing all of this,
A thousand billion different things—
More than I can tell you, more than I can list.

I'm seeing countless bodhisattvas,
Sands-of-the-Ganges many, seeking Buddha's way,
Some of them practicing giving—
Gold and silver, coral fine,
Pearls and magic jewels,
Conch shells, agate, diamonds that shine,
Servants, carriages, everything they own,
Precious decorated sedan chairs—
Giving it all with happy hearts,
Dedicating it to Buddha's care,
Hoping for that supreme vehicle,
The one all Buddhas praise everywhere.

Some bodhisattvas giving four-horse jeweled carts,
Railings and flower canopies decorated so nice.
And I see bodhisattvas giving their own flesh and limbs,
Even wives and children—that's the ultimate sacrifice,
Seeking that light of wisdom supreme.

I see bodhisattvas giving heads and eyes and bodies,
Happy to offer them all up,
Seeking Buddha-wisdom—
Filling that wisdom-cup.

Mañjuśrī, I see kings
Going to where Buddha stays,
Asking about the highest path,
Then giving up their pleasant kingdoms, palaces and all,
Shaving off their hair,
Putting on the Dharma robes—
Answering that highest call.

I see bodhisattvas becoming monks,
Living alone in quiet places,
Happy to chant the scriptures day and night,
Peace all over their faces.

I see bodhisattvas brave and vigorous,
Going into deep mountains where the world can't follow,
Thinking about the Buddha's path,
Where the truth ain't hollow.

I see ones who've left desire behind,
Always staying in empty space,
Going deep into meditation,
Getting five spiritual powers by grace.

I see bodhisattvas sitting quiet in meditation, palms together,
Praising the Dharma-kings
With thousands and millions of verses—
Singing those wisdom-things.

I see bodhisattvas with wisdom deep and will firm,
Able to question all the Buddhas,
Hearing everything and holding onto it—
Not letting go of nothing, never.

I see Buddha-children
With meditation and wisdom both complete,
Using countless metaphors
To teach the assembly, making wisdom sweet,
Happy to be teaching Dharma,
Transforming bodhisattvas all around,
Breaking the demon armies,
Beating that Dharma drum sound!

I see bodhisattvas silent and peaceful,
Gods and dragons respecting them all the way—
But they don't get happy about it,
Don't let it make their day.

I see bodhisattvas in forests sending out light,
Relieving the suffering of hell,
Causing beings to enter Buddha's path—
Doing it well.

I see Buddha-children who never sleep,
Walking meditation through the forest night and day,
Seeking Buddha's path with all they got—
Working hard that way.

I see ones holding precepts perfect,
Dignity and deportment without a single crack,
Pure as a jewel,
Nothing that they lack,
Seeking Buddha's path like that.

I see Buddha-children living in the power of patience,
When arrogant people curse and beat them down,
They able to endure it all,
Don't let it turn them around—
Seeking Buddha's path without a frown.

I see bodhisattvas leaving behind jokes and foolishness,
Leaving behind ignorant companions too,
Getting close to wise people instead,
Single-mindedly removing confusion—that's what they do.
Collecting their thoughts in mountain forests,
For billions and millions of years, it's true,
Seeking Buddha's path all the way through.

Some bodhisattvas I see
Offering fine food and drink,
Hundred kinds of medicinal soups,
To Buddha and the sangha—don't even blink.
Fine clothes worth millions,
Or priceless robes beyond counting,
Offering to Buddha and sangha—
Benefits mounting.

Thousands of millions of kinds
Of sandalwood treasure halls,
Wonderful beds and bedding,
Offering to Buddha and sangha—
Answering that call.

Pure gardens and forests,
Flowers and fruit growing thick,
Flowing springs and bathing pools,
Offering to Buddha and sangha—
That's the trick.

All these different kinds of giving,
Offerings wonderful and profound,
Doing it with joy and no weariness,
Seeking that highest path around.

Some bodhisattvas teaching the Dharma of peace and stillness,
Using all kinds of instructions to guide,
Teaching countless living beings—
Walking by their side.

Some bodhisattvas contemplating the nature of all things,
Seeing there ain't no duality there,
Like empty space—
Nothing to compare.

And I see Buddha-children
With minds that don't cling to nothing at all,
Using this wonderful wisdom
To seek the highest path—standing tall.

Mañjuśrī, I see more bodhisattvas
After Buddha's gone into extinction,
Making offerings to relics—
That's their mission.

I see Buddha-children
Building stupas and shrines,
Countless as Ganges sands,
Decorating all the lands with designs.

Jeweled stupas tall and wonderful—
Five thousand yojanas high,
Two thousand yojanas wide and deep—
Reaching toward the sky.

Every single stupa there
With a thousand banners and flags,
Jeweled bells ringing in harmony,
Pearl netting hanging from the crags.

Gods and dragons and spirits,
Humans and non-humans all around,
Always making offerings there
With incense, flowers, and music-sound.

Mañjuśrī, these Buddha-children,
Making offerings to relics this way,
Their countries naturally become
Specially wonderful and beautiful—you could say
Like the king of heavenly trees
When its flowers open up and bloom.

The Buddha let out just one light—
One light clearing all the gloom—
And me and this whole assembly
Seeing all those lands so fine,
All those Buddha-powers
And wisdom-signs.

One pure light
Lighting up countless lands—
We seeing all this,
Never seen nothing like it—understand?

Buddha-son Mañjuśrī,
Please decide everybody's doubt,
The four assemblies looking up hopeful
At you and me—what's this all about?
Why's the World-Honored One
Letting this light shine out?

Buddha-son, answer now,
Decide this doubt and make us glad.
What benefit's coming
From this light—this holy light we've had?

Is Buddha sitting in the place of enlightenment
About to teach that wonderful Dharma he's got?
Or is he about to give predictions of awakening?
Is that what this means or not?

He's showing us Buddha-lands
Decorated with countless jewels so clean,
And we seeing all these Buddhas—
This ain't no small scene.

Mañjuśrī, you know—
Four assemblies, dragons, gods—
They all looking at you, waiting to hear:
What are you gonna say about these signs we see here?

## MAÑJUŚRĪ REMEMBERS THE ANCIENT PATTERN

Right then, Mañjuśrī spoke to Maitreya Bodhisattva and all those great beings and good men, saying:

"The way I figure it, the World-Honored One right now is about to teach a great Dharma, about to rain down a great Dharma-rain, about to blow the great Dharma-conch, about to beat the great Dharma-drum, about to explain the great Dharma-meaning.

"Good men, in the past, with other Buddhas, I've seen signs like this. After they let out this kind of light, they taught the great Dharma. So you should know: the Buddha's showing this light now for the same reason. He wants to help all living beings hear and know this Dharma that's hard for the whole world to believe. That's why he's showing these signs."

And Mañjuśrī started telling a story—a story from so long ago you can't count the years:

"Good men, way back in the past—I'm talking about countless, boundless, inconceivable, uncountable eons ago—there was a Buddha called Moon-Sun-Lamp Thus Come One, Worthy of Offerings, Perfectly Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.

"He taught the true Dharma—good in the beginning, good in the middle, good in the end. His meaning was deep and far-reaching. His words were loving and wonderful. Pure and unmixed, complete and perfect, he laid out what the holy life looks like.

"For people seeking to be hearers, he taught the Dharma of the Four Noble Truths—how to cross over birth and old age and sickness and death, how to reach the final peace of nirvāṇa.

"For people seeking to be solitary realizers, he taught the Dharma of the Twelve Links of Dependent Origination.

"For the bodhisattvas, he taught the Six Perfections—how to attain that highest perfect awakening, how to accomplish all-knowing wisdom."

And then Mañjuśrī said something that'll make you sit up:

"And then there was another Buddha, also called Moon-Sun-Lamp. And then another Buddha, also called Moon-Sun-Lamp. Like this, twenty thousand Buddhas, all with the same name—Moon-Sun-Lamp. And they all had the same family name too: Bharadvāja.

"Maitreya, you should know: from the first Buddha to the last Buddha, they all had the same name, Moon-Sun-Lamp, complete with all ten titles. And the Dharma they taught was good in the beginning, middle, and end.

"Now that last Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha, before he left home to seek the way, he had eight sons. Their names were: Having Intent, Good Intent, Infinite Intent, Jewel Intent, Increasing Intent, Doubt-Removing Intent, Toward Intent, and Dharma Intent.

"These eight princes had power and dignity and freedom. Each one ruled over four continents. When these princes heard that their father had left home and attained the highest perfect awakening, every one of them gave up their kingdoms and followed him into homelessness. They raised their minds to the Great Vehicle, always practiced the holy life, and all became Dharma-teachers. They'd already planted roots of goodness with thousands and millions of Buddhas.

"Now when that Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha taught the scripture of the Great Vehicle called 'Sutra of Infinite Meanings'—a teaching for bodhisattvas, a teaching that Buddha protects and keeps in mind—after he finished teaching it, right there in the great assembly, he crossed his legs up into meditation and entered that concentration on infinite meanings. His body and mind stopped moving.

"And right then, heaven rained down mandarava flowers, great mandarava flowers, manjusaka flowers, great manjusaka flowers, scattering them on the Buddha and the great assembly. And the whole Buddha-world shook six ways.

"And then everybody in the assembly—monks, nuns, laymen, laywomen, gods, dragons, night-wanderers, gandharvas, asuras, garudas, kinnaras, mahoragas, humans and non-humans, all the little kings and wheel-turning holy kings—all of them got something they never had before. Joy! They put their palms together and looked at the Buddha with one mind."

Mañjuśrī kept going with his story:

"And then that Thus Come One let out a beam of light from between his eyebrows, lighting up eighteen thousand Buddha-lands to the east, reaching everywhere, just like the lands we're seeing right now.

"Maitreya, you should know: in that assembly back then, there were two billion bodhisattvas who wanted to hear the Dharma. When these bodhisattvas saw that light shining all over those Buddha-lands, they got something they never had before, and they wanted to know what that light was about.

"There was a bodhisattva there named Wonderful Light, and he had eight hundred disciples.

"Right then, Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha came out of his meditation and, turning to Wonderful Light Bodhisattva, he taught the scripture of the Great Vehicle called 'The Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma'—a teaching for bodhisattvas, a teaching that Buddha protects and keeps in mind.

"He taught that sutra for sixty short eons, never getting up from his seat. And everybody listening sat there in one place for sixty short eons, their bodies and minds not moving, hearing what the Buddha said, and it felt like just the time it takes to eat a meal. During all that time, there wasn't a single person whose body or mind got tired or restless.

"After Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha finished teaching that sutra for sixty short eons, he made this announcement to the assembly of Brahmas and Maras and ascetics and brahmins and gods and humans and asuras:

"'Tonight at midnight, the Thus Come One will enter the nirvāṇa without remainder.'

"There was a bodhisattva there named Virtue Storehouse. Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha gave him a prediction right then and there. He told all the monks: 'This Virtue Storehouse Bodhisattva will be the next Buddha. His name will be Pure Body, Tathāgata, Arhat, Perfectly Enlightened.'

"After giving this prediction, at midnight, Buddha entered the nirvāṇa without remainder.

"After the Buddha passed on, Wonderful Light Bodhisattva held onto that Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma and taught it to people for eighty short eons.

"Now those eight sons of Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha all became students of Wonderful Light. Wonderful Light taught and transformed them, made them firm in their resolve for the highest perfect awakening. After they'd made offerings to countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of Buddhas, they all attained Buddhahood. The last one to become a Buddha was named Burning Lamp.

"Among those eight hundred disciples, there was one person called Seeker of Fame. He was greedy for gain and offerings. Even though he'd study and recite all kinds of scriptures, he didn't really get them—he'd forget most of what he learned. That's why they called him Seeker of Fame.

"But even he, through planting various roots of goodness, got to meet countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of Buddhas, make offerings to them, honor them, respect them, praise them."

And then Mañjuśrī brought it all home:

"Maitreya, you should know: that Wonderful Light Bodhisattva back then—you think he was somebody else? He was me. I'm that person.

"And that Seeker of Fame Bodhisattva? That was you. You're that person.

"Now I'm seeing these signs, and they're no different from what I saw before. That's why I figure: today the Thus Come One is about to teach that scripture of the Great Vehicle called 'The Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma,' a teaching for bodhisattvas, a teaching that Buddha protects and keeps in mind."

## MAÑJUŚRĪ SPEAKS IN VERSE

Right then, in front of that whole great assembly, Mañjuśrī wanted to say it all again in verse form. So he spoke in rhythm and rhyme:

I'm remembering the past—
Countless, numberless eons back—
There was a Buddha, honored among humans,
Named Moon-Sun-Lamp, that's a fact.

The World-Honored One taught Dharma,
Saved countless living beings,
Numberless billions of bodhisattvas,
Brought them into Buddha-wisdom's meanings.

Before Buddha left home,
He had eight royal sons.
When they saw the great holy one leave home,
They followed him—every single one.

Then Buddha taught the Great Vehicle,
A scripture called Infinite Meanings,
Among that great assembly there,
He explained its teachings with all their leanings.

After Buddha finished teaching that sutra,
Right there on his Dharma-seat,
He sat cross-legged in concentration—
Infinite Meanings made complete.

Heaven rained mandarava flowers,
Heavenly drums sounded on their own,
Gods and dragons and spirits and all
Offered to the human-honored one.

All the Buddha-lands right then
Shook powerfully and strong.
Buddha let light out from between his eyebrows—
Rare signs coming along.

That light shone east
Over eighteen thousand Buddha-lands,
Showing all living beings
The places where birth-death and karma stands.

Some saw Buddha-lands
Decorated with countless jewels,
Lapis and crystal colors—
Lit by Buddha-light like swimming pools.

And saw gods and humans,
Dragons, spirits, night-walking crowds,
Gandharvas and kinnaras—
Each one making offerings, humble and proud.

And saw the Thus Come Ones
Naturally attaining Buddha's way,
Bodies colored like golden mountains,
Dignified and subtle in every way.

Like in pure lapis lazuli
A true golden image shines inside—
World-Honored Ones among great assemblies,
Explaining deep Dharma far and wide.

In every single Buddha-land,
Countless hearer-disciples there,
Because of Buddha's light shining,
We could see those great assemblies everywhere.

Some monks were there
In mountain forests dwelling,
Vigorously holding pure precepts
Like they were guarding a jewel—protecting, not selling.

And saw countless bodhisattvas
Practicing giving and patience and more,
Numerous as Ganges sands—
All lit by Buddha-light at the door.

And saw bodhisattvas
Deep in various meditations,
Bodies and minds silent and unmoving,
Seeking the highest way—their destination.

And saw bodhisattvas
Knowing the still and quiet character of Dharma,
Each one in their own land
Teaching Dharma, seeking Buddha—no drama.

Right then the four assemblies
Saw Moon-Sun-Lamp Buddha's great spiritual powers,
Their hearts all filled with joy—
That was their finest hour.

Each one asking each other:
"What's the cause of all this we're seeing?"
The one honored by gods and humans
Just came out of meditation—freed and freeing.

He praised Wonderful Light Bodhisattva:
"You are the world's eye,
The one everyone trusts and believes in,
Able to hold the Dharma-treasury high.

The Dharma I have taught—
Only you can truly know it."
The World-Honored One, after praising him that way,
Made Wonderful Light rejoice and glow with it.

Then he taught this Lotus Sutra of the Dharma,
For sixty short eons sitting there,
Not getting up from that seat once—
Staying in one place with care.

The wonderful supreme Dharma he spoke—
Wonderful Light Dharma-teacher held it all,
Every bit of it, didn't lose a word—
Didn't let one syllable fall.

After Buddha finished teaching that Lotus Sutra of the Dharma,
Making everyone joyful and glad,
Right that very day
He told the gods and humans—every man and lad:

"The meaning of the reality of all things—
I've already explained it to you straight.
Now tonight at midnight
I'm entering nirvāṇa—that's my fate.

You all better focus your minds,
Stay away from being slack,
Buddhas are extremely hard to meet—
Once in billions of eons they come back."

The World-Honored One's children—
When they heard Buddha entering nirvāṇa's door,
Each one of them filled with sorrow:
"How come Buddha's leaving so fast? No more?"

The holy lord, the king of Dharma,
Comforted countless people in that crowd:
"When I enter extinction,
Y'all shouldn't worry or grieve so loud.

This Virtue Storehouse Bodhisattva—
About reality without defilement he's gotten clear.
Next he'll become Buddha,
Named Pure Body—have no fear.
He'll save countless beings too—
Keep them dear."

That very night Buddha entered extinction,
Like firewood burning up, the fire goes out,
They divided up the relics,
Built countless stupas all about.

Monks and nuns—
Their number like Ganges sands—
They doubled their vigorous effort,
Seeking the highest path with working hands.

Wonderful Light Dharma-teacher,
He held onto Buddha's Dharma-treasury with care,
For eighty short eons
He widely taught the Lotus Sutra everywhere.

Those eight princes—
Wonderful Light opened them up and taught them right,
Made them firm in the highest path,
They were gonna see countless Buddhas' light.

After making offerings to those Buddhas,
Following and walking the great path's track,
One after another they attained Buddhahood,
Each one predicting the next one's coming back.

The last one, the heaven among heavens,
Was named Burning Lamp Buddha in the end,
The guide for all the sages,
Saving countless beings—everybody's friend.

That Wonderful Light Dharma-teacher—
He had one disciple back then,
Whose mind was always lazy,
Greedy for fame and gain.

Seeking fame and gain without satisfaction,
Visiting wealthy families all the time,
Abandoning what he'd memorized and studied,
Forgetting it all—couldn't remember one line.

Because of that cause and condition,
They named him Seeker of Fame.
But he also practiced many good deeds,
Got to see countless Buddhas just the same.

Made offerings to all those Buddhas,
Followed and walked the great path's way,
Perfected the Six Perfections,
Now he's seeing Śākya Lion today.

Later on he'll become Buddha,
Named Maitreya, that's gonna be his call,
Widely saving living beings—
Countless beings, one and all.

After that Buddha passed into extinction,
That lazy one—that's you, Maitreya, don't you see?
And that Wonderful Light Dharma-teacher?
That was me—that person was me.

I saw Burning Lamp Buddha
With original light-signs just like this we see,
That's how I know today's Buddha
Is about to teach the Lotus Sutra—certainly.

Today's signs are like the original signs—
This is all the Buddhas' loving tricks.
Now Buddha's letting out this light
To help reveal reality's deepest scenes.

All you people better know:
Put your palms together, wait with single mind,
Buddha's about to rain the Dharma-rain,
Filling up all those seeking the path—you'll find.

All you people seeking the three vehicles,
If you got doubts or regrets inside your chest,
Buddha's gonna remove them and cut them off—
Won't leave a single one—he'll handle the rest.

---

### END OF CHAPTER ONE: THE OPENING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

This interpretation takes Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese text and pours it through the blues tradition—that great river of African American spiritual and musical expression that's always known how to carry deep wisdom in everyday language.

What I kept:
- The famous names like Mañjuśrī, Avalokiteśvara, Śāriputra, Maitreya
- The sequence and structure of the original
- The depth of meaning—blues ain't shallow, and neither is this sutra

What I changed:
- Technical terms became lived experience: "阿羅漢" became people who "made it through to the other side," who "dried up all the leaking wounds of craving"
- Descriptive names got interpreted: "日月燈明如來" became "Moon-Sun-Lamp Thus Come One"
- The eight princes: 有意 became "Having Intent," 善意 became "Good Intent," and so on
- Abstract Buddhist terms became concrete images: nirvāṇa is "that final peace beyond peace," meditation is going so deep "you can't think your way into it," dhāraṇī is "a power to hold onto the truth no matter how hard the wind blows"

The verses especially got the blues treatment—repetition, rhythm, call-and-response feeling, direct address ("Listen now," "you hear me?"), the music of the language itself.

This Chapter One tells the story of the great gathering on Vulture Peak, the Buddha's mysterious entry into meditation, the light from between his eyebrows showing countless worlds, Maitreya's question, and Mañjuśrī's memory of ancient Buddhas doing the same thing before teaching the Lotus Sutra. It's the opening act, the gathering storm before the great teaching comes.

The blues has always carried sacred truth in the language of the people. This interpretation tries to honor both traditions—the ancient Buddhist teaching and the deep American wisdom-stream that knows how to make the high come low, the distant come near, and the abstract become as real as your own heartbeat.

# CHAPTER TWO: THE LOVING TRICKS

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

## THE BUDDHA SPEAKS OF DEEP WISDOM

Now at that time, you see, the World-Honored One—he rose up from his meditation, rose up calm and peaceful-like, and he turned to Śāriputra and said:

"Listen here, Śāriputra. The wisdom of the Buddhas—it goes down deep, deeper than any well you ever tried to draw from. It's measureless, you hear me? Can't nobody measure how deep it goes. The door to that wisdom, now—it's hard to understand, hard to get through. All them disciples and solitary-walkers, all them learned folks who think they got it figured—they can't know this thing.

"And why's that? I'm gonna tell you why. The Buddha, he walked alongside a hundred thousand million billion Buddhas, ain't no way to count them all. He practiced every single path those Buddhas taught, every last one. Brave and fierce, he crossed them rivers. His name got known everywhere, you understand? He accomplished things so deep, things never done before. And when he teaches, he speaks according to what folks need, but what he's really getting at—that's hard to understand.

"Śāriputra, ever since I became a Buddha, I been using all kinds of causes, all kinds of stories, all kinds of comparisons, pouring out teachings like water. Countless loving tricks, that's what I been using, leading people, guiding them away from all their clinging and holding on. And why can I do this? Because the Tathāgata, he's perfected all them loving tricks, all that perfect knowing and seeing.

"Śāriputra, the Tathāgata's knowing and seeing—it's vast and deep and far-reaching. It's without measure, without any walls or boundaries. He got the powers, he got the fearlessness, he got the meditations and the liberations and the deep states—he's gone into them without any bottom to hit. He's accomplished everything that ain't never been accomplished before.

"Śāriputra, the Tathāgata, he can break things down every which way, explain the teachings with skill, and his words—they soft, they gentle, they please every heart.

"Śāriputra, let me put it to you straight: all them measureless, boundless, never-been-done things—the Buddha's accomplished them all."

Then the Buddha said: "Stop, Śāriputra. Don't need to say no more. And why's that? What the Buddha's accomplished—it's the rarest, hardest-to-understand thing there is. Only a Buddha together with a Buddha can exhaust the true reality of all things. That means: the suchness of their appearance, the suchness of their nature, the suchness of their substance, the suchness of their power, the suchness of their function, the suchness of their causes, the suchness of their conditions, the suchness of their effects, the suchness of their results, and the suchness of their beginning and end being ultimately equal."

At that time, the World-Honored One, wanting to speak this meaning again, he sang it out in verse:

The Hero of the World—can't measure him,
All them heavenly beings and worldly folk,
Every kind of living creature there is—
Ain't nobody can know the Buddha's truth.

The Buddha's powers and his fearlessness,
His liberations and his deep meditations,
And all them other Buddha-things he got—
Ain't nobody can measure what he knows.

From countless Buddhas he learned his way,
Crossed every bridge there was to cross,
The dharma deep and subtle, hard to see,
So difficult to understand and know.

For measureless millions upon millions of ages,
He crossed all them waters till he reached the shore,
Reached the place of awakening, got the fruit—
I done seen it all, I know it all.

A fruit as great as this one here,
All its different natures, meanings, marks—
Only me and all them Buddhas in ten directions,
Only we can know this thing.

This dharma—can't be shown by pointing,
Words fall silent when you try to speak it,
All them other kinds of living beings—
Ain't nobody among them understands,

Except for the bodhisattvas,
Them whose faith-power stands strong and firm.
The Buddha's disciples, you understand,
Who done served and honored countless Buddhas,

Who done exhausted all their outflows,
Who standing in their final body now—
Even folks like this here,
They ain't got the strength to bear this teaching.

Even if you filled the whole world up
With people smart as Śāriputra,
And they put their heads together trying to measure—
They couldn't measure the Buddha's wisdom.

Even if you filled up all ten directions,
Filled them full of folks like Śāriputra,
And all the other disciples too,
Filled up every Buddha-land there is,

Put their heads together trying to measure—
Still couldn't know this thing at all.
Them solitary-walkers with their sharp intelligence,
Without outflows, in their final body,

They fill up all ten directions too,
Numerous as bamboo in a bamboo forest,
And they all of one mind, you hear me?
For billions and billions of countless ages,

They wanting to think about the Buddha's real wisdom—
Couldn't know even the smallest little bit of it.
Them newly inspired bodhisattvas,
Who served and honored countless Buddhas,

Who understand all kinds of meanings,
Who can preach the dharma lovingly,
Numerous as rice plants and hemp and bamboo and reeds,
Filling up all the lands in ten directions,

With one mind using their wonderful wisdom,
For ages numerous as Ganges River sands,
All of them thinking about it together—
Still couldn't know the Buddha's wisdom.

Them non-backsliding bodhisattvas,
Numerous as Ganges River sands,
With one mind seeking together—
Still couldn't know this thing neither.

I'm telling you again, Śāriputra:
The dharma without outflow, inconceivable,
So deep and subtle, so wonderful—
I done fully obtained it now.

Only I know its true reality,
And all them Buddhas in ten directions too.
Śāriputra, you ought to know this:
The words of the Buddhas don't differ none.

Concerning the dharma that the Buddha speaks,
You got to generate great faith-power.
After the World-Honored One taught a long time,
He finally got to speak the real truth.

I'm telling all you disciples,
And them seeking the solitary-walker vehicle—
I freed you from the bonds of suffering,
Let you reach nirvāṇa, that's what I did.

But the Buddha used his loving power,
Showed you three different vehicles of teaching.
Living beings get attached all over the place—
I led them so they could get themselves free.

## THE ASSEMBLY'S DOUBT AND ŚĀRIPUTRA'S REQUEST

At that time, in the great assembly, there was disciples—them with no more outflows, them arhats—Ājñāta-Kauṇḍinya and the rest, twelve hundred of them. And there was others too, monks and nuns, laymen and laywomen, who done set their mind on the disciple vehicle or the solitary-walker vehicle. Every one of them thought to themselves:

"Right now, why's the World-Honored One going on so earnestly, praising and exalting loving tricks like this? He says the dharma the Buddha obtained is so deep and hard to understand. What he speaks about got a meaning that's difficult to know. All them disciples and solitary-walkers can't reach it. The Buddha explained one liberation doctrine—we obtained that dharma too, reached nirvāṇa with it. But now we don't know where this teaching is heading."

At that time, Śāriputra, he knew the four groups in the assembly was doubting, and he himself didn't understand it neither. So he addressed the Buddha, saying:

"World-Honored One, for what cause, for what reason are you so earnestly praising the Buddhas' foremost loving tricks, this deep, subtle, difficult-to-understand dharma? From way back till now, I ain't never heard you speak like this before. Right now, all four groups got doubts. I only wish the World-Honored One would explain this matter. World-Honored One, why are you so earnestly praising this deep, subtle, difficult-to-understand dharma?"

Then Śāriputra, wanting to speak this meaning again, he said it in verse:

Sun of Wisdom, Great Holy Honored One,
After such a long time you speaking this dharma now.
You say you obtained these kind of things—
Powers, fearlessness, meditations,

Concentration, liberation, all the rest,
Inconceivable dharmas like these.
The dharma you got at the place of awakening—
Ain't nobody can raise a question about it.

My mind's so hard to fathom out,
And ain't nobody can ask about it neither.
Without nobody asking, you just speak on your own,
Praising the journey you made yourself.

Your wisdom so subtle and wonderful,
The very thing all Buddhas obtained.
Them arhats without outflow,
And them seeking nirvāṇa too—

Now they all done fell into a net of doubt,
Wondering why the Buddha speaking this.
Them seeking to be solitary-walkers,
Monks and nuns alike,

Heavenly beings, dragons, spirits, gods,
And gandharvas and such—
They looking at each other, full of wondering,
Gazing up at the Honored One of Two Feet.

What is this matter all about?
We hoping the Buddha will explain it clear.
Among all them disciple folks,
The Buddha says I'm number one.

But right now, concerning my own wisdom,
I'm doubting, confused, can't understand—
Is this the ultimate dharma here,
Or is it just the way we travel?

We who was born from the Buddha's mouth,
We standing with palms pressed together, gazing up and waiting,
Hoping you'll send forth your subtle, wonderful voice,
Speak to us at this time just like it really is.

Them heavenly beings, dragons, spirits, and gods,
Numerous as Ganges River sands,
Them bodhisattvas seeking to be Buddhas,
Eighty thousand in their great number,

And from millions and billions of lands,
Wheel-turning holy kings done arrived,
Palms pressed together with reverent minds—
They wanting to hear about the complete road.

## THE BUDDHA'S THREE REFUSALS

At that time, the Buddha told Śāriputra: "Stop, stop. Don't need to say no more. If I speak about this matter, all them beings in all the worlds—the heavenly folk and the human folk—they all gonna be shocked and full of doubt."

Śāriputra addressed the Buddha again, saying: "World-Honored One, I only wish you would speak about it, only wish you would speak! And why's that? In this assembly here, there's countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of innumerable living beings who done seen Buddhas before. Their faculties are sharp and fierce, their wisdom bright and clear. When they hear what the Buddha speaks, they gonna be able to respect it and believe it."

At that time, Śāriputra, wanting to speak this meaning again, he said it in verse:

Dharma King, Most High Honored One,
Just speak it out, don't you worry none.
In this assembly of measureless folks,
There's some who can respect and believe.

The Buddha again stopped Śāriputra: "If I speak about this matter, all them beings in all the worlds—heavenly folk, human folk, asuras—they all gonna be shocked and full of doubt. And them arrogant monks, they gonna fall into the great pit."

At that time, the World-Honored One spoke again in verse, saying:

Stop, stop, don't need to speak about it.
My dharma is wonderful and hard to conceive.
Them arrogant ones full of themselves—
If they hear it, they surely won't respect or believe.

At that time, Śāriputra addressed the Buddha again: "World-Honored One, I only wish you would speak about it, only wish you would speak! Right now in this assembly here, folks like me and more—hundreds of thousands of millions of billions—world after world we been receiving the Buddha's transforming teaching. Folks like this, they surely can respect and believe. They gonna have long-night peace and safety, gonna gain much benefit."

At that time, Śāriputra, wanting to speak this meaning again, he said it in verse:

Most High Honored One of Two Feet,
I wish you'd speak the foremost dharma.
I'm the Buddha's eldest son—
I only beg you to explain it clear and distinct.

This assembly of measureless folks,
They able to respect and believe this dharma.
The Buddha already, world after world,
Done taught and transformed folks like these.

They all with one mind, palms pressed together,
Wanting to listen and receive the Buddha's words.
We twelve hundred here,
And others seeking to be Buddhas—

We wish, for the sake of this whole assembly,
You'd just explain it clear and distinct.
When these folks hear this dharma,
They gonna be born with great rejoicing.

## FIVE THOUSAND LEAVE THE ASSEMBLY

At that time, the World-Honored One told Śāriputra: "You done earnestly asked three times now. How can I not speak? Now you listen carefully, think about it well, remember it good. I'm gonna break it down for you clear and distinct."
