The blues is born from recognizing that the house is on fire, that the world as given is not safe, not healthy, not home.

"Shit and piss," "gnawing on dead bodies," "demons squatting on dirt mounds"—I didn't sanitize any of it. The Chinese doesn't sanitize it either. This is the world in its full horror, and the children are PLAYING in the middle of all this death and decay.

The gospel tradition has its own versions of this: "This world is not my home, I'm just a-passing through." "Sometimes I feel like a motherless child, a long way from home." The recognition that this world, as it is, is a place of suffering.

### THE FATHER'S DESPERATION

"Even though I can get myself out through this burning door safe and sound, my children—they in there in that fire house, playing games, having fun, don't notice nothing, don't know nothing, ain't scared of nothing."

This is every parent's nightmare. And it's also the Buddha's situation. He's awakened—he's safe—but everyone else is still in danger and won't listen to him when he tells them to get out.

The father thinks: "I could carry them out in my robe, or I could use a table or something, get them out that way." (以衣祴、若以幾案) But then: "But this house only got one door, and it's narrow too."

This is the theological problem: Force doesn't work. You can't drag someone to enlightenment. The door is narrow (one vehicle, one truth) but the father has to convince the children to WANT to go through it.

So he uses loving tricks (方便): He tells them about toys outside. He promises them what they want (three different carts for different tastes) to get them to safety. Then, once they're safe, he gives them something better than they imagined.

### THE THREE CARTS AND THE ONE CART

"Sheep cart, deer cart, ox cart" (羊車、鹿車、牛車)—I kept this concrete. These are the three vehicles (三乘):
- Sheep cart = Voice-hearer vehicle (śrāvaka)
- Deer cart = Solitary-realizer vehicle (pratyekabuddha)
- Ox cart = Bodhisattva vehicle

The children want different things, have different desires. The father promises them all what they want individually. But what he actually gives them is one great cart (一大車) pulled by "white oxen—skin bright and clean, beautiful bodies, real strong and powerful, walking smooth and steady, fast as the wind."

This is abundance beyond promise. This is God's generosity in the gospel tradition: "I came that they might have life, and have it abundantly." This is the blues recognition that when things are truly good, they're better than you dared to hope.

The cart is described in loving, luxurious detail: jewels, bells, canopies, cushions, pearls, servants. This isn't minimalist enlightenment. This is wealth, beauty, comfort, joy. The Buddha-vehicle isn't a harder path—it's a BETTER path, a richer path, a more beautiful path.

### ŚĀRIPUTRA AND THE BUDDHA: CALL AND RESPONSE

The dialogue between Śāriputra and the Buddha (lines 973-974) is structured like call and response:

Buddha: "Did that elder lie?"
Śāriputra: "No, World-Honored One. That elder just wanted his children to get free from the fire disaster, to keep their bodies alive—that ain't no lie."

This is the structure of African American preaching: The preacher asks, the congregation answers. The preacher makes a point, the congregation affirms it. "Can I get an amen?" "Amen!"

Śāriputra's response is both legal argument (proving the father didn't lie) and theological affirmation (upholding the Buddha's loving tricks). I kept the logic clear but made the language conversational: "that ain't no lie," "How much more so when..."

### THE DIRE WARNINGS: KARMIC CONSEQUENCES

The long section on the punishments for those who reject the sutra (lines 1232-1295) is harsh, graphic, relentless. If you slander this teaching, you will:
- Fall into Avici Hell for a whole eon
- Be reborn as animals: dogs, jackals, camels, donkeys, pythons
- If human, be disabled, despised, diseased, poor, possessed by demons
- Never see a Buddha, never hear the Dharma

This is fire-and-brimstone preaching. This is "Choose this day whom you will serve." This is the blues recognition that choices have consequences, that some paths lead to destruction.

I didn't soften any of it because the text doesn't soften it. But I made sure the language had the rhythm of warning, of prophetic judgment:

"They gonna enter Avici Hell,
stay there a whole age.
When the age ends, they born again,
like this, turning over and over,
for numberless ages."

The repetition creates a drumbeat of doom. The concrete details make it visceral: "Short, ugly, crippled, lame, blind, deaf, hunchbacked... their breath always stinking, possessed by ghosts and demons."

This isn't cruelty—it's urgency. The stakes are ultimate. The warning is serious because the love is serious. A father who doesn't warn his children about real danger doesn't really love them.

### WHO SHOULD RECEIVE THIS TEACHING

The positive counterpart (lines 1300-1337) lists who CAN receive this sutra:
- People with sharp faculties and clear wisdom
- People who've served countless Buddhas
- People diligent in compassion
- People respectful, separated from fools
- People who seek the Dharma like Buddha's relics

I structured these as a litany, a list of blessings:

"If there's people with sharp faculties,
wisdom clear and bright,
much learning, strong memory,
seeking the buddha-way—
People like these,
you can teach it to them."

The repetition of "People like these, / you can teach it to them" (如是之人，乃可為說) becomes a refrain, an affirmation, a blessing. This is the prophetic voice recognizing the worthy, calling out the ready, affirming the prepared.

### THEOLOGICAL DEPTH PRESERVED

Despite the vernacular language, I preserved all the key Buddhist concepts:

- **The One Vehicle** (一佛乘): There aren't really three vehicles. There's only one Way, but the Buddha teaches it three ways to reach different people.

- **Loving Tricks** (方便): The father's "tricks" aren't lies—they're compassionate strategy. The Buddha's provisional teachings aren't false—they're stepping stones to truth.

- **The Triple World** (三界): The burning house represents samsara—the realm of desire, form, and formlessness—all of it on fire with birth, aging, sickness, death.

- **The Buddha as Father** (世間之父): All living beings are the Buddha's children. This isn't metaphor—it's the fundamental relationship between awakening and those not yet awakened.

- **The Great Vehicle** (大乘): The one ox-cart is the Mahayana path—not a path of individual salvation but of universal liberation, not escape from the world but transformation of the world.

---

## PARALLELS: BLUES/GOSPEL AND BUDDHIST WISDOM

### THE WORLD ON FIRE

Blues: "This world is not my home" / "Motherless child, long way from home"
Buddhism: The triple world is a burning house (三界火宅)

Both traditions recognize that suffering isn't occasional—it's structural. The house itself is on fire. The world itself is the problem.

### THE FATHER'S COMPASSION

Gospel: "God so loved the world that he gave..."
Buddhism: The Buddha's great compassion (大慈大悲) drives everything

The father in the parable doesn't give up when the children won't listen. He doesn't say "Fine, stay in the fire." He finds another way. This is what compassion looks like—relentless creativity in service of salvation.

### HOLY TRICKERY

Gospel: Jesus teaching in parables, hiding truth from the "wise" and revealing it to "children"
Buddhism: Loving tricks (upaya) using provisional teachings to lead to ultimate truth

Both traditions know that sometimes you have to meet people where they are, speak their language, promise them what they want, to get them moving in the right direction. Then, once they're moving, you can give them what they truly need.

### THE GIFT EXCEEDS THE PROMISE

Gospel: "Eye hath not seen, nor ear heard, neither have entered into the heart of man, the things which God hath prepared for them that love him"
Buddhism: The one great cart far exceeds the three promised carts

Both traditions insist that reality—true reality, awakened reality—is better than anything we can imagine from within our unawakened state. The father doesn't give worse carts than promised. He gives infinitely better.

### THE URGENCY OF NOW

Blues: "Nobody knows the trouble I've seen" / "Sometimes I feel like a motherless child"
Buddhism: The recognition that birth, aging, sickness, and death are happening NOW, not someday

Both traditions refuse to let suffering be theoretical or distant. The house is on fire NOW. Your children are in danger NOW. The time for action is NOW.

---

## WHAT THE BLUES VOICE ADDS

Using blues/gospel vernacular isn't just about accessibility (though it is accessible). It's about recognizing a spiritual kinship between Buddhist wisdom and African American sacred tradition:

1. **Both emerge from suffering**: The blues comes from slavery, Jim Crow, ongoing oppression. Buddhism comes from the recognition of dukkha—the unsatisfactoriness of conditioned existence. Neither tradition romanticizes suffering, but both face it directly.

2. **Both insist on joy despite suffering**: The blues can laugh through tears. Buddhism promises nirvāṇa. The one great cart isn't grim escape—it's beautiful, jeweled, accompanied by servants, fast as wind.

3. **Both know the power of the collective**: Gospel music is congregational. The blues is in dialogue with the community. Buddhism's great vehicle is about saving all beings together, not escaping alone.

4. **Both use rhythm and repetition**: The verses in this chapter repeat structures, build through accumulation, create momentum through recurrence. This is how wisdom enters not just the mind but the body, the memory, the heart.

5. **Both speak to the marginalized**: The Lotus Sutra was revolutionary in saying everyone can become a Buddha. The blues/gospel tradition insists that the last shall be first, that God has a special care for the lowly. These are traditions of the dispossessed claiming ultimate worth.

---

## FINAL NOTES

This chapter is the dramatic and theological center of the early Lotus Sutra. Śāriputra's joy at receiving his prophecy, the detailed parable of the burning house, the Buddha's explanation of loving tricks, the warnings about rejection, the description of who can receive this teaching—it all builds a complete picture:

The world is on fire.
The Buddha is our father.
We are playing in the flames.
He calls us out with promises.
What he gives us is better than what he promised.
Those who trust him are saved.
Those who reject him are lost.
This is urgent. This is now. This is everything.

The blues/gospel vernacular doesn't simplify this—it amplifies it, gives it emotional resonance, makes it something you can feel in your body, hear in your ears, remember in your heart.

Because that's what this teaching is: not a philosophical proposition to be analyzed, but a truth to be embodied, lived, carried forward through fire.

# CHAPTER FOUR: FAITH AND UNDERSTANDING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

The Parable of the Prodigal Son - A Blues Story of Coming Home

Now listen here, 'cause this is how it went down:

When the Buddha had finished speaking them words, old Subhūti and Mahākātyāyana and Mahākāśyapa and Mahāmaudgalyāyana - they heard something they never heard before. The World-Honored One had just told Śāriputra he was gonna be a Buddha, gonna get that supreme perfect enlightenment, and these four old men - they felt something rare stir up in their hearts. Joy came bubbling up like a spring. They stood up from where they was sitting, straightened out their robes, bared that right shoulder the way you do when you showing respect, dropped down on their right knee, put their palms together, bowed down low, and looked up at the Buddha's face. And they said:

"Lord, we're the eldest ones here, the heads of this congregation. We're old now, getting on in years. We thought we'd made it to the end of the road - thought we'd reached nirvāṇa, that final rest. We figured we wasn't capable of nothing more, wasn't no use trying for that supreme perfect enlightenment no more.

"World-Honored One, when you was preaching all them years past, I was sitting right there in the assembly. But my body got tired, my mind got weary. I just kept thinking about emptiness and signlessness and wishlessness - them three doors to liberation. As for all that Bodhisattva business - playing in supernatural powers, purifying Buddha lands, bringing beings to ripeness - that didn't make my heart sing none. Know why? 'Cause you, World-Honored One, you already brought us out of the three realms of suffering, gave us the certificate of nirvāṇa. And we're old now, getting ancient. When you was teaching them Bodhisattvas about that supreme perfect enlightenment, we didn't feel one spark of desire for it in our hearts.

"But now - oh, now! - we standing here before the Buddha, and we heard you give us hearers that prophecy of supreme perfect enlightenment, and our hearts is full of joy, overflowing with something we never felt before. We never expected this. We never thought that on this day, all of a sudden, we'd hear this rare teaching, this precious thing. We're congratulating ourselves, celebrating deep down, 'cause we done received this great good fortune. We got these infinite treasures - and we wasn't even looking for them. They just came to us on their own."

## THE PARABLE OF THE PRODIGAL SON: A BLUES STORY

"World-Honored One, we got a story to tell, a parable to make this meaning clear. Let us lay it out for you:

Once there was a young man, just a boy really, didn't know no better. He up and left his father's house, ran away from home, went wandering off to a far country. And he stayed gone - ten years, twenty years, might've been fifty years. Time got away from him.

As the years rolled by, the boy grew into a man, but he fell on hard times. Got poor. Got desperate. He was running from place to place, chasing after his next meal, looking for clothes to cover his back. He kept drifting, wandering, and wouldn't you know it - his footsteps led him back toward his home country, though he didn't even know it.

Now his father - the father had been looking for that boy all along. Looked everywhere, couldn't find him. Finally the old man settled down in one city to wait. And that father? He got rich. I mean RICH. His house was overflowing with treasure - gold and silver, lapis lazuli and coral and amber and crystal and pearls. His storehouses was so full they was about to burst. He had servants by the dozens, officials and administrators and workers, elephants and horses, carriages and oxen and sheep - couldn't even count them all. His business dealings spread out across other countries, merchants and traders coming and going, crowds of people everywhere.

Meanwhile that poor wandering son - he was going through villages and towns, drifting from country to country, and he came right to the city where his father lived. Didn't even know it was home.

Now the father, he'd been thinking about that boy every day for fifty-some years. Never told nobody about it. Just kept it inside, thinking and mourning, full of regret. He'd say to himself: 'I'm getting old now. I got all this wealth - gold and silver and jewels, storehouses overflowing - but I ain't got no heir, no son to pass it to. One day I'm gonna die, and all this wealth gonna scatter to the wind, won't have nobody to leave it to.' That thought was eating him up inside. He kept remembering his son, couldn't stop thinking about him.

And he'd say to himself: 'If I could just find my boy, if I could just pass all this wealth on to him, then I could rest easy. I could be at peace. Wouldn't have nothing to worry about no more.'

## THE SON COMES HOME - BUT HE DON'T KNOW IT

"World-Honored One, this is how it went:

That poor son was hiring himself out for day labor, going wherever the work was. And his wandering feet brought him right to his father's mansion. He stopped outside the gate, standing off to the side.

From where he stood, he could see through them gates. He saw his father sitting on a lion throne, a jeweled footstool under his feet. Brahmins and nobles and householders all around him, showing respect, surrounding him with honor. The old man was draped in pearl necklaces worth ten million, all kinds of jewels adorning his body. Officials and servants stood on his left and right, holding white whisks, attending to him. Above him was a canopy made of precious things, silk banners hanging down. They'd sprinkled scented water on the ground, scattered rare flowers everywhere. Treasures was being brought in and taken out, transactions happening, all kinds of magnificent decorations. The old man's authority was powerful, his dignity was overwhelming.

The poor son saw all this, saw his father wielding all that power, and fear grabbed hold of his heart. He regretted coming to this place. He thought to himself: 'This must be a king, or somebody equal to a king. This ain't no place for a man like me to hire out his labor and earn his keep. Better I go to some poor neighborhood where a man can work with his hands, where food is easy to come by. If I stay here too long, they might force me to work, might press me into service against my will.'

Soon as he thought that, he turned around and ran. Ran fast as he could.

## THE FATHER SEES HIS SON

"Now the rich old man was sitting up on that lion throne, and he saw the boy. Recognized him immediately. His heart exploded with joy. He thought to himself: 'NOW I got somebody to inherit all my wealth and treasures and storehouses! I been thinking about this child day and night, had no way to see him, and now he just showed up on his own - this is exactly what I been praying for! I'm old now, getting ancient, but I still care about all this wealth, still want to pass it on.'

Right away he sent people running after the boy. 'Go quick!' he said. 'Bring him back!'

The servants ran after that poor son and grabbed hold of him.

The boy was startled, terrified. He started yelling: 'I ain't done nothing to you! Why you grabbing me? I didn't do nothing wrong!'

But the servants held on tighter, dragged him back by force.

That poor son, he was thinking to himself: 'I'm innocent, I ain't committed no crime, and they got me like a prisoner - they must be fixing to kill me!' The fear got worse and worse until he fainted dead away, fell out on the ground.

The father saw all this from a distance. He told the servants: 'I don't need this man brought in by force. Let him go. Sprinkle cold water on his face so he wakes up, but don't say nothing else to him.'

Why'd the father do that? 'Cause he knew his son's spirit was beaten down low. He knew his own wealth and power was frightening to the boy. He knew for certain this was his child, but he used loving tricks - he didn't tell nobody else, 'This is my son.'

The servants said to the boy: 'You free to go now. Go wherever you want.'

That poor son - he was happy as he'd ever been. He got up off the ground and headed straight for the poor part of town to look for food and work.

## THE FATHER'S PLAN - LOVING TRICKS

"Now the old man, he wanted to draw his son back home, so he set up a plan, used loving tricks. He secretly sent two men - men who looked poor and worn down, didn't have no impressive appearance. He told them: 'Go find that poor man and tell him gently: We got work here, and we'll pay you double wages. If he agrees, bring him here to work. If he asks what kind of work, tell him: We'll hire you to clear away dung and trash. Tell him we'll be working right alongside him.'

So them two men went and found the poor son. When they found him, they laid out the whole proposition.

The poor son, he agreed to the price right away and came along to shovel dung.

The father saw his son doing this work, and his heart hurt for him. He felt pity and wonder at the same time.

Then one day, the father looked out through a window and saw his son's body - thin and worn down, covered in dust and dung, filthy and pitiful.

Right then, the father took off his pearl necklaces, took off his fine soft robes and all his ornaments. He put on rough, dirty, tattered clothes, covered himself in dust. He took a dung-shoveling tool in his right hand, made himself look scared and humble. Then he went out to where the workers was and said: 'Y'all work hard now, don't be lazy.'

This was loving tricks, so he could get close to his son.

After a while, he spoke to his son directly. Said: 'Hey there, young man. You stay here and keep working, don't go nowhere else. I'll raise your wages. Whatever you need - pots and pans, rice flour, salt and vinegar - don't hesitate to ask. I got some old servants here, too - if you need anything, they'll provide it. Put your mind at ease. I'm like a father to you, don't you worry about nothing.

'Why do I say that? 'Cause I'm old now, and you're young and strong. Long as you been working here, you ain't never been lazy or deceitful, ain't never been angry or resentful or complained. I don't see in you none of them bad qualities I see in other workers. From this day forward, you like a son born to me.'

Right then and there, the old man gave the boy a new name, called him 'son.'

Now the poor son - he was glad about this, but deep down he still thought of himself as just a hired hand, a lowly worker. And so it went for twenty years - the father kept him cleaning out dung and trash.

After them twenty years passed, they'd built up trust between them, and the son could come and go freely in the house. But even so, he still lived in his same place, that little shack outside.

## THE FATHER GETS READY TO PASS IT ON

"World-Honored One, then the time came when the old man got sick. He knew death wasn't far off. He said to the poor son: 'I got a lot of gold and silver and jewels, and my storehouses is overflowing. You know how much is in there, what needs to be brought in and taken out - you know all about it. This is what I want - you need to understand my heart in this. Why am I telling you? 'Cause now, you and me ain't no different. You need to pay careful attention so nothing gets lost or wasted.'

Right away the poor son received these instructions. He took charge of all them goods - gold and silver and jewels and all them storehouses. But he didn't want to take not even one meal's worth for himself. He still lived in his same spot outside. That low-down feeling in his heart - he couldn't shake it, couldn't let it go.

Time passed. The father saw his son's mind was gradually opening up, growing bigger. His will was getting stronger, his aspirations was rising. He was starting to feel ashamed of how small he used to think.

When the father's death was getting close, he called his son to him. He gathered all his relatives, the king and his great ministers, the nobles and householders - got everybody together. Then he made this announcement:

'Gentlemen, listen here! This is my son, born from my own body. Years ago in a certain city, he left me and ran away. He wandered suffering and struggling for fifty-some years. His original name was such-and-such, and my name is such-and-such. Back in my home city, I was full of sorrow, searching everywhere for him. Then suddenly in this place, we met again and I found him. This truly is my son, and I truly am his father. Now everything I own - all my wealth and property - it all belongs to him. Everything that's been going in and out of the storehouses - he's known about all of it.'

World-Honored One, when the poor son heard his father say these words, his joy overflowed, burst out of him like he'd never known before. He thought to himself: 'Originally, I had no mind to hope for nothing, wasn't looking for nothing. But now all these treasures have come to me naturally, on their own!'

## THE APPLICATION - THIS IS US

"World-Honored One, that rich old man - that's the Tathāgata. And we're all like Buddha's children. The Tathāgata has always been saying we are his sons.

"World-Honored One, because of the three kinds of suffering, we been burning up with torment in birth-and-death, confused and ignorant, attached to small teachings, loving little dharmas.

"But today the World-Honored One made us think, made us sweep away all that garbage of discriminating thoughts about dharmas. We worked hard at it, put in effort, and we reached nirvāṇa - got that one day's wages. Once we got that, our hearts was full of joy, and we thought we was satisfied. We said to ourselves: 'Because we worked so hard in the Buddha's teaching, we got a whole lot.'

"But the World-Honored One - he already knew our hearts was stuck on shabby little desires, loving them small teachings. So he just let us be, didn't try to force us. He didn't say to us: 'Y'all got a share in the Tathāgata's treasury of knowledge and vision.' The World-Honored One used loving tricks to preach about the Tathāgata's wisdom. We followed the Buddha and got that one day's wages of nirvāṇa, and we thought it was a huge accomplishment. We had no ambition for this Great Vehicle.

"We also explained the Tathāgata's wisdom for the Bodhisattvas, opened it up and demonstrated it and taught it. But we ourselves had no aspiration for it. Why not? The Buddha knew our hearts loved small teachings, so he used loving tricks and taught according to what we could handle. And we didn't know we was really Buddha's children.

"Now - NOW - we finally know: The World-Honored One don't hold back none of his Buddha-wisdom. Why we know that? Because all along, we really BEEN Buddha's children. It's just that we loved them small teachings. If we'd had minds that loved the great teachings, the Buddha would've preached the Great Vehicle to us.

"In this scripture, he only preaches the One Vehicle. But back in the day, in front of the Bodhisattvas, he used to criticize them hearers who loved small teachings. But really, the Buddha was teaching the Great Vehicle all along.

"That's why we're saying: Originally, we had no mind to hope for nothing, wasn't looking for nothing. But now the Dharma King's great treasure has come to us naturally, on its own. Everything that Buddha's children ought to receive - we done already received it all."

## THE VERSES - MAHĀKĀŚYAPA SINGS IT AGAIN

Then Mahākāśyapa, wanting to say this meaning again, spoke in verse:

Today we heard
the Buddha's teaching voice,
Joy danced in us,
something we never knew before.

The Buddha said hearers
gonna become Buddhas -
this supreme treasure pile
came without us even asking.

It's like a young boy,
didn't know no better,
left his father's house,
wandered to a far-off land,

Drifted through countries
for fifty-some years.
His father mourned,
searched in every direction,

Searched till he was tired,
finally stopped in one city,
Built himself a house,
enjoyed the five desires.

That house was wealthy,
had gold and silver,
mother-of-pearl and agate,
genuine pearls and lapis lazuli,

Elephants, horses, cattle, sheep,
sedan chairs and carriages,
Farms and servants,
crowds of people everywhere,

His business dealings
spread to other countries,
Merchants and traders
was everywhere you looked,

Thousands and millions
surrounded him with respect,
The king himself
always held him dear,

Ministers and nobles
all honored him deeply.
For all these reasons,
crowds came and went,

Wealthy and powerful like this,
he had great influence.
But he was getting old,
and worrying about his son even more,

Morning and night thinking,
death was getting near:
'My foolish child left me
fifty-some years ago,

All these storehouses of goods -
what's gonna happen to them?'
Meanwhile the poor son
was searching for food and clothes,

Going from town to town,
from country to country,
Sometimes he got something,
sometimes he got nothing,

Hungry and starving and thin,
his body covered with sores,
Step by step he traveled
till he came to his father's city,

Hiring himself out along the way,
finally reached his father's house.
Right then the old man
was inside his gate,

Under a great jeweled canopy,
sitting on a lion throne,
Surrounded by his household,
people attending and guarding him,

Some was calculating
gold and silver treasures,
Recording incoming and outgoing wealth,
making notes on ledgers.

The poor son saw his father -
so wealthy and honorable and stern -
Thought he must be a king
or somebody equal to a king,

Got scared, startled at himself,
wondering why he came to this place.
He said to himself:
'If I stay here long,

I might get pressed into service,
forced to work against my will.'
Soon as he thought that,
he turned and ran away,

Asked directions to the poor part of town,
wanted to go hire himself out.
Right then the old man
was sitting on that lion throne,

Saw his son from a distance,
recognized him without saying a word,
Immediately told his servants:
'Go chase him down and bring him here!'

The poor son cried out in fear,
fainted and fell to the ground:
'This man grabbing me
must be fixing to kill me -

Why I need food and clothes
if they bringing me here to die?'
The old man knew his son
was foolish and narrow-minded,

Wouldn't believe what he said,
wouldn't believe he was the father.
So he used loving tricks,
sent some other people instead,

One-eyed and ugly,
didn't have no impressive appearance:
'Tell him we'll hire him,
say we'll pay him wages,

To clear away dung and garbage,
we'll pay him double price.'
The poor son heard this
and came along, happy,

Cleared away the dung,
cleaned out all the rooms.
The old man from his window
kept watching his son,

Thought about the boy's foolishness,
how he enjoyed lowly work.
So the old man
put on shabby dirty clothes,

Picked up a dung-clearing tool,
went to where his son was,
Used loving tricks to get close,
told him to work hard:

'I'll raise your wages,
put oil on your feet,
Give you plenty to eat,
thick warm bedding,

Like this' - rough words -
'you better work hard!'
Then gentle words:
'You like my own son.'

The old man was wise,
gradually let him come and go,
For twenty years
he handled household business,

Showed him the gold and silver,
genuine pearls and crystal,
All the goods coming in and out -
let him know about all of it.

But he still stayed outside the gate,
sleeping in a grass hut,
Thinking his poor thoughts:
'I don't own none of this.'

The father knew his son's mind
was gradually growing bigger,
Wanted to give him the wealth,
so he gathered his relatives,

The king and great ministers,
nobles and householders.
Before this great assembly
he said: 'This is my son,

He left me and went away
for fifty years.
Since I saw my son return,
it's been twenty years,

Long ago in a certain city
I lost this boy,
Went searching everywhere,
finally came to this place.

Everything I own -
house and people -
I'm giving it all to him,
let him use it as he wishes.'

The son remembered his past poverty,
his low-down mentality,
Now at his father's place
he received great treasures,

Including the house
and all the wealth,
He was filled with joy,
something he never knew before.

The Buddha's like that too,
knew I loved small things,
Never came right out and said:
'Y'all gonna be Buddhas.'

Instead he said we
attained the outflows-free state,
Accomplished the Small Vehicle,
disciples who are hearers.

The Buddha commanded us
to preach the supreme path:
'Those who cultivate this
will become Buddhas.'

We followed the Buddha's teaching,
preached to the great Bodhisattvas,
Using all kinds of causes and conditions,
various parables,

Many different words,
teaching the supreme path.
The Buddha's children
heard the Dharma from us,

Thought about it day and night,
practiced diligently,
Then the Buddhas
gave them their prophecies:

'In a future age
you'll become Buddhas.'
All the Buddhas'
secret treasury teachings,

They just preached them to Bodhisattvas,
taught the real truth,
But didn't preach to us
these essential teachings.

Like that poor son
who got close to his father -
Even though he knew about all the goods,
his heart didn't wish to take them.

We preached
the Buddha's Dharma treasury,
But had no aspiration ourselves -
it was just like that.

Inside ourselves, we extinguished afflictions,
thought that was enough,
Only understood this one thing,
didn't think there was nothing else.

When we heard about
purifying Buddha lands,
Teaching and transforming beings,
we had no joy in it at all.

Why was that?
All dharmas
Are completely empty and still,
no birth and no death,

No great and no small,
no outflows, no conditioned action.
Thinking like this,
we didn't feel no joy.

For long nights
in the Buddha's wisdom
We had no greed, no attachment,
no aspirations at all,

And concerning the Dharma for ourselves,
we thought that was the ultimate.
For long nights
we cultivated empty dharmas,

Got free from the three realms'
suffering and afflictions,
Dwelling in the final body,
the remainder-nirvāṇa.

The Buddha's teaching
achieved the Way without fail,
We thought we'd already
repaid the Buddha's kindness.

Even though we taught
the Buddha's children
The Bodhisattva Dharma
for seeking the Buddha Way,

Regarding this Dharma
we never had no wish or joy.
The Guide Teacher let us be,
observed our minds,

Never pushed us at first
to talk about real benefit.
Like that rich old man
who knew his son's will was low,

Used loving tricks
to soften his heart,
Then after that
gave him all the wealth.

The Buddha's like that too,
showing rare wonders,
Knowing those who love small things,
using loving tricks

To tame their minds,
then teaching great wisdom.
We today
got something we never had,

Never hoped for it before,
but now we got it ourselves,
Like that poor son
receiving infinite treasures.

World-Honored One, today
we got the Way, got the fruit,
In the outflows-free Dharma
we got pure eyes.

For long nights
we kept the Buddha's pure precepts,
Only today
we got the fruit of that,

In the Dharma King's Dharma
we cultivated the holy life for ages,
Now we attained the outflows-free,
supreme great fruit.

We today
are truly hearers,
With the Buddha Way's voice
making everyone hear.

We today
are true Arhats,
Among all the world's
people and gods, māras and Brahmās,

Universally among them
we should receive offerings.
The World-Honored One's great kindness,
using rare wonders,

Showing pity, teaching and transforming,
benefiting us -
Through infinite millions of eons,
who could repay this?

Serving with hands and feet,
bowing with head to ground,
Every kind of offering
can't repay it.

Even if we carried him on our heads,
bore him on both shoulders,
For Ganges' sands of eons,
with wholehearted reverence,

And also offered fine food,
infinite precious robes,
All kinds of bedding,
various medicines,

Ox-head sandalwood
and all kinds of jewels,
Building stupas and temples,
spreading precious cloth on the ground,

All these things
used for making offerings,
For Ganges' sands of eons,
still couldn't repay him.

The Buddhas are rare,
infinite and limitless,
Inconceivable,
great supernatural power,

Outflows-free and unconditioned,
kings of all dharmas,
Able for the sake of the lowly
to endure these matters,

Taking up ordinary people clinging to marks,
teaching according to what's appropriate.
The Buddhas in the Dharma
got complete freedom,

Knowing all beings'
various desires and joys,
And their will-power,
according to what they can handle,

Using infinite parables
to teach the Dharma for them,
According to beings'
past good roots,

Also knowing those who are ripe
and those not yet ripe,
Calculating in various ways,
discriminating and knowing,

On the One Vehicle path,
teaching three according to what's appropriate.

### END OF CHAPTER FOUR: FAITH AND UNDERSTANDING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

Chapter 4:

This blues vernacular rendering of the Prodigal Son parable preserves the emotional architecture of Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese while interpreting it into the idiom of the blues tradition - a tradition that knows intimately about leaving home, hard times, shame, fear, fathers who wait, and unexpected inheritance.

Key metaphorical choices:
- "One day's wages" for nirvāṇa - the limited payment that seemed like everything
- "Dung-shoveling" as the humble spiritual work the disciples thought was all they were fit for
- The father's patient loving tricks shown through concrete gestures: changing clothes, picking up the shovel, working alongside
- The son's "low-down feeling" and "poverty consciousness" as psychological realism
- The inheritance "coming naturally" without seeking - grace, not achievement

The parable tracks the journey from:
 recognition of true identity
 heir
 knowing the wealth was always yours
 joy and homecoming

This is the blues truth: sometimes what's keeping us poor ain't the world - it's what we think about ourselves. And sometimes there's a father who's been waiting all along, ready to give us everything, soon as we ready to receive it.

# CHAPTER FIVE: THE PARABLE OF THE MEDICINAL HERBS

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
A Blues Interpretation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese

---

## THE BUDDHA PRAISES MAHĀKĀŚYAPA

Now when the Buddha heard what Mahākāśyapa and them other great disciples had to say, he smiled and said:

"That's right, that's right, Kāśyapa. You done spoke the truth about the Tathāgata - you told it plain. But let me tell you something: the Tathāgata got powers and virtues you ain't even started to count yet. You could talk for a billion kalpas - lifetimes stacked on lifetimes - and still wouldn't get to the end of what the Buddha can do.

"Listen here, Kāśyapa: The Tathāgata is the king of all dharmas - king of all truth, you hear me? And when the Buddha speaks, ain't nothing false come out his mouth. Every teaching he gives, he gives it with wisdom and loving tricks, and every teaching leads straight to the ground of all-knowledge. The Tathāgata sees where every dharma is heading, knows where every truth is going. He knows what's deep in every living being's heart, knows what they're doing, thinking, walking toward. Nothing blocks his sight. He sees clear through to the bottom of all things, and he shows all beings the path to perfect wisdom."

## THE PARABLE OF THE ONE RAIN

"Now Kāśyapa, let me tell you a story. Let me paint you a picture:

"Imagine the whole world - this whole three-thousand-fold universe with all its mountains and valleys, its streams and flatlands, every inch of dirt and rock. And growing up from that ground you got all kinds of plants - you got bushes and forests, you got medicinal herbs and wildflowers, every kind and color you can name. All different, you understand? Different names, different natures, different ways of being.

"Now here come a great cloud - a mighty rain cloud - spreading across the whole sky, covering this entire three-thousand-fold world. And that cloud, it opens up and lets the rain fall. One rain, falling everywhere at once. The rain falls equal on every living thing.

"And all them bushes and forests, all them medicinal herbs - they drinking that rain according to what they are:

"The little herbs with little roots, little stems, little branches, little leaves - they take what they can take.

"The medium herbs with medium roots, medium stems, medium branches, medium leaves - they take what they need.

"The big herbs with big roots, big stems, big branches, big leaves - they drink deep.

"And the trees - small trees and great trees, high and low - every one of them receives according to its nature.

"One cloud. One rain. But each plant takes what it can hold. Each grows according to its own kind - each puts out its flowers and fruit according to what it is.

"You see? They all growing from the same earth, all drinking from the same rain, but each plant different from the next. The rain don't pick and choose. The rain just falls."

## THE APPLICATION - THE BUDDHA IS LIKE THE RAIN

"Now Kāśyapa, understand this: The Tathāgata is just like that. When the Buddha appears in this world, he's like that great cloud rising up. His voice - his great voice - spreads everywhere, reaching the gods and humans and asuras, covering the whole world like that cloud covers the three-thousand-fold universe.

"And the Buddha stands before the multitude and declares:

"'I am the Tathāgata - the One Who Comes Thus. I am the Arhat, the Perfectly Enlightened One, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed One, the Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, the Buddha, the World-Honored One.

"'Those who ain't crossed over yet - I'll get them across. Those who ain't understood yet - I'll make it clear. Those who ain't found peace yet - I'll give them rest. Those who ain't reached nirvāṇa yet - I'll lead them there.

"'I know this present world and the world to come. I know them as they truly are. I'm the all-knowing one, the all-seeing one. I know the path, I opened the path, I teach the path.

"'So all you gods and humans and asuras - come on here. Come and listen to the Dharma.'

"And when the Buddha calls like that, countless millions of living beings come to hear the teaching.

"Now the Tathāgata looks out at all them beings and sees who got sharp faculties and who got dull ones, who's diligent and who's lazy. And according to what each one can bear, he teaches the Dharma - gives it to them in countless different ways, makes each one glad, helps each one get some benefit.

"And these beings, when they hear this teaching, they find peace in this present life. And when they die, they born in good places. They experience joy through practicing the path. They get to hear the Dharma again. And hearing it, they break through all the obstacles. According to their strength - according to what power they got - they gradually enter the Way.

"Just like that great cloud raining down on all the bushes and forests and medicinal herbs, and each plant - according to its kind - receives the moisture and grows.

## THE ONE FLAVOR OF LIBERATION

"Listen: When the Tathāgata teaches the Dharma, it's one mark, one flavor. What flavor is that? The flavor of liberation - the mark of freedom, the mark of letting go, the mark of extinction. It all leads to one place: the ground of all-knowing wisdom.

"Now the beings who hear the Tathāgata's teaching - who uphold it, read it, recite it, practice it just like it's taught - they don't even know themselves what merit they're getting. Why not? Because only the Tathāgata knows. Only the Buddha sees clear.

"The Buddha knows each being's mark and nature. He knows what they're thinking about, what they're pondering, what they're practicing. How they think, how they ponder, how they practice. What dharma they're thinking about, what dharma they're pondering, what dharma they're practicing. What dharma they're using to attain what dharma.

"These beings living in all different stations - only the Tathāgata sees them as they really are, sees them clear with no obstruction.

"Just like them bushes and forests and medicinal herbs - they don't know their own nature, don't know if they're high, middle, or low. But the Tathāgata knows.

"The Buddha knows this is the one-mark, one-flavor Dharma - the mark of liberation, the mark of freedom, the mark of letting go, the ultimate mark of nirvāṇa, the always-still-and-quiet mark that ends in emptiness.

"And knowing this, the Buddha watches what beings want and need, and he protects them. That's why he don't just come right out and teach the all-knowing wisdom straight away. He waits. He watches. He knows the time.

"Now you, Kāśyapa, and the rest of you - it's rare what you done. You understood that the Tathāgata teaches according to what's appropriate. You believed it. You accepted it. Why is that rare? Because when the buddhas - the World-Honored Ones - teach according to what's appropriate, that teaching is hard to grasp, hard to understand."

## THE VERSES: THE PARABLE TOLD AGAIN IN SONG

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say this teaching again, spoke it in verse:

The Dharma King who breaks through existence
Appears in this world,
Teaching in all kinds of ways
According to what beings desire.

The Tathāgata - honored and revered,
His wisdom deep and far-reaching -
He kept this essential teaching quiet for a long time,
Wasn't in no hurry to speak it out.

Those with wisdom, when they hear it,
They can believe and understand.
Those without wisdom doubt and regret,
And they lose it forever.

That's why, Kāśyapa,
The Buddha teaches according to people's power,
Using all kinds of causes and conditions
To help them get to right seeing.

Now Kāśyapa, understand:
It's like a great cloud
Rising up in the world,
Spreading over everything.

That wisdom-cloud full of moisture,
Lightning flashing bright,
Thunder rolling in the distance,
Making everybody glad.

The sun's light gets covered over,
The ground gets cool,
The cloud hangs low and thick,
Looking like you could reach up and grab it.

And that rain falls equal everywhere -
North, south, east, west -
Pouring down without measure,
Soaking the whole earth.

In the mountain valleys and dangerous ravines,
In the hidden and secret places,
All the grasses and medicinal herbs grow,
Big trees and little trees,

The hundred grains and crops,
Sugarcane and grape vines -
The rain moistens them all,
None go without.

The dry ground gets watered everywhere,
The herbs and trees flourish together.
That cloud pours out
One flavor of water.

But the grasses and trees and forests -
Each takes its portion of moisture.
All the trees -
High ones, middle ones, low ones -

According to their size,
Each grows as it can.
Roots, stems, branches, leaves,
Flowers, fruits, colors, and light -

All made fresh by that one rain.
According to their body, their nature,
Their portion, their size,
The moisture is one

But each flourishes in its own way.
The Buddha is like this too,
Appearing in this world
Like that great cloud covering everything.

When he comes into the world,
He teaches beings
And explains the truth
Of all things.

The Great Holy World-Honored One
Among all the gods and humans,
In the whole assembly,
Declares and says:

"I am the Tathāgata,
Most honored among two-legged beings.
I appear in the world
Like a great cloud

Giving moisture to all
The dried-up living beings,
Making them all leave suffering behind
And find peace and joy and happiness -

The happiness of this world
And the happiness of nirvāṇa.

"You gods and humans,
Listen with one mind!
Come here, all of you,
To see the Unsurpassed Honored One.

"I am the World-Honored One -
Ain't nobody can compare.
To give peace to living beings,
That's why I appear in the world.

"I teach the assembly
The pure Dharma like sweet dew.
That Dharma is one flavor:
Liberation and nirvāṇa.

"With one wonderful sound
I explain this meaning,
Always making great causes and conditions
For the Great Vehicle.

"I look at everything
And see it all equal -
No this and that,
No love and hate.

"I got no greed or attachment,
No limits or obstructions.
I always teach everybody
With the same equal teaching.

"Whether it's one person
Or a crowd of many,
It's all the same to me.
I'm always teaching the Dharma -

"Never doing nothing else.
Coming, going, sitting, standing,
I never get tired.
I fill up the world

"Like rain soaking everything.
High-class and low-class,
Those who keep the precepts and those who break them,
Those with perfect dignity and those without,

"Right views and wrong views,
Sharp faculties and dull faculties -
I rain the Dharma rain on all of them equally,
Never getting tired or lazy.

"All living beings
Who hear my teaching
Receive it according to their power
And dwell in various stations.

"Some dwelling among humans and gods,
Wheel-turning sage kings,
Kings like Śakra and Brahmā -
These are the small medicinal herbs.

"Those who know the undefiled Dharma,
Who can attain nirvāṇa,
Who raise up the six spiritual powers
And attain the three clear knowledges,

"Who dwell alone in mountain forests,
Always practicing meditation and stillness,
Who attain the realization of the Pratyekabuddha -
These are the medium medicinal herbs.

"Those who seek the World-Honored One's station,
Saying 'I will become Buddha,'
Who practice with diligence and concentration -
These are the superior medicinal herbs.

"And the Buddha's children
Who give their whole mind to the Buddha's path,
Always practicing kindness and compassion,
Knowing they themselves will become buddhas,

"Certain without any doubt -
These are called small trees.
Those who abide in spiritual powers,
Who turn the wheel that don't turn back,

"Who save countless hundreds of thousands
Of millions of living beings -
Bodhisattvas like this
Are called great trees.

"The Buddha's equal teaching
Is like that one-flavor rain,
But according to beings' natures,
What they receive is different -

"Just like them grasses and trees,
Each takes in different amounts.
The Buddha uses this parable
As loving tricks to reveal and show,

"Using all kinds of words
To teach the one Dharma.
In relation to the Buddha's wisdom,
It's like one drop from the ocean.

"I rain the Dharma rain,
Filling up the whole world.
This one-flavor Dharma -
People practice it according to their power,

"Just like them forests,
Medicinal herbs and trees,
According to their size,
Gradually growing more and more lush.

"The buddhas' Dharma
Is always this one flavor,
Making all the worlds
Reach complete fulfillment.

"Through gradual practice,
All attain the fruit of the path.
The śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas
Dwelling in mountain forests,

"Living in their final bodies,
Hearing the Dharma and attaining the fruit -
These are called medicinal herbs,
Each getting its increase.

"If there are bodhisattvas
Whose wisdom is firm and solid,
Who understand the three realms completely
And seek the supreme vehicle -

"These are called small trees,
Getting their increase.
Then there are those who dwell in meditation,
Who attain spiritual powers,

"Who hear about the emptiness of all dharmas
And their hearts are filled with great joy,
Who emit countless lights
And save many living beings -

"These are called great trees,
Getting their increase.
Like this, Kāśyapa,
The Dharma the Buddha teaches

"Is like that great cloud
With its one-flavor rain
Moistening human flowers,
Each bearing its fruit.

"Kāśyapa, you should know:
Using all kinds of causes and conditions,
All kinds of parables,
I reveal and show the Buddha's path.

"This is my loving tricks.
All buddhas are like this too.
Now for you all
I'm telling the most real thing:

"All you in the śrāvaka assembly -
You ain't reached extinction yet.
What you're practicing right now,
That's the bodhisattva path.

"If you gradually study and learn,
You all will become buddhas."

---
### END OF CHAPTER FIVE: THE PARABLE OF THE MEDICINAL HERBS

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

Chapter 5

This interpretation renders Chapter 5 (藥草喻品) into blues vernacular, maintaining the core teaching while making it accessible through everyday language and imagery familiar to the African American oral tradition.

**Key Metaphors:**

THE RAIN = The Buddha's teaching, falling equally on everyone
THE PLANTS = Living beings with different capacities (small herbs, medium herbs, large herbs, small trees, great trees)
ONE FLAVOR = The single truth of liberation taught in many ways
THE GROWTH = Each being's progress according to their nature and capacity

**The Five Types of Plants:**

1. **Small medicinal herbs** (小藥草) = Humans, gods, worldly kings - those seeking worldly happiness
2. **Medium medicinal herbs** (中藥草) = Śrāvakas and Pratyekabuddhas seeking personal liberation
3. **Superior medicinal herbs** (上藥草) = Beginning bodhisattvas aspiring to buddhahood
4. **Small trees** (小樹) = Established bodhisattvas certain of their future buddhahood
5. **Great trees** (大樹) = Advanced bodhisattvas with spiritual powers saving countless beings

**Core Teaching:**

This parable addresses the paradox at the heart of the Lotus Sutra: How can there be "one vehicle" when beings seem to attain different results? The answer: The teaching is one (like one rain), but beings receive it according to their capacity (like different plants). The rain doesn't discriminate - the plants take what they can hold. Similarly, the Buddha teaches the one Dharma of liberation, but beings understand and practice at different levels.

The blues vernacular makes this democratic vision concrete: like rain falling on rich and poor alike, like grace that doesn't pick favorites, the Buddha's teaching is available to everyone. What you receive depends on what you can hold, what you're ready for - but the offering is universal and impartial.

The final revelation (lines 1697-1699) is crucial: Even the śrāvakas who think they've reached final nirvāṇa are actually on the bodhisattva path - they just don't know it yet. They're still growing. The rain is still falling. The journey continues.

**Why Blues Language?**

The blues tradition understands:
- Universal suffering and universal grace
- The democracy of hardship and hope
- One truth received differently by different people
- The patience of gradual growth
- The impartiality of what falls from heaven (rain, grace, teaching)

This parable, in the blues telling, becomes a song about how wisdom comes to everyone but each person takes what they can hold - and that's not failure, that's nature. The rain keeps falling. You keep growing. And one day, that little herb might become a mighty tree.

# CHAPTER SIX: THE NAMING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
(When the Teacher Calls Out Who You're Gonna Be)

Now when the World-Honored One finished singing that verse, he turned to all the folks gathered round and he said it loud and clear:

"I got something to tell you about this disciple of mine, Mahākāśyapa. I see him. I see who he's gonna be. In the world that's coming, he gonna meet three hundred million billion Buddhas. Three hundred million billion! He gonna serve them, honor them, respect them, praise them. He gonna spread the boundless teaching far and wide. And then—you hear me now—in his very last body, he gonna become a Buddha himself.

"His name gonna be Light Brightness Tathāgata, the Worthy One, the Perfectly Awakened, the One Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed Guide, the Tamer of Hearts, the Teacher of Gods and Human Folk, the Buddha, the World-Honored One.

"His world gonna be called Light and Virtue. The age gonna be called Great Majesty. That Buddha gonna live twelve small ages. The true Dharma gonna stand for twenty small ages, and the reflected Dharma gonna stand another twenty.

"And let me tell you about that land: It gonna be beautiful. No filth, no rubble, no thorns, no waste. That ground gonna be level—no hills, no valleys, no pits, no bumps. The ground gonna be made of lapis lazuli, and treasure trees gonna stand in rows. Golden ropes gonna mark the boundaries of the roads, and precious flowers gonna be scattered everywhere. That whole land gonna be clean, clean, clean.

"In that country, there gonna be countless thousands of billions of bodhisattvas. The voice-hearers gonna be beyond counting too. There won't be no trouble from demons—though demons and demon-folk will be there, they all gonna protect the Buddha's teaching."

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say it again in song, spoke these verses:

I'm telling you monks here,
I'm telling you what I see with my Buddha-eye—
I see Kassapa clear,
I see where he's going by and by.

After ages beyond counting,
He gonna become a Buddha too.
In the world that's coming,
He gonna serve and honor what's true—

Three hundred million billion Buddhas,
World-Honored Ones every one.
For the sake of Buddha-wisdom,
That pure holy life gonna be done.

After serving the highest,
The Two-Footed Honored Ones,
After learning all wisdom,
The unsurpassed wisdom that comes—

In his very last body,
He gonna become a Buddha at last.
That land gonna be pure and holy,
Lapis lazuli ground stretched vast.

Many treasure trees standing,
All lined up along the road,
Golden ropes dividing,
Joy in everyone who beholds.

Sweet fragrance always rising,
Flowers of every name scattered round,
All manner of wonders surprising,
Making that whole land renowned.

That ground gonna be level,
No hills and no pits to see.
Bodhisattvas beyond measure,
More than you could count, more than could be—

Their hearts gonna be gentle,
They gonna have mighty spiritual power,
Holding to the Buddha-teaching central,
The Great Vehicle every hour.

Voice-hearers beyond counting,
In their final flawless frame,
Sons and daughters of the Dharma-King mounting,
More than you could name—

Even with the eye of heaven
You couldn't count them all.
That Buddha gonna live eleven,
Twelve small ages standing tall.

The true Dharma gonna last
Twenty small ages true,
The reflected Dharma past
Twenty small ages too.

Light Brightness, World-Honored One—
This is how it's gonna be done.

***

Now at that time, Mahāmaudgalyāyana and Subhūti and Mahākātyāyana, they all started trembling. They put their palms together and they looked up at the Honored One's face and they couldn't take their eyes away. And together, with one voice, they sang out in verse:

Great Hero, World-Honored One,
Dharma-King of all Śākya's line,
Have mercy on us, every one,
And give us that Buddha-voice divine.

If you know what's in our deep heart,
If you see us and call our name,
It'll be like cool water when the heat departs,
Like sweet dew putting out the flame.

Like a man from a hungry land
Who suddenly sees a royal feast—
His heart still scared to understand,
Still afraid, to say the least.

He don't dare just eat right away,
Not till the king himself says "Come."
Then and only then, that day,
He knows it's safe and he ain't dumb.

We're just like that, you know—
We been thinking about our small-vehicle mistake.
We don't know how we gonna go
And get that Buddha-wisdom for wisdom's sake.

We heard you say with your Buddha-voice
That we all gonna become Buddhas too,
But our hearts still scared to rejoice,
Still afraid it ain't really true.

But if you name us, if you call our name,
If you give us that prophecy—
Then we gonna be happy, and ain't no shame,
Then we gonna rest easy.

Great Hero, World-Honored One,
You always trying to give the world peace—
Please give us that naming, every one,
Like a hungry man needs to feast.

***

Now the World-Honored One, he knew what was in his great disciples' hearts, and he turned to all the monks and said:

"This Subhūti here—in the world that's coming, he gonna meet and serve three hundred million billion nayuta Buddhas. He gonna serve them, honor them, respect them, praise them. He gonna practice that pure holy life always. He gonna complete the bodhisattva way. And then in his very last body, he gonna become a Buddha.

"His name gonna be Fame Mark Tathāgata, the Worthy One, the Perfectly Awakened, the One Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed Guide, the Tamer of Hearts, the Teacher of Gods and Human Folk, the Buddha, the World-Honored One.

"The age gonna be called Having Treasure. The world gonna be called Treasure Born.

"That land gonna be level, with crystal for the ground. Treasure trees gonna make it beautiful. No hills, no pits, no sand, no gravel, no thorns, no filth. Precious flowers gonna cover the ground, clean all over. The people of that land gonna live in jeweled pavilions, in wonderful towers and terraces.

"The voice-hearer disciples gonna be beyond measure, beyond counting—you couldn't calculate them with numbers or comparisons. The bodhisattvas gonna number in the countless thousands of millions of billions of nayutas.

"That Buddha gonna live twelve small ages. The true Dharma gonna stand twenty small ages, and the reflected Dharma gonna stand another twenty.

"That Buddha gonna dwell always in emptiness, teaching the Dharma to the assembly, liberating countless bodhisattvas and voice-hearers."

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say it again in song, spoke these verses:

You monks gathered here,
I'm telling you now,
Listen with your whole heart clear,
Hear what I'm gonna avow.

My great disciple here,
Subhūti, this one—
He gonna become a Buddha, it's clear,
His name gonna be Fame Mark, the Honored One.

He gonna serve countless,
Millions and billions of Buddhas every one,
Following where each Buddha goes,
Step by step till the great way is done.

In his very last body earned,
The thirty-two marks gonna be his,
Handsome and beautiful, we learned,
Like a treasure mountain is.

That Buddha's land gonna be
The purest and the best,
And everyone who comes to see
Gonna love it, gonna be blessed.

That Buddha in the middle there
Gonna liberate countless souls.
In that Buddha's teaching, everywhere,
Many bodhisattvas gonna fill the roles—

All of them sharp and keen,
Turning the wheel that don't turn back.
That country always gonna be seen
Full of bodhisattvas, nothing lack.

Voice-hearers beyond all count,
All perfected in the threefold knowledge,
The six spiritual powers paramount,
Dwelling in the eightfold liberation's privilege.

That Buddha's teaching gonna be
A display of boundless power,
Spiritual transformations you gotta see,
Beyond thought every hour.

Gods and human folk there
Countless as the Ganges sand,
All with their palms together,
Listening to the Buddha's word firsthand.

That Buddha gonna live, we're told,
Twelve small ages long.
True Dharma twenty ages old,
Reflected Dharma twenty ages strong.

***

Then the World-Honored One turned again to the assembly and said:

"Now I'm telling you about this Great Kātyāyana here. In the world that's coming, he gonna serve and honor eight thousand billion Buddhas with all manner of offerings. He gonna respect them, revere them.

"After each of those Buddhas passes on, he gonna build them a stupa—a thousand yojanas high, five hundred yojanas wide and deep, all perfectly square. He gonna make them out of gold and silver and lapis lazuli and mother-of-pearl and agate and pearl and rose quartz—all seven precious things combined. He gonna offer to those stupas flowers and garlands, ointments and incense powders and burning incense, silk canopies and banners.

"After that's done, he gonna serve another twenty million billion Buddhas, just the same way.

"After serving all those Buddhas, after completing the bodhisattva way, he gonna become a Buddha himself.

"His name gonna be Jambū River Gold Light Tathāgata, the Worthy One, the Perfectly Awakened, the One Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed Guide, the Tamer of Hearts, the Teacher of Gods and Human Folk, the Buddha, the World-Honored One.

"That land gonna be level, with crystal for the ground. Treasure trees gonna make it beautiful. Golden ropes gonna mark the roads. Wonderful flowers gonna cover the ground, clean all over, and everyone who sees it gonna rejoice.

"There won't be no four evil paths there—no hell, no hungry ghosts, no animals, no fighting demons. There gonna be many gods and many human folk, many voice-hearers and many bodhisattvas—countless millions and billions beautifying that land.

"That Buddha gonna live twelve small ages. The true Dharma gonna stand twenty small ages, and the reflected Dharma gonna stand another twenty."

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say it again in song, spoke these verses:

All you monks here,
Listen with one heart,
What I'm saying is true and clear,
Ain't no false part.

This Kātyāyana here,
He gonna serve with all kinds of
Beautiful offerings sincere
All the Buddhas above.

After those Buddhas gone,
He gonna raise up seven-jeweled towers,
He gonna offer flowers and incense on
Those relics for hours and hours.

In his very last frame,
He gonna gain the Buddha-wisdom complete,
The perfect awakening by name.
That land gonna be pure and sweet,

Liberating countless souls,
Millions and billions set free,
All the ten directions' goals—
They gonna honor him, you see.

That Buddha's light gonna be
Unmatched, can't be outdone.
That Buddha's name gonna be
Jambū River Gold Light, the Honored One.

Bodhisattvas and voice-hearers there,
All existence cut through and done—
Countless, beyond compare,
Beautifying that land, every one.

***

Then the World-Honored One turned once more to the great assembly and said:

"Now I'm telling you about Great Maudgalyāyana here. He gonna serve eight thousand Buddhas with all manner of offerings, honoring them, revering them.

"After each of those Buddhas passes on, he gonna build them a stupa—a thousand yojanas high, five hundred yojanas wide and deep, all perfectly square. He gonna make them out of gold and silver and lapis lazuli and mother-of-pearl and agate and pearl and rose quartz—all seven precious things combined. He gonna offer to those stupas flowers and garlands, ointments and incense powders and burning incense, silk canopies and banners.

"After that's done, he gonna serve another two hundred million billion Buddhas, just the same way.

"Then he gonna become a Buddha himself.

"His name gonna be Tamāla Sandalwood Fragrance Tathāgata, the Worthy One, the Perfectly Awakened, the One Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed Guide, the Tamer of Hearts, the Teacher of Gods and Human Folk, the Buddha, the World-Honored One.

"The age gonna be called Joy Fulfilled. The world gonna be called Delightful.

"That land gonna be level, with crystal for the ground. Treasure trees gonna make it beautiful. Pearls gonna be scattered like flowers, clean all over, and everyone who sees it gonna rejoice.

"There gonna be many gods and many human folk, many bodhisattvas and many voice-hearers, beyond all counting.

"That Buddha gonna live twenty-four small ages. The true Dharma gonna stand forty small ages, and the reflected Dharma gonna stand another forty."

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say it again in song, spoke these verses:

This disciple of mine here,
Great Maudgalyāyana by name—
When he sheds this body, it's clear,
He gonna see eighty-two hundred million

Buddhas, World-Honored Ones.
For the sake of the Buddha-way, you understand,
He gonna serve with honor, every one,
Everything that the teaching demands.

In the presence of all those Buddhas there,
He gonna practice that pure life holy,
For countless ages everywhere
He gonna uphold the Buddha-Dharma solely.

After those Buddhas passed away,
He gonna raise up seven-jeweled towers,
Long poles with golden banners to display,
Offerings of music, incense, and flowers

To honor all those Buddha-stupas high.
Step by step completing, you can tell,
The bodhisattva way by and by,
In the land called Delightful he gonna dwell.

There he gonna become a Buddha true,
Called Tamāla Sandalwood Fragrance by name.
That Buddha's life—I'm telling you—
Twenty-four ages, that's his claim.

Always for gods and human folk
Teaching the Buddha-way profound.
Voice-hearers beyond what could be spoke,
Like Ganges River sands, that's how they're found—

All perfected in the threefold sight,
The six spiritual powers theirs to claim,
Having great majestic might.
Bodhisattvas you can't number or name,

Their will set firm, their effort strong,
In Buddha-wisdom never turning back.
After that Buddha's gone,
True Dharma gonna stand—ain't nothing it lack—

Forty small ages long,
Reflected Dharma just the same.
My disciples, healthy and strong,
Five hundred of them I'm gonna name—

All of them I'm gonna call,
In the world that's coming they gonna be
Buddhas, every one and all.
Me and you, this company—

We got causes from way back when,
From ancient lifetimes, you and me.
What I'm about to tell you then,
You listen good, you gonna see.

***

### END OF CHAPTER SIX: THE NAMING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

Chapter 6

This chapter is all about **being seen and being named**—one of the most powerful acts in any tradition. The Buddha is saying: "I see you. I know who you are. And I'm gonna tell you who you're going to become."

**Key Metaphors and Choices:**

1. **"The Naming" as the chapter title** - In blues and gospel traditions, naming carries profound power. To be named is to be called into existence, to have your destiny spoken aloud. The Buddha isn't just predicting; he's recognizing and affirming who these disciples already are and will be.

2. **"I see him clear, I see where he's going by and by"** - The blues has always been about seeing clearly, about truth-telling. The Buddha's vision isn't mystical abstraction; it's recognition, the way a master bluesman sees the music in a young player.

3. **The disciples' request: "Like a hungry man needs to feast"** - They use this metaphor themselves in the Chinese text. The hunger for recognition, for validation, for being seen—this is human hunger at its deepest. They're saying: "We heard you say it, but we're still scared to believe it. We need you to say our names."

4. **Each prophecy's details matter** - The specifics aren't filler. Each Buddha-name, each world-name, each measurement tells these disciples: "This is real. This is particular. This is YOUR future, not just anybody's." That's why I kept the details—the twelve ages, the twenty ages, the crystal ground, the jeweled towers. Particularity is how love speaks.

5. **"In his very last body"** - This phrase recurs like a refrain, like the turn in a blues verse. It marks the culmination, the arrival, the moment when all that walking finally gets you home.

6. **The Buddha's epithets** - I kept the full tenfold list for each Buddha-to-be because in oral tradition, repetition isn't redundancy—it's respect, it's rhythm, it's the way you mark something sacred.

The emotional arc is everything here: The disciples are trembling, palms together, unable to look away. They're hungry. They're scared to hope. And then the Buddha calls each name, one by one, and tells them exactly who they're going to be. That's the blues truth: Sometimes you need someone who can see to tell you who you are, so you can walk into who you're gonna be.

# CHAPTER SEVEN: THE PHANTOM CITY BLUES

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
A Blues Interpretation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese

---

## THE STORY OF TIME SO DEEP YOU CAN'T EVEN COUNT IT

Now the Buddha, he gathered them monks around him and said:

"Listen here, brothers and sisters. I'm gonna tell you about something that happened so far back, so long ago, that your mind can't even stretch that far. We talking about time past counting, time past measuring, time past imagining—we talking about uncountable cosmic ages ago.

Back in that time, there was a Buddha. His name was Great Universal Wisdom Excellence—that's what they called him: the Tathāgata, the Arhat, the Perfectly Enlightened One, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, the Well-Gone One, Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed One, the Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, the Buddha, the World-Honored One. His country was called Good City, and that cosmic age was called Great Mark.

Now brothers and sisters, listen up. That Buddha passed on into final rest so long ago, the time's so vast you can't wrap your head around it. Let me paint you a picture:

Imagine you took all the earth in three thousand great thousand-fold worlds—every bit of dirt, every grain of sand, every rock and stone—and you ground it all down into ink, finest ink you ever saw. Then you start walking east, and after you pass a thousand worlds, you put down one tiny drop, small as a speck of dust. Then you walk another thousand worlds, and you put down another tiny drop. And you keep on doing this, walking and dropping, walking and dropping, until all that ink is gone, every last bit used up.

Now tell me—could any mathematician, any accountant, any number-cruncher or their best student—could they count up all them worlds you passed through, both the ones where you dropped ink and the ones you didn't? Could they reach the edge of that number and tell you what it is?"

"No, World-Honored One," they said. "They couldn't do it."

"Brothers," the Buddha said, "take all them worlds that man passed through—the ones with ink drops and the ones without—and grind them all down into dust. Every single speck of dust, that's one cosmic age. The time since that Buddha Great Universal Wisdom Excellence passed into final rest is even longer than that—we talking countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of uncountable ages beyond even that number.

But I'm telling you true: with the power of the Tathāgata's knowing and seeing, when I look back at that distant time, it's clear as yesterday. I can see it like it's happening right now."

---

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say it again so it sink down deep, spoke it out in verse:

I remember way back in the past,
Countless ages, time so vast,
There was a Buddha, honored and true,
Named Great Universal Wisdom Excellence, through and through.

Like someone grinding with all their might,
Three thousand great thousand worlds to dust so light,
Taking all that earth and making it ink,
More than you could count or even think.

Passing through a thousand worlds, you go,
Drop one speck of dust, let it show,
Keep on moving, dropping as you roam,
Till all that dust-ink finds a home.

All them worlds where you dropped or not,
Grind them down, every single spot,
Turn it all to dust, don't miss a grain—
Each dust speck equals one age's span, I'm saying plain.

These countless specks of dust you see,
The ages since that Buddha passed are even more than these.
That Buddha's been gone, entered into rest,
For unmeasured ages, I attest.

The Tathāgata's wisdom, clear and bright,
Knows when that Buddha passed from sight,
Knows his disciples, the hearers and bodhisattvas too,
Sees them clear as today's morning dew.

You monks should know and understand,
The Buddha's wisdom is pure and grand,
Without outflows, without any wall,
It penetrates through ages, encompasses all.

---

## THE BUDDHA WHO SAT STILL FOR TEN SMALL AGES

The Buddha told them monks:

"This Buddha called Great Universal Wisdom Excellence, he lived for five hundred forty million billion nayuta ages. Now when he first sat down at the place of enlightenment and broke through the demon armies—when he was right there on the verge of getting that supreme perfect enlightenment—the Buddha-truths still wouldn't show themselves to him. They just wouldn't appear.

He sat there like that for one small age. Then ten small ages. Sitting cross-legged, body still, mind still, not moving at all. And still them Buddha-truths wouldn't come into view."

---

## THE HEAVENLY KINGS PREPARE THE THRONE

"Now the gods from the Thirty-Three Heavens, they had already prepared for this Buddha. Under that bodhi tree, they laid out a lion throne, high as a mile up in the air. The Buddha was meant to sit on that throne and attain supreme perfect enlightenment.

Soon as he sat down on that seat, all the Brahma kings rained down heavenly flowers—flowers falling like rain across a hundred miles. A fragrant wind would come and blow away the wilted flowers, and fresh new ones would rain down to take their place. This went on without stopping for ten small ages, making offerings to that Buddha—kept going like that right up till he passed away, always raining them flowers.

The Four Heavenly Kings and their gods, they struck the heavenly drums to make offerings to the Buddha. The other gods played heavenly music. For ten full small ages they kept it up, right until he passed into final rest—even then, they kept on playing."

---

## THE SIXTEEN PRINCES COME TO SEE THEIR FATHER THE BUDDHA

"Brothers," the Buddha said, "after ten small ages passed, the Buddha-truths finally appeared before Great Universal Wisdom Excellence Buddha, and he achieved that supreme perfect enlightenment.

Now before he left home, that Buddha had sixteen sons. The first one was named Wisdom Accumulation. Each of them sons had all kinds of precious toys and treasures they loved to play with. But when they heard their father had achieved that supreme perfect enlightenment, they let go of all them precious things and went to where the Buddha was. Their mothers cried tears and followed along behind them.

Their grandfather was a wheel-turning holy king. He came with a hundred great ministers and hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of other people, all surrounding them, following them to the place of enlightenment. They all wanted to get close to Great Universal Wisdom Excellence Tathāgata, to make offerings, to show respect, to honor and praise him.

When they arrived, they bowed their heads to his feet, walked around him in respect, then put their palms together and gazed up at the World-Honored One. They sang their praise in verse:

Great World-Honored One of mighty virtue and power,
You worked to free all beings hour by hour,
Through countless hundreds of millions of ages long,
Finally became a Buddha, righteous and strong.

All your vows are now complete and whole,
Good fortune! Most excellent goal!
World-Honored One, so rare to see,
You sat for ten small ages patiently.

Your body, hands, and feet so still,
Quiet, peaceful, holding to your will,
Your mind calm and serene always,
Never once scattered in any ways.

Ultimately reaching final silence and peace,
Dwelling firm where outflows cease.
Now we see the World-Honored One
Safe and stable, the Buddha-work done.

We receive this good fortune today,
Celebrate with joy, come what may!
But living beings suffer constant pain,
Blind and lost without a guide to explain.

They don't know the path where suffering ends,
Don't know how to seek freedom, my friends.
Long dark night makes evil paths increase,
While heavenly beings decrease and decrease.

From darkness into darkness they go,
Never hearing the Buddha's name, don't you know.
Now the Buddha's got that highest way,
Safe and stable, where outflows hold no sway.

We along with gods and human folk
Receive the greatest benefit, ain't no joke!
That's why we bow our heads down low,
Take refuge in the Highest One we know."

---

## THE SIXTEEN PRINCES REQUEST THE DHARMA WHEEL

Then them sixteen princes, after they finished singing the Buddha's praise, urged the World-Honored One to turn the Dharma wheel. They all said together:

"World-Honored One, please speak the Dharma! You'll bring safety and peace, compassion and benefit to all the gods and human beings!"

Then they spoke it again in verse:

O World Hero, ain't nobody like you,
Adorned with a hundred blessings, through and through,
You got that supreme wisdom, yes you do—
Please speak it out for all the world to view!

Free us and all the living beings too,
Show us clear, distinguish what is true,
Help us gain this wisdom like you got,
So we can follow in your footsteps, hit that spot.

If we become Buddhas one day soon,
All beings can too, under the same moon.
World-Honored One, you know what's deep inside,
What beings are thinking, what they try to hide.

You know the path that each one walks,
You know their wisdom-power when anybody talks,
Their desires, their joys, the good they cultivate,
The karma from past lives that shapes their fate.

World-Honored One, you know it all complete—
Now turn that supreme wheel, don't miss a beat!

---

## WHEN THE BUDDHA ACHIEVED ENLIGHTENMENT, THE WHOLE UNIVERSE SHOOK
