"And they'll be protected and thought of by all the Buddhas in other lands, Buddhas who are alive right now in those worlds. These people got great faith-power, great will-power, great roots of goodness.

"You need to know: These people sleep in the same room as the Buddha. The Buddha himself lays his hand on their heads. He touches them."

"Medicine King, wherever this sutra is spoken, wherever it's read, wherever it's recited, wherever it's copied down, wherever the scripture-scroll stays—in all those places you should build a stupa made of seven precious materials. Make it extremely high, extremely wide, extremely decorated and beautiful.

"You don't need to put relics in it. You hear me? No need for relics.

"Why? Because this sutra right here already contains the Buddha's whole body. The complete body is already in there.

"This stupa should receive offerings of all kinds of flowers, incense, necklaces, silk canopies, banners and flags, music and singing. People should make offerings, show reverence, honor it, praise it.

"If anybody sees this stupa, bows to it, makes offerings to it—you need to know all these people are getting close to that supreme perfect enlightenment. They're almost there."

"Medicine King, there's a lot of people—whether they left home or still got a home—who say they're walking the bodhisattva path. But if they can't see this sutra, can't hear it, can't read it, can't recite it, can't copy it, can't uphold it, can't make offerings to it—you need to know these people haven't really walked the bodhisattva path the right way. They ain't doing it properly.

"But if there's beings who hear this sutra-teaching, who truly hear it—then they know how to walk the bodhisattva path the right way.

"Any being who's seeking the Buddha-way—if they see this Lotus Sutra or hear it, and after hearing it they believe it, understand it, receive it, uphold it—you need to know this person is getting close to that supreme perfect enlightenment. They're almost home."

"Medicine King, it's like this—imagine there's a person who's thirsty, desperate for water, needs it bad. So they go up on a high plateau, a high dry place, and they start digging, searching for water, working for it.

"At first they just see dry soil, dusty dirt. They know the water's still far off, still way down there.

"But they don't give up. They keep working, keep applying effort, don't quit. After a while they start seeing damp soil, wet dirt. Then gradually they hit mud.

"Their heart becomes certain, becomes settled. They know for sure: Water's definitely close now. It's right there.

"It's the same way with bodhisattvas. If they haven't heard this Lotus Sutra, haven't understood it, haven't been able to practice and cultivate it—you need to know these people are still far from that supreme perfect enlightenment. The water's still way down there.

"But if they hear it, understand it, think about it deeply, practice and cultivate it—then you can be certain, you can know for sure they're close to that supreme perfect enlightenment. The water's right there.

"Why? Because all bodhisattvas' supreme perfect enlightenment belongs to this sutra. This sutra opens the gate of loving tricks. It reveals the true mark of reality. This Lotus Sutra treasury is deep, solid, hidden, distant. Nobody can reach it on their own.

"But now the Buddha teaches it, helps beings mature, brings bodhisattvas to completion, and opens it up for them. Shows them what's been hidden."

"Medicine King, if there's a bodhisattva who hears this Lotus Sutra and gets startled, doubtful, frightened—you need to know that's a brand-new bodhisattva, just started out. That's normal for beginners.

"If a voice-hearer, a disciple, hears this sutra and gets startled, doubtful, frightened—you need to know that's someone full of pride. That's arrogance talking."

"Medicine King, if a good man or good woman wants to teach this Lotus Sutra to the four assemblies after the Buddha's gone—how should they teach it? How should they do it?

"This good man or good woman needs to enter the Buddha's room, wear the Buddha's robe, sit on the Buddha's seat. Only then should they widely teach this sutra to the four assemblies.

"What's the Buddha's room? Great compassion for all beings—that's the Buddha's room.

"What's the Buddha's robe? Gentleness and patience—that's the Buddha's robe.

"What's the Buddha's seat? The emptiness of all things—that's the Buddha's seat.

"Settle yourself in these three. Establish yourself there. Then, with a heart that doesn't slack off, doesn't get lazy, teach this Lotus Sutra to the bodhisattvas and the four assemblies. Teach it wide."

"Medicine King, when I'm in other lands, other countries, I'll send magical people, conjured-up people, to gather an audience to hear the Dharma. I'll send magical monks, nuns, laymen, laywomen to listen to the teaching.

"These magical people, they'll hear the Dharma, believe it, receive it, follow it, not oppose it.

"If the teacher's in a secluded place, an empty quiet place, I'll send a whole host at that time—heavenly dragons, ghosts and spirits, gandharvas, asuras, all kinds of beings—to listen to the teaching.

"Even though I'm in another land, another country, I'll make sure the teacher sees me from time to time. If they forget a line of the sutra, forget the words, I'll appear and tell them again, help them get it complete."

At that time the World-Honored One, wanting to state this meaning again, sings it in verse:

If you want to cast off laziness,
Want to leave all slacking behind,
You should listen to this sutra here,
This sutra's hard to hear, hard to find.

Those who believe and receive it—
They're hard to find too, you see,
Like a person thirsty for water
Digging on a plateau, digging to get free.

Even when they see dry dusty soil,
They know the water's still far below,
But gradually they see damp dirt and mud,
They know for certain the water's close, they know.

Medicine King, you need to understand,
These people are like that, every one:
If they never heard the Lotus Sutra,
They're far from Buddha-wisdom, far from the sun.

But if they hear this deep sutra here,
This teaching that clarifies the voice-hearer's way,
This king of all the sutras,
And after hearing it they think on it, meditate and pray—

You need to know these people
Are close to Buddha-wisdom, getting near,
If somebody teaches this sutra to others,
They should enter the Buddha's room without fear.

They should put on the Buddha's robe,
Sit down on the Buddha's seat,
Then face the crowd without being scared,
Widely explain it, make the teaching complete.

Great compassion—that's the room,
Gentleness and patience—that's the robe you wear,
The emptiness of all things—that's the seat,
Settle in these and teach from there.

If while you're teaching this sutra
Somebody comes with an evil mouth to curse,
With swords and sticks and stones to attack,
Remember the Buddha and bear it—don't rehearse.

In thousands of millions of billions of lands
I show my pure and solid body still,
For countless billions of eons
I teach the Dharma to beings, that's my will.

After I'm gone from this world,
If there's somebody who can teach this sutra true,
I'll send four magical assemblies—
Monks and nuns, laymen and women too—

To make offerings to this Dharma-teacher,
To guide and gather the crowds all around,
To bring the beings together
So they can hear this teaching, hear this sound.

If people want to do evil,
Come with swords and sticks and stones to harm,
I'll send magical people to appear
To guard and protect them, keep them from alarm.

If the teacher's all alone,
In a secluded empty place somewhere,
Quiet and still with no human voices,
Reading and reciting this scripture there—

At that time I'll appear for them,
Show them my pure bright shining form,
If they forget a line or phrase,
I'll tell them again, help them perform.

If somebody has these virtues,
Whether teaching the four assemblies or alone,
Reading and reciting in empty places—
They all get to see me, get to see my throne.

If somebody's in a secluded spot,
I'll send heavenly dragon-kings their way,
Yakṣas, ghosts and spirits,
To be their Dharma-listening audience that day.

This person loves to teach the Dharma,
Explains it clearly without any block,
Because all the Buddhas protect and remember them,
They can make the great crowd rejoice, the whole flock.

If you stay close to a Dharma-teacher,
You'll quickly gain the bodhisattva way,
Follow and learn from this teacher,
You'll see Buddhas like Ganges sands one day.

### END OF CHAPTER TEN: HONOR THE WORD-CARRIERS

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

This chapter is about honoring the carriers of wisdom, the teachers and preachers and truth-speakers who keep the word alive. In the blues/gospel tradition, there's deep reverence for those who carry the song forward, who keep the story going, who stand up and testify even when it's hard. This interpretation tries to honor that tradition.

**Key Interpretive Choices:**

**The Title:** "Honor the Word-Carriers" captures the chapter's essence—this is about respecting those who carry, protect, and transmit the teaching.

**Central Metaphors:**
 damp soil → mud → water is universal human experience. Anyone who's ever dug a well, planted a garden, searched for something underground knows this progression. The metaphor teaches: signs of progress matter; persistence pays off; you can tell you're getting close before you arrive.

- "The Buddha's room, robe, and seat" - Kept these concrete, physical metaphors. Great compassion is a room you enter. Gentleness is a robe you put on. Emptiness is a seat you sit down on. These aren't abstract concepts—they're actions you take before you start teaching.

- "Carried on the Buddha's shoulders" - A powerful image of being supported, held up, borne along by the Buddha himself.

**The Five Practices:**
The chapter emphasizes five ways of engaging with the sutra:
1. Receiving and upholding (受持)
2. Reading (讀)
3. Reciting (誦)
4. Explaining (解說)
5. Copying (書寫)

I preserved all five explicitly because each one matters. This isn't just about passive reception—it's about active engagement, transmission, embodiment.

**Voice and Tone:**
- Direct address throughout: "You need to know," "Listen now," "You hear me?"
- Repetition for emphasis: "Far, far heavier," "Every last one," "Countless"
- The Buddha speaks with authority but also intimacy—he's teaching, but he's also pleading with people to honor those who carry the word
- Used "ain't" and "gonna" sparingly but strategically for rhythm and emphasis

**The Warning Section:**
The sharp contrast between cursing the Buddha (lighter sin) and badmouthing a teacher of the sutra (heavier sin) is shocking—and meant to be. I kept that shock. This teaching says: The living carriers of wisdom are more vulnerable and thus deserve more protection than the Buddha himself. Don't you dare speak against the preachers, the teachers, the word-carriers.

**Key Theological Points Preserved:**

1. **Universal accessibility**: "Even one verse, even one moment of joy" = enlightenment is promised to all
2. **Teachers are already enlightened**: They chose to be born here out of compassion
3. **The sutra contains Buddha's whole body**: No relics needed where this teaching is
4. **Buddha's ongoing protection**: He sends magical audiences, appears to help teachers who forget lines
5. **Rarity and preciousness**: This teaching is harder to find than water in the desert
6. **The three garments formula**: Compassion (room) + Patience (robe) + Emptiness (seat) = how to teach

**Structural Choices:**
- Maintained prose/verse alternation
- Verses are singable, with rhythm and internal rhyme where natural
- Used rhetorical questions for engagement
- Employed call-and-response patterns in places ("You hear me?" "That's why.")

**The Chapter's Heart:**
This is ultimately about the sanctity of transmission, the holiness of teaching, the honor due to those who carry truth forward. In blues/gospel tradition, the preacher, the singer, the testifier, the one who stands up and speaks the word—these people are sacred. They deserve protection, respect, offerings, gratitude. This chapter says the same thing in Buddhist terms.

The water-seeker parable is the perfect center: We're all thirsty. We're all digging. The teacher is the one who's hit mud, who knows the water's close, who can tell you to keep digging right there. Honor them. Listen to them. They're showing you where to find what you're desperate for.

# CHAPTER ELEVEN: WHEN THE JEWELED TOWER ROSE UP

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
(Seeing the Jeweled Stupa)

Now at that time, right there in front of the Buddha, a tower made of seven kinds of precious jewels comes rising up out of the ground. A tower! Five hundred yojanas tall—that's so high you can't even see the top—and two hundred and fifty yojanas wide. Coming up from under the earth, rising, rising, until it's hanging there in the middle of the air, just floating in space.

And this tower, it's decorated with every kind of precious thing you can imagine. It's got five thousand railings and balconies, ten thousand chambers and rooms, countless banners and flags hanging from it for adornment. Jeweled necklaces dripping down from it. Ten thousand billion precious bells hanging and ringing. And from all four sides of this tower comes the fragrance of tamāla and sandalwood incense, filling up the whole world with that sweet smell.

All the banners and canopies on this tower are made of gold, silver, lapis lazuli, mother-of-pearl, agate, real pearls, rose-jade—seven precious materials all combined together. And this tower, it rises up so high it reaches all the way to the palaces of the Four Heavenly Kings.

And the thirty-three gods in the Heaven of the Thirty-Three, they start raining down mandārava flowers as offerings to the jeweled tower. And all the other heavenly beings, the dragons, yakṣas, gandharvas, asuras, garuḍas, kinnaras, mahoragas, humans and non-humans—thousands, ten thousands, billions of them—they all make offerings to this tower with every kind of flower, incense, necklaces, banners, canopies, and music, honoring it, respecting it, singing its praises.

Right then and there, from inside that jeweled tower, a great voice comes booming out, shouting in praise:

"Excellent! Excellent! Śākyamuni, World-Honored One! You're able to use your equal great wisdom to teach the Dharma for bodhisattvas, the Dharma that all Buddhas protect and keep in mind—this Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra—teaching it to this great assembly. Yes! Yes! Śākyamuni, World-Honored One! Everything you're saying is TRUE. Every word is REAL."

At that time the four assemblies—they see this great jeweled tower hanging there in the air, and they hear that voice coming from inside the tower, and they all receive Dharma-joy, happiness rising up in them. They can't believe what they're seeing—never seen anything like this before in their lives. So they all stand up from their seats, respectfully bring their palms together, and step back to one side.

Now at that time there's a bodhisattva mahāsattva named Great Joy in Preaching. He knows what's in the hearts of all the world's heavenly beings, humans, asuras, and the rest—knows they're all wondering, all questioning. So he speaks to the Buddha and says:

"World-Honored One, what's the reason for this? What's the cause? Why did this jeweled tower rise up from the ground? And who's that voice speaking from inside it?"

Right then the Buddha tells Great Joy in Preaching Bodhisattva:

"Inside this jeweled tower there's the complete body of a tathāgata. Way back in the past, in the eastern direction, beyond countless thousands, ten thousands, billions of incalculable worlds, there's a land called Jewel-Purity. In that land there was a buddha named Prabhūtaratna—Many Treasures.

"When that buddha was walking the bodhisattva path, he made a great vow, a great promise. He said: 'If after I become a buddha and pass into extinction, in any of the lands in the ten directions where somebody is teaching the Dharma Flower Sutra, my stupa—my tower, my monument—will rise up and appear right there in front of them to bear witness to the teaching, to testify that it's true, to shout out "Excellent!"'

"After that buddha achieved the Way, when he was about to enter extinction, he spoke to the great assembly of heavenly and human beings, told all the monks: 'After I pass away, if anybody wants to make offerings to my complete body, they should build one great stupa.'

"And through that buddha's supernatural power and the power of his vow, throughout all the worlds in the ten directions, wherever, in whatever place, someone is teaching the Dharma Flower Sutra—that jeweled tower of his rises up right there in front of them, with his complete body inside it, and it calls out in praise: 'Excellent! Excellent!'

"Great Joy in Preaching, right now Prabhūtaratna Tathāgata's tower heard that the Dharma Flower Sutra was being taught, and so it rose up from the ground and is calling out in praise: 'Excellent! Excellent!'"

At that time Great Joy in Preaching Bodhisattva, through the Buddha's supernatural power, speaks to the Buddha and says:

"World-Honored One, we all wish we could see that buddha's body."

The Buddha tells Great Joy in Preaching Bodhisattva Mahāsattva:

"This Prabhūtaratna Buddha has a deep and weighty vow. He said: 'When my jeweled tower rises up before the buddhas to hear the Dharma Flower Sutra being taught, if somebody wants me to show my body to the four assemblies, then first all that buddha's emanation bodies—all his manifestations teaching the Dharma in the ten directions throughout all the worlds—those must all gather together and return to one place. Only then will my body appear.'

"Great Joy in Preaching, all my emanation bodies—all my manifestations throughout the worlds in the ten directions teaching the Dharma—they need to gather together now."

Great Joy in Preaching says to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, we also want to see your emanation bodies, all those World-Honored Ones. We want to bow to them and make offerings to them."

Right then the Buddha sends out a ray of white light from between his eyebrows, and immediately everyone sees the lands of the eastern direction—five hundred ten thousand billion nayutas of lands, as many as the sands of the Ganges River. And all those lands have lapis lazuli for their ground, adorned with jeweled trees and jeweled clothing. Countless thousands, ten thousands, billions of bodhisattvas fill up those lands. Jeweled canopies are spread everywhere, jeweled nets are draped above.

And the buddhas in all those lands are teaching the Dharma with great wonderful voices. And everyone sees countless thousands, ten thousands, billions of bodhisattvas filling those lands, teaching the Dharma to the multitudes.

The southern direction, the western direction, the northern direction, the four intermediate directions, the zenith and the nadir—wherever that white light from between the Buddha's eyebrows shines, it's the same in all those places.

At that time the buddhas in all ten directions each speak to the assemblies of bodhisattvas:

"Good sons, now we must go to the Sahā world, to where Śākyamuni Buddha is, and make offerings to Prabhūtaratna Tathāgata's jeweled tower."

Right then the Sahā world transforms and becomes pure. The ground is made of lapis lazuli, adorned with jeweled trees. Golden ropes mark out eight roads. There are no more villages, towns, cities. No more oceans, rivers, streams, or mountains, forests, thickets. Great precious incense is burning. Mandārava flowers cover the ground everywhere. Jeweled nets and canopies are spread over everything. Jeweled bells are hanging.

Only this assembly is left here. All the other heavenly and human beings are moved to other lands.

At that time all the buddhas, each one bringing with them one great bodhisattva as an attendant, come to the Sahā world and arrive at the base of jeweled trees.

Every one of those jeweled trees is five hundred yojanas tall, with branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits adorning them in perfect order. Under each jeweled tree there's a lion throne, five yojanas high, also decorated with great precious materials.

At that time all those buddhas each sit down cross-legged on one of those lion seats.

And they keep arriving, one after another, filling up the entire three-thousand great thousand-world system. And still all the emanation bodies of Śākyamuni Buddha from just one direction—just the eastern direction—haven't all arrived yet. There's not enough room.

So Śākyamuni Buddha, wanting to make room for all his emanation bodies to sit down, transforms another two hundred ten thousand billion nayuta lands in each of the eight directions. He makes them all pure—no hells, no hungry ghosts, no animals, no asuras. And he moves all the heavenly and human beings to other lands.

These newly transformed lands also have lapis lazuli for their ground, adorned with jeweled trees. The trees are five hundred yojanas tall, with branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits perfectly adorned in order. Under each tree there's a jeweled lion throne, five yojanas high, adorned with various precious materials.

And there are no more oceans, rivers, or mountains—no Mount Mucilinda, no Great Mount Mucilinda, no Iron Ring Mountains, no Great Iron Ring Mountains, no Mount Sumeru, none of those mountain kings. Everything merges into one buddha-land. The jeweled ground is level and flat. Jeweled canopies cover everything. Banners and flags are hanging. Precious incense is burning. Heavenly jeweled flowers cover the ground.

Still not enough room!

So Śākyamuni Buddha transforms another two hundred ten thousand billion nayuta lands in each of the eight directions, all made pure—no hells, no hungry ghosts, no animals, no asuras. All the heavenly and human beings are moved to other lands.

These newly transformed lands also have lapis lazuli for their ground, adorned with jeweled trees five hundred yojanas tall, with branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits perfectly adorned. Under each tree there's a jeweled lion throne, five yojanas high, also decorated with great precious materials.

No oceans, no rivers, no mountains—no Mount Mucilinda, no Great Mount Mucilinda, no Iron Ring Mountains, no Great Iron Ring Mountains, no Mount Sumeru, none of those. Everything merges into one buddha-land. The jeweled ground is level and flat. Jeweled canopies cover everything. Banners and flags are hanging. Precious incense is burning. Heavenly jeweled flowers cover the ground everywhere.

At that time the emanation bodies of Śākyamuni Buddha from the eastern direction—from hundreds of thousands of tens of thousands of billions of nayutas of lands as numerous as Ganges sands—all those buddhas arrive one after another and gather together here.

And like this, gradually, the buddhas from all ten directions all arrive and gather, sitting in the eight directions.

At that time, in each of the eight directions, there are four hundred ten thousand billion nayuta lands filled completely with buddhas, tathāgatas.

At that time all those buddhas are each sitting under a jeweled tree on a lion throne, and they each send their attendants to go inquire after Śākyamuni Buddha. They each give the attendant a handful of jeweled flowers and tell them:

"Good son, you go to Gṛdhrakūṭa Mountain, to where Śākyamuni Buddha is, and speak to him in our name, saying: 'Are you well? Are you free from illness and trouble? Are your strength and energy comfortable? Are the bodhisattvas and voice-hearers all safe and peaceful?' Then scatter these jeweled flowers before the Buddha as an offering, and say: 'That certain buddha wishes to have this jeweled tower opened.'"

All the buddhas send their messengers like this.

At that time Śākyamuni Buddha sees all his emanation bodies—they've all arrived and gathered. Each one is sitting on a lion throne. And he hears all those buddhas saying they want the jeweled tower opened together.

So right then Śākyamuni Buddha rises up from his seat and stands there in the air, floating in space.

All the four assemblies stand up too, bringing their palms together, single-mindedly watching the Buddha.

Then Śākyamuni Buddha uses his right finger to open the door of the seven-jeweled tower. It makes a great sound—a loud sound—like pulling back the bolt and opening the gate of a great city.

Immediately, right then, everyone in that whole assembly sees Prabhūtaratna Tathāgata sitting on a lion throne inside the jeweled tower, his body complete, not scattered, not decayed, like he's in deep meditation.

And they hear him say:

"Excellent! Excellent! Śākyamuni Buddha, you've spoken this Dharma Flower Sutra joyfully, gladly. I came here to listen to this sutra. That's why I'm here."

At that time the four assemblies—seeing this buddha who passed into extinction countless thousands, ten thousands, billions of eons ago, hearing him speak these words—they're amazed beyond measure, never seen anything like it. They scatter heaps of heavenly jeweled flowers on Prabhūtaratna Buddha and Śākyamuni Buddha.

Right then Prabhūtaratna Buddha, inside that jeweled tower, moves over and makes half his seat available. Then he speaks to Śākyamuni Buddha, saying:

"Śākyamuni Buddha, come sit here."

Immediately Śākyamuni Buddha enters that tower and sits down on half the seat, sitting cross-legged.

At that time the great assembly sees two tathāgatas inside the seven-jeweled tower sitting together on the lion throne in full lotus position.

And everyone in the assembly thinks to themselves: "The buddhas are sitting way up high, too far away. We wish the Thus-Come One would use his supernatural power to let all of us be in space too."

Right then Śākyamuni Buddha, using his supernatural power, lifts the whole great assembly up into the air.

Then, with a great voice, a mighty voice, Śākyamuni Buddha speaks to all the four assemblies:

"WHO can teach this Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra far and wide in this Sahā world? Now is the time! Now is the moment!

"The Thus-Come One is about to enter nirvāṇa soon. The Buddha wants to entrust this Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra to someone, to put it in someone's hands."

At that time the World-Honored One, wanting to state this meaning again, wanting it to sink down deep into everyone's hearts, sings it out in verse:

That holy lord, that world-honored one,
Though he passed away so long ago,
He's sitting in this jeweled tower now,
He came here for the Dharma, you know.

How can you people not be diligent,
Not work hard for the sake of the Dharma too?
This buddha's been extinct for countless eons,
He goes place to place to hear it taught—that's what he'll do.

That buddha made a vow when he was alive:
"After I'm gone from this world here,
Wherever I go, wherever I travel,
I'm always going to hear this Dharma, year after year."

And all my emanation bodies—
Countless buddhas numerous as Ganges sands—
They came here to hear the Dharma taught,
They came to see Many Treasures, left their own lands.

They each abandoned their wonderful pure lands,
Left behind their disciples and crowds,
Left the heavenly dragons, spirits, and gods,
All the offerings, all the vows.

They came here to make the Dharma last long,
That's why they're here now, you see.
And to seat all these buddhas properly,
I used supernatural power to move countless beings,
Made this whole land pure and clean.

Each buddha went to a jeweled tree,
Like lotuses adorning a clear pure pond.
Under each jeweled tree there's a lion throne,
With the buddhas sitting on them, bright and strong.

Sitting there, they shine with radiant light,
Adorning everything all around,
Like in the dark night when you light a great torch—
That's how bright these buddhas look, how they sound.

Their bodies give off wonderful fragrance,
Filling up the lands in all ten directions,
Living beings smell that perfume and rejoice,
Can't control their joy—it's beyond their conceptions.

It's like a great wind shaking little tree branches—
That's how I use this loving device
To make the Dharma last forever,
To make it endure, to make it suffice.

I'm telling all of you in this great assembly:
After I'm gone from this world here,
Who can protect, uphold, read, and teach this sutra?
Come stand before the Buddha now, let me hear!

That Prabhūtaratna Buddha,
Though he passed away so long ago,
Made a great vow and gave a lion's roar—
He came here to testify, to let you know.

Many Treasures Thus-Come One
And me myself, my very own body,
And all these gathered emanation buddhas—
You need to understand this intention, you need to see.

All you children of the Buddha,
Who among you can protect this Dharma-teaching?
You should make a great vow right now,
Make it last forever—that's what I'm beseeching.

Whoever can protect this sutra,
This Dharma, this teaching, this precious thing,
They're making offerings to me
And to Many Treasures—that's what it will bring.

This Prabhūtaratna Buddha
Lives in his jeweled tower you see,
Always traveling throughout the ten directions
For the sake of this sutra—he comes to hear me.

And they'll also be making offerings
To all these emanation buddhas who've come here,
Who adorn and illuminate all these worlds—
If you teach this sutra, you honor them, that's clear.

If you can teach this sutra,
Then you'll see me, you understand?
You'll see Many Treasures Thus-Come One
And all the emanation buddhas throughout the land.

All you good sons, all you good daughters,
Each one of you needs to think carefully about this—
This is a difficult matter, a hard thing,
You should make a great vow—don't miss!

All the other sutras and scriptures—
There's as many as sands in the Ganges, it's true,
Even if you taught all of those,
That wouldn't be difficult to do.

If you grabbed hold of Mount Sumeru
And threw it to another buddha-land,
Countless lands away, far far away—
That also wouldn't be too hard to manage or withstand.

If you used your toe to kick
This whole great thousand-world realm
And sent it flying to another land—
That wouldn't be too hard at the helm.

If you stood at the peak of existence,
The highest point of all there is,
And taught countless other sutras to the crowds—
That wouldn't be too difficult, that's an easy quiz.

But after the Buddha's gone, if you're in an evil age,
An age of hatred and trouble and strife,
And you can teach this sutra then—
NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT. That's the test of your life.

Suppose someone could grab empty space in their hand
And walk around carrying it here and there—
That wouldn't be too difficult either,
That'd be easier to accomplish, I declare.

But after I'm gone,
If you can copy this sutra out by hand,
Or get someone else to copy it down—
NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT to command.

Suppose you took this whole great earth,
Placed it on your toenail, just sitting there light,
And then climbed up to the Brahmā heaven,
Balancing it the whole way—that wouldn't be too tight.

But after the Buddha's passed away, in that evil world,
If you can read this sutra even for a moment's time—
NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT to pull off,
That's the hardest mountain to climb.

Suppose when the eon's burning up,
When cosmic fire's destroying everything around,
You load up dry grass on your back
And walk through those flames without burning down—

That wouldn't be too difficult, no.

But after I'm gone,
If you can uphold this sutra here
And teach it to even one person, just one alone—
NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT, crystal clear.

Suppose you could uphold and carry
Eighty-four thousand teachings in your mind,
All the twelve divisions of the whole Dharma treasury,
And taught them to people, making them refined—

Made all the listeners gain six supernatural powers,
Gave them all these benefits, these precious gifts—
Even if you could do all that,
That wouldn't be too difficult, those aren't the hardest lifts.

But after I'm gone from this world,
If you can hear this sutra, listen and receive,
And ask about its meaning, inquire into it deeply—
NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT to achieve.

If someone teaches the Dharma
And causes thousands, ten thousands, billions—
Countless, measureless, numerous as Ganges sands—
To become arhats with six powers by the millions—

Even if you could bring that benefit,
Even if you could do all that,
That still wouldn't be too difficult,
That's not where the hardest test is at.

But after I'm gone from here,
If you can reverently uphold
A sutra like this one—
NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT, hard as gold.

For the Buddha-way, for the sake of awakening,
Throughout countless lands from beginning until now,
I've widely taught many sutras,
But among all of them, this one stands out somehow.

This sutra is number one.
This one is foremost, supreme.

If you can uphold this teaching,
Then you're upholding the Buddha's body, the Buddha's dream.

All you good sons, all you good daughters,
After I'm gone from this world here,
Who among you can receive and uphold,
Can read and recite this sutra without fear?

Come stand before the Buddha now,
Speak your vow out loud, make it known!

This sutra is difficult to uphold, to hold onto,
But if someone can uphold it even briefly, for just a breath—
I'll be filled with joy,
And all the buddhas will rejoice at that, beyond death.

A person like this—
All the buddhas praise them, every one.
This is what it means to be brave,
This is what it means to be someone who gets it done.

This is what it means to keep the precepts,
To practice the ascetic way without deviation—
This means you'll quickly gain
That supreme Buddha-Way, that highest station.

In the world to come,
If you can read and uphold this sutra here,
This is a true child of Buddha,
Living on pure and good ground, crystal clear.

After the Buddha's passed into extinction,
If you can understand this sutra's meaning deep,
You become the eyes of the world,
The eyes of all the heavenly and human beings—they'll weep!

In that frightening age,
That age of terror and fear,
If you can teach this sutra even for a moment,
Even just briefly, just right here—

All the heavenly beings and human beings
Should make offerings to you,
Should bow down and honor you—
You're carrying what's precious and true.

### END OF CHAPTER ELEVEN: WHEN THE JEWELED TOWER ROSE UP

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

This chapter is pure cosmic theater—supernatural testimony, infinite gathering, and prophetic challenge rolled into one spectacular vision. In the blues/gospel tradition, this is revival meeting energy, the testimony of the long-dead rising up to speak truth, the gathering of witnesses from every direction, and then the call: "Who's gonna carry this word forward when I'm gone?" This interpretation tries to capture that drama, that urgency, that cosmic scale.

**Key Interpretive Choices:**

**The Title:** "When the Jeweled Tower Rose Up" captures the central dramatic moment—something impossible happening, the ancient testifying to the present.

**Central Images:**

**The Rising Tower** - I kept this concrete and visual: rising from the ground, hanging in the air, covered with jewels, bells ringing, incense pouring out. This is spectacular, impossible, overwhelming. The blues tradition knows visions like this—Ezekiel's wheel, Jacob's ladder, John's Revelation. Supernatural things breaking into the ordinary world to bear witness.

**The Voice from Inside** - A dead Buddha speaking from inside his own monument: "What you're teaching is TRUE!" This is testimony from beyond, validation from the other side. The blues/gospel tradition is full of this—the cloud of witnesses, the communion of saints, the dead rising up to testify.

**The Gathering of Infinite Buddhas** - This is the most spectacular gathering scene in the whole sutra. Buddhas from every direction, so many they fill multiple transformed worlds, all sitting under jeweled trees like lotuses in a pond. This is the heavenly congregation, the assembly of all the faithful across time and space. I tried to capture the scale, the repetition, the overwhelming number.

**Two Buddhas Sitting Together** - This is the theological and visual climax. Prabhūtaratna moves over on his throne: "Come sit here." And Śākyamuni enters the floating tower and sits beside him. This is past and present together, testimony and teaching united, witness and prophet side by side. I kept this moment simple and direct because it's already perfect.

**The Six Difficulties** - This is prophetic challenge in full force. The Buddha lists impossible things—moving Mount Sumeru, kicking the universe to another cosmos, walking through cosmic fire—and says ALL OF THOSE are easier than teaching this sutra after he's gone in an evil age. I preserved the call-and-response structure: "That wouldn't be too difficult... BUT THIS—this is difficult!" The repetition builds, the stakes rise, the challenge gets clearer.

**Voice and Structure:**

**Direct Narration** - I used present tense for the opening: "a tower... comes rising up out of the ground." This makes it immediate, happening right now.

**Repetition for Scale** - The text repeats the transformation of the worlds three times. I preserved this because repetition communicates scale. One transformation isn't enough. Two isn't enough. The third time you finally understand: There are SO MANY Buddhas that the entire cosmos has to be remade multiple times just to seat them.

**The Challenge Section** - "WHO can teach this?" I used all caps because this is a shout, a call, a challenge thrown out to the whole assembly. This is "Who will go for us?" This is "Here am I, send me!" This is the moment when the prophet asks for volunteers.

**The Verses** - I structured these with rhythm and internal rhyme where natural, but didn't force it. The verses are singable but not jingly. The "That wouldn't be too difficult... NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT" refrain builds power through repetition.

**Key Theological Points Preserved:**

1. **Testimony and Witness**: Prabhūtaratna's whole purpose is to testify that the teaching is true. He made a vow to show up wherever this sutra is taught just to say "This is real!" The blues/gospel tradition is built on testimony—standing up and saying "I was there, I saw it, I'm here to tell you it's true."

2. **The Vow that Transcends Death**: Prabhūtaratna died countless eons ago, but his vow is still alive. He still shows up. This is the power of commitment that outlasts your own life. Anyone who's ever carried forward someone else's dream, someone else's teaching, someone else's word—they know this.

3. **The Unity of All Buddhas**: The emanation bodies aren't separate beings—they're all Śākyamuni teaching in different places. But they're also real enough to gather, to sit on thrones, to send messengers. This is mystical unity and multiplicity at once. I didn't try to explain it—just presented it.

4. **The Prophetic Challenge**: "Who will carry this forward after I'm gone?" This is Moses asking who will lead the people into the promised land. This is Jesus saying "Go into all the world." This is Martin Luther King saying "I may not get there with you, but we as a people will get to the promised land." Someone has to carry it forward.

5. **The Measurement of Difficulty**: The six difficulties aren't hyperbole—they're theological statement. Teaching truth in an age of lies, carrying light in an age of darkness, speaking love in an age of hatred—these ARE harder than any physical impossibility. Anyone who's tried knows.

6. **"This sutra is the Buddha's body"** - This is not metaphor. The teaching IS the teacher. If you carry this sutra, you're carrying Buddha himself. The blues/gospel tradition knows this too: The song carries the singer. The word carries the one who spoke it. Truth outlasts the truth-teller.

**Stylistic Choices:**

- **"Countless, ten thousands, billions"** - I kept piling up the numbers because that's what the text does. This isn't vague—it's trying to express a number beyond counting by listing every word for "many" all at once.

- **"No more oceans, no more rivers, no more mountains"** - The transformed world has none of the rough, dangerous, dividing features of ordinary reality. It's all one level jeweled ground. I kept the repetition of "no more, no more, no more" because it's liturgical, hypnotic, transformative.

- **"NOW THAT'S DIFFICULT"** - This phrase becomes a refrain, a drumbeat, a hammer coming down. By the seventh time you hear it, you feel the weight of what's being asked.

- **Present tense and direct speech** - "He's sitting in this jeweled tower now, / He came here for the Dharma, you know." This makes it immediate, personal, right here right now.

**The Chapter's Heart:**

This chapter is about the weight of transmission. It's about understanding that carrying truth forward through dark times is the hardest thing there is. It's about the gathering of all the witnesses—across time, across space—to say "This matters. This is real. Someone needs to carry this."

In the blues tradition, there's always this moment. The elder musician is dying. Who's gonna carry the song? The preacher is getting old. Who's gonna carry the word? The movement leader is going to prison, going into exile, going to die. Who's gonna carry the work forward?

This chapter says: That moment is here. The assembly of witnesses is gathered. Past and present are sitting together on one throne. And the question is thrown out into the air: "Who can do this? Who's brave enough? Who loves the truth enough to carry it through fire?"

The answer isn't given in this chapter. That comes later. But the question is asked with the full weight of cosmic drama behind it, with infinite Buddhas watching, with the ancient and the present both testifying: This teaching is true. It's real. It's precious. Somebody needs to carry it.

And that's the blues: The old ones testify. The community gathers. The challenge is issued. And then we wait to see who stands up and says "I'll do it. I'll carry it forward. No matter what it costs."

# CHAPTER TWELVE: WHEN YOUR ENEMY WAS YOUR TEACHER

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
(Devadatta)

Right then the Buddha speaks to all the bodhisattvas and the four assemblies—all the heavenly and human beings gathered there. He says:

"Listen now. Way back in the past, further back than you can count, beyond measureless kalpas, I was searching for this Lotus Sutra teaching. Searching and searching, and I never got tired, never gave up, never said 'I can't do this no more.'

"For many kalpas—age after age—I was born as a king. A king! Had everything a man could want. But I made a vow, made a promise in my heart: I'm gonna seek the supreme enlightenment, that highest awakening. And my heart never turned back, never said 'Maybe tomorrow,' never said 'This is good enough.'

"I wanted to fulfill the six perfections—generosity, discipline, patience, effort, meditation, wisdom—all six of them complete. So I practiced giving, practiced it hard. Gave away everything. Didn't hold back nothing. Elephants—gave them away. Horses—gave them away. The seven precious treasures—gave them away. My kingdom—gave it away. My cities—gave them away. My wife, my children—gave them away. My servants, my attendants—gave them away. Even gave away my head, my eyes, my marrow and my brain, my flesh, my hands, my feet. Didn't care about my body, didn't care about my life. Just gave.

"At that time, people lived long lives, lived for measureless years. But for the sake of the Dharma—for the sake of the truth—I gave up my throne, turned over the government to my son, and I beat a drum and sent out a proclamation to the four directions, calling out: 'Who can teach me the great vehicle? Who's got the real teaching? Whoever can speak the great vehicle for me—the Mahayana—I'll serve you my whole life. I'll be your servant. I'll work for you. I'll run when you say run.'

"And then this ascetic came. This hermit. He came to the palace and said to the king: 'I've got the great vehicle. I've got a teaching called the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra. But you got to listen to me. If you don't go against me, if you do what I say, I'll teach it to you.'

"When the king heard what the ascetic said, he was so happy he jumped for joy! Jumped up! Said 'Yes!' Right away he followed that ascetic, attended to him, gave him everything he needed. I went and gathered fruit for him. I drew water from the well for him. I chopped firewood for him. I prepared his food for him. I even used my own body as his bed, as his seat, so he could sit and rest. Laid down flat so he could sit on my back.

"And my body never got tired. My mind never got tired. I served him like that for a thousand years. One thousand years! All for the sake of the Dharma. Working hard, serving, making sure he never lacked for nothing."

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say this again, speaks in verse:

I remember the old days, long past time,
When I was seeking the great Dharma in my prime.
Though I was a king of the world, sitting on a throne,
I didn't want the five desires—didn't want what I owned.

I rang the bell, sent word to the four directions wide:
"Who's got the great Dharma? Come on, step inside.
Whoever can teach me, explain it to my face,
I'll be your servant, take the lowest place."

Then came an ascetic, old hermit man,
Came to the great king, said "I've got a plan.
I've got a subtle, wonderful teaching, rare to find,
The kind of Dharma that blows the worldly mind.

If you can practice it, if you can walk this road,
I'll teach it to you, lighten your load."
When the king heard the ascetic's word,
His heart filled up with joy—best thing he ever heard!

Right away he followed that hermit man,
Gave him everything, did all he can.
Gathered firewood, picked fruit from the vine,
Gave it to him respectfully, right on time.

My body kept the wonderful Dharma close at hand,
Body and mind never tired, never said "I can't stand."
I did it for all living beings, every single one,
Sought the great Dharma for everyone under the sun.

Didn't do it for myself or the five desires' pleasure,
Did it as the king of a great country, seeking treasure.
Sought and got this Dharma, didn't quit the course,
And that's why I became a buddha, found the source.
Now I'm telling you this story—here's the force.

The Buddha tells all the monks:

"That king back then—that was me. I was that king.

"And the ascetic, that hermit man—that was Devadatta. That's who he is now.

"Because Devadatta was a good spiritual friend to me, because he taught me, because he pushed me, I was able to perfect the six perfections. I got compassion perfected. I got loving-kindness perfected. I got sympathetic joy perfected. I got equanimity perfected. I got the thirty-two marks. I got the eighty minor characteristics. I got this purple-golden color. I got the ten powers, the four fearlessnesses, the four methods of attraction, the eighteen unique qualities. I got supernatural penetrations and the power of the Way.

"I became perfectly, completely enlightened. I teach beings far and wide.

"All because Devadatta was a good spiritual friend."

The Buddha tells the four assemblies:

"Devadatta—after incalculable kalpas have passed, he's gonna become a buddha. You hear me? He's gonna be called Heaven-King Tathāgata, Worthy of Offerings, Perfectly Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Devas and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.

"His world will be called Heaven-Way.

"When Heaven-King Buddha is dwelling in the world, he'll be there for twenty intermediate kalpas, teaching beings the wonderful Dharma. As many beings as there are grains of sand in the Ganges River—that many will attain arhat-fruit. Countless beings will awaken the mind of the pratyekabuddha, the solitary realizer. As many beings as grains of sand in the Ganges will awaken the supreme enlightenment mind, will attain the patience of non-arising, will reach non-regression.

"After Heaven-King Buddha enters nirvāṇa, the Correct Dharma will remain in the world for twenty intermediate kalpas.

"His whole-body relics will be in a stupa made of the seven precious treasures—a tower sixty yojanas high, forty yojanas wide on every side. All the devas and human beings will make offerings to it with mixed flowers, powdered incense, burning incense, perfume paste, clothing, necklaces, banners, flags, jeweled canopies, music, and singing. They'll bow down and honor that wonderful seven-jeweled tower.

"Countless beings will attain arhat-fruit. Countless beings will awaken to the pratyekabuddha way. Inconceivable numbers of beings will bring forth the enlightenment mind and reach non-regression."

The Buddha tells all the monks:

"In a future age, if there are good men or good women who hear this Devadatta Chapter of the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra, and they believe it with pure hearts, respect it, don't doubt it—they won't fall into hell, won't fall into the realm of hungry ghosts, won't fall into the animal realm.

"They'll be born right there in front of buddhas in the ten directions. Wherever they're born, they'll always be hearing this sutra.

"If they're born among humans or devas, they'll receive supremely wonderful happiness.

"If they're born in the presence of a buddha, they'll be born by transformation from a lotus flower."

Right then, Wisdom-Accumulation Bodhisattva—the one who came with Many Treasures World-Honored One from below—he says to Many Treasures Buddha:

"We should return to our homeland now."

But Śākyamuni Buddha tells Wisdom-Accumulation:

"Good son, wait a little while, just a little bit. There's a bodhisattva here named Mañjuśrī—Wonderful Virtue. You should meet him. You should talk with him about the wonderful Dharma. Then you can return to your homeland."

And right at that moment, Mañjuśrī is sitting on a thousand-petaled lotus flower, big as a chariot wheel. And the bodhisattvas who came with him are also sitting on jeweled lotus flowers. They rise up naturally from the great ocean, from the palace of Sāgara the Dragon King. They come up, suspended in empty space. They go to Vulture Peak Mountain. They step down from their lotus flowers and go to where the Buddha is. They bow their heads to the ground, honoring the feet of both World-Honored Ones—both buddhas sitting there. When they finish paying respects, they go to where Wisdom-Accumulation is sitting. They exchange greetings, ask how each other is doing. Then they sit down to one side.

Wisdom-Accumulation Bodhisattva asks Mañjuśrī:

"You went to the dragon palace. How many beings did you teach down there? How many did you convert?"

Mañjuśrī says:

"The number is immeasurable. Can't count them. Can't calculate them. Can't speak it with the mouth. Can't measure it with the mind. Just wait a little while. You'll have your proof. You'll see for yourself."

Before he even finishes speaking, countless bodhisattvas sitting on jeweled lotus flowers come rising up from the ocean. They come to Vulture Peak Mountain and stay there, suspended in empty space.

All these bodhisattvas were taught and transformed by Mañjuśrī. They've all accomplished the bodhisattva practices. They're all discussing the six perfections together.

The ones who used to be śrāvakas, hearers—they're up there in empty space, but they're teaching the śrāvaka practices. But now they're all practicing the emptiness teaching of the great vehicle.

Mañjuśrī says to Wisdom-Accumulation:

"The teaching and transforming I did in the ocean—it went like this. That's what happened."

Then Wisdom-Accumulation Bodhisattva speaks in verse to praise him:

You have great wisdom, virtue, courage, strength—
You've taught and transformed countless beings, great length.
Now this great assembly and I have all seen
The real characteristics expounded, what they mean.

You've revealed the one vehicle Dharma, opened it clear,
You've guided all living beings, brought them near.
You help them quickly achieve the enlightenment way—
That's what you've been doing, night and day.

Mañjuśrī says:

"When I was in the ocean, I only kept teaching the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra. Nothing else. Just this."

Wisdom-Accumulation asks Mañjuśrī:

"This sutra is so deep, so subtle, so wonderful. It's the treasure of all sutras. It's rare in the world. Is there any living being who practiced this sutra diligently, put real effort into it, and quickly became a buddha? Tell me—did that happen?"

Mañjuśrī says:

"There's the daughter of Sāgara the Dragon King. She just turned eight years old. Eight! But she's got sharp wisdom, keen intelligence. She understands all the roots and karmic actions of living beings. She's got dhāraṇī. She can receive and hold all the deep secret treasures taught by the buddhas—every word, she holds it.

"She's entered deep into meditation. She thoroughly understands all dharmas. In the space of an instant—one moment—she brought forth the enlightenment mind. She reached non-regression. Her eloquence has no obstruction, no barrier.

"She thinks of living beings with compassion, like they're her own children. Her merit and virtue are complete. When she thinks something in her mind, when she speaks it with her mouth, it's subtle, wonderful, vast, and great. She's compassionate, humane, yielding, gentle. Her will and spirit are harmonious and refined.

"She can reach enlightenment. She can arrive at bodhi."

Wisdom-Accumulation Bodhisattva says:

"I've seen Śākyamuni Tathāgata. For immeasurable kalpas he practiced difficult and bitter practices. He accumulated merit, piled up virtue, seeking the bodhisattva way. He never stopped, not even for a moment. I look at this great thousand-world system—every part of it, down to a space as small as a mustard seed—there's not one spot that's not a place where the Bodhisattva gave up his body and life for the sake of living beings. Only after all that did he achieve the enlightenment way.

"I don't believe this girl, in just a brief instant, could achieve perfect enlightenment. Can't believe it."

Before he even finishes speaking, the dragon king's daughter suddenly appears right there in front of them. She bows, touching her head to the ground in respect, then stands to one side. She speaks in verse to praise the Buddha:

You deeply understand the marks of sin and merit,
You illuminate the ten directions—all can hear it.
Your subtle, wonderful, pure dharma body, so sublime,
Has the thirty-two marks, perfect in every sign.

The eighty minor characteristics adorn the dharma body bright,
Honored by devas and humans, worshipped day and night.
Dragons and spirits all bow down with respect—
Every kind of living being honors you, every sect.

I've also heard you've achieved the enlightenment way,
Only a buddha can bear witness to this, I say.
I'm revealing the great vehicle teaching, opening it wide,
To rescue suffering beings on every side.

Right then, Śāriputra speaks to the dragon girl and says:

"You say you're about to quickly attain supreme enlightenment? But that's hard to believe. Can't be trusted. Why not?

"A woman's body is filthy, impure. It's not a vessel for the Dharma. How could you attain supreme enlightenment?

"The buddha-way is so vast, so far away! You have to practice through immeasurable kalpas, endure bitterness, pile up practices, perfect all the perfections. Only then do you succeed.

"Plus, a woman's body has five obstacles. What are they?

"First, she can't become a Brahma Heaven King.
"Second, she can't become Indra.
"Third, she can't become Mara, the demon king.
"Fourth, she can't become a wheel-turning sage king.
"Fifth, she can't become a buddha.

"So how could a woman's body quickly achieve buddhahood? Can't happen."

Right then the dragon girl had a precious jewel, worth as much as a great thousand-world system—worth three thousand great thousand worlds. She held it up and presented it to the Buddha.

The Buddha received it immediately.

The dragon girl said to Wisdom-Accumulation Bodhisattva and to the Venerable Śāriputra:

"I presented a precious jewel, and the World-Honored One accepted it. Was that quick or not?"

They answered: "Very quick!"

The girl said:

"Use your spiritual power. Watch me become a buddha. It's gonna be even faster than that."

Right at that moment, everyone in the assembly saw the dragon girl suddenly—in an instant—transform into a male. She perfected the bodhisattva practices. Immediately she went south to the Spotless World. She sat down on a jeweled lotus flower and achieved perfect, complete enlightenment. She had the thirty-two marks. She had the eighty minor characteristics. She taught the wonderful Dharma for all living beings throughout the ten directions.

At that time, in this Saha world—all the bodhisattvas, śrāvakas, the eight classes of devas and dragons, humans and non-humans—they all saw from far away that dragon girl becoming a buddha, teaching the Dharma to all the devas and humans gathered at that time.

Their hearts filled up with great joy. They all bowed in reverence from a distance.

Countless living beings heard the Dharma, understood it, awakened to it. They attained non-regression.

Countless living beings received prophecies of their future enlightenment.

The Spotless World shook in six ways.

Three thousand living beings in this Saha world reached the ground of non-regression. Three thousand living beings brought forth the enlightenment mind and received prophecies.

Wisdom-Accumulation Bodhisattva, the Venerable Śāriputra, and the whole assembly—they all silently believed and accepted this.

### END OF CHAPTER TWELVE: WHEN YOUR ENEMY WAS YOUR TEACHER

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

This is Chapter 12 of the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra, translated from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese (406 CE) into blues/gospel vernacular English.

This chapter carries one of the most revolutionary messages in the entire sutra: that enlightenment is immediate and available to all, regardless of social barriers. The Buddha reveals that Devadatta—his cousin who tried to kill him, who split the sangha, who is in hell for his crimes—was actually his greatest teacher. Through opposition and resistance, Devadatta pushed the Buddha toward complete awakening.

Then comes the dragon princess—an eight-year-old girl, non-human, doubly excluded by the "five obstacles" doctrine—who demonstrates instant enlightenment right there in front of everyone who said it was impossible.

Key metaphors in this interpretation:

**"Your Enemy Was Your Teacher"** - The chapter title captures the radical teaching that life's hardest trials and greatest opponents can be our deepest educators. This resonates with the blues tradition's understanding that suffering teaches, that the hard road makes you wise.

**The King Becoming a Servant** - I rendered the Buddha's past-life story of royal power surrendered for truth as a blues narrative of status reversed: "I was a king / Had everything a man could want." This mirrors the gospel theme of the last being first, the high brought low.

**"Beat a drum and sent out a proclamation"** - Kept the visceral action of the king literally calling out for teaching, like a town crier, like a preacher calling for testimony.

**"Laid down flat so he could sit on my back"** - Made concrete and physical what the Chinese describes: using one's own body as furniture for the sake of learning truth.

**"Watch how fast this is"** - The dragon princess's challenge becomes vernacular direct speech, the kind of thing someone would actually say before demonstrating impossible grace: "Use your spiritual power. Watch me become a buddha. It's gonna be even faster than that."

**Instant transformation** - The sudden shift from female to male to enlightened buddha happens "right at that moment," "in an instant," "suddenly"—language of the spontaneous grace moment central to gospel testimony, when everything changes in the blink of an eye.

The verse sections maintain rhythm and internal rhyme ("in my prime," "throne/owned," "wide/inside," "man/plan") to create oral memorability and musical flow, appropriate to the blues tradition's method of carrying wisdom.

This chapter is about barriers broken, impossibilities made possible, enemies revealed as friends, and the instantaneous nature of awakening—themes that resonate deeply with the African American spiritual tradition's understanding of liberation, reversal, and sudden grace.

# CHAPTER THIRTEEN: WHEN THE STORM COMES DOWN, YOU GOTTA KEEP STANDING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
勸持品第十三 (Exhortation to Maintain)

Blues/Gospel Interpretation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese (406 CE)

## THE FIRST VOW - MEDICINE KING AND GREAT JOY STEP FORWARD

Now at that time, Medicine King Bodhisattva and Great Joy in Preaching Bodhisattva—these two great souls—they stood up together with twenty thousand of their brother and sister bodhisattvas. They all gathered right there in front of the Buddha, and they made a promise, you hear me, they made a VOW:

"World-Honored One, don't you worry yourself none. Don't let your mind be troubled about what's coming. When you gone from this world, when you passed on to the other shore, WE gonna be here. We gonna hold this teaching. We gonna read it, recite it, explain it, and live it out.

"Now we know—Lord, we KNOW—that the times coming after you leave gonna be dark times, evil times. The people gonna have less and less good roots growing in their hearts. They gonna be full up with pride, puffed up like balloons thinking they somebody special. They gonna be hungry for profit, greedy for respect, chasing after offerings and honors. They gonna feed the wrong seeds in their souls, and they gonna be far, far away from freedom.

"Teaching them gonna be like trying to get water from a stone. It's gonna be HARD. But we making a promise right here, right now: We gonna raise up a GREAT PATIENCE inside ourselves. We gonna read this sutra, hold it, speak it, write it down, and honor it every way we can. And we ain't gonna hold back. We ain't gonna spare our bodies. We ain't gonna count the cost. Even if it take our very lives—we gonna carry this teaching forward."

## THE SECOND VOW - FIVE HUNDRED ARHATS STAND UP

Now right then, five hundred arhats in that assembly—these was the ones who already got their prophecy from Buddha, who already been told their names gonna shine—they spoke up to the Buddha, saying:

"World-Honored One, we making our own vow too. We gonna take this sutra to OTHER LANDS. We gonna go to foreign countries, strange territories, places far from here, and we gonna spread this teaching wide."

## THE THIRD VOW - EIGHT THOUSAND DISCIPLES JOIN IN

And then there was eight thousand more—some still learning, some done with learning, all of them just got their prophecy too. They rose up from their seats, put their palms together in respect, and they said to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, we gonna go to other lands too. We gonna spread this sutra far and wide in those places.

"And why we doing that? Why we going AWAY from here? Because THIS Sahā world, this endurance-land we standing in right now—Lord have mercy—it's FULL of sorry, trifling people. Full of folks with their chests puffed out, full of arrogance and shallow virtue. Full of anger and corruption, full of crooked hearts and lying tongues. Ain't no truth in them.

"So we taking this teaching somewhere else, where maybe folks got a chance to hear it clean."

## THE BUDDHA'S AUNT GETS HER PROPHECY

Now at that time, the Buddha's aunt—her name was Mahāprajāpatī, and she was a nun, a bhikṣuṇī—she stood up together with six thousand other nuns, some still learning and some done with learning. They all rose from their seats as one, pressed their palms together, and they looked up at the Blessed One's face. They looked at him with their whole hearts, couldn't take their eyes off him, not even for a second.

And the World-Honored One, he saw that look on her face and he said to her:

"Gautamī"—that was her family name, Gautamī—"why you looking at me with such worry in your eyes? Why you gazing at the Tathāgata like your heart about to break? You thinking maybe I ain't said your name? You thinking maybe I ain't given you your prophecy of supreme, perfect enlightenment—anuttara-samyak-sambodhi?"

And then Buddha said:

"Gautamī, listen here. When I spoke before about ALL the voice-hearers getting their prophecy, you was INCLUDED in that, you hear me? But now, if you want to know the particulars, if you want to know YOUR specific name and YOUR specific promise, then listen:

"In the world that's coming, you gonna be a GREAT DHARMA TEACHER in the Dharma of sixty-eight thousand billion Buddhas. Count them—SIXTY-EIGHT THOUSAND BILLION! And these six thousand nuns with you, still learning and done with learning, they gonna be Dharma teachers right alongside you.

"And you gonna walk that Bodhisattva path step by step, gradually perfecting yourself, until you BECOME a Buddha yourself. And your name gonna be: ALL-BEINGS-REJOICE-TO-SEE TATHĀGATA, ARHAT, PERFECTLY ENLIGHTENED ONE, PERFECT IN KNOWLEDGE AND CONDUCT, WELL-GONE, KNOWER OF THE WORLD, UNSURPASSED ONE, TAMER OF BEINGS, TEACHER OF DEVAS AND HUMANS, BUDDHA, WORLD-HONORED ONE.

"And Gautamī, when you become that Buddha—All-Beings-Rejoice-to-See Buddha—you and these six thousand bodhisattvas with you, y'all gonna take turns giving prophecies to each other, passing on the promise of supreme, perfect enlightenment from one to the next like a torch that never goes out."

## RĀHULA'S MOTHER GETS HER PROPHECY

Now at that time, Rāhula's mother—her name was Yaśodharā, and she was a nun too—she had a thought in her mind. She thought:

"The World-Honored One, when he giving out all these prophecies, he ain't said MY name. He ain't called ME out specifically."

And the Buddha, he knew her heart. He told Yaśodharā:

"In the world that's coming, in a hundred thousand million billion Buddha-Dharmas, you gonna walk the Bodhisattva path. You gonna be a GREAT DHARMA TEACHER. And gradually, step by step, you gonna complete the Buddha-way.

"And in a land called Excellent, a GOOD country, you gonna become a Buddha. Your name gonna be: POSSESSING-MYRIAD-LIGHT-MARKS TATHĀGATA, ARHAT, PERFECTLY ENLIGHTENED ONE, PERFECT IN KNOWLEDGE AND CONDUCT, WELL-GONE, KNOWER OF THE WORLD, UNSURPASSED ONE, TAMER OF BEINGS, TEACHER OF DEVAS AND HUMANS, BUDDHA, WORLD-HONORED ONE.

"And that Buddha's lifespan? It gonna be beyond measure, countless as the grains of sand in the Ganges, lasting through innumerable, incalculable kalpas."

## THE NUNS SING THEIR JOY

At that time, Mahāprajāpatī the nun and Yaśodharā the nun, along with all their companions, they were filled up with joy like they never known before. Their hearts was OVERFLOWING. And right there in front of the Buddha, they sang out in verse:

 World-Honored One, you our guide and teacher,
 You bring peace to all the gods and humans,
 Now we done HEARD our prophecy,
 And our hearts is FULL, our hearts is SATISFIED,
 Our hearts is resting easy, got everything we need.

And when the nuns finished singing that verse, they said to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, we ready too. We gonna go to other lands, foreign countries, and we gonna spread this sutra far and wide."

## THE GREAT VOW - EIGHT HUNDRED MILLION BODHISATTVAS MAKE THE ULTIMATE PROMISE

Now at that time, the World-Honored One, he looked out over the assembly. He looked at eight hundred million nayutas of bodhisattva-mahāsattvas—count them, EIGHT HUNDRED MILLION NAYUTAS of great beings!

These wasn't no beginners. These was bodhisattvas who CANNOT FALL BACK—avinivartanīya—they done passed the point of no return. They turn the wheel of no-retreating Dharma. They got all the dhāraṇīs, all the mystical formulas and powers.

These great souls, they rose up from their seats together and went before the Buddha. They pressed their palms together with one mind, one heart, and they thought to themselves:

"If the World-Honored One tell us to uphold and teach this sutra, then we gonna follow his instruction. We gonna spread this Dharma wide like he say."

But then they thought again:

"But the Buddha, he sitting there SILENT. He ain't saying nothing. He ain't giving us no command. So what we supposed to do?"

Now these bodhisattvas, they understood the Buddha's mind. They knew his heart. They wanted to RESPECT his intention, and they also wanted to FULFILL their own original vows, the promises they made long, long ago.

So right there in front of the Buddha, they let out a LION'S ROAR—that's what they call it when you speak with power and authority and ain't scared of nothing—and they made this vow. They said:

"World-Honored One, after you gone—after the Tathāgata done passed into final nirvāṇa—we gonna travel back and forth through the ten directions, all the worlds everywhere. We gonna make it so beings can WRITE this sutra down, HOLD it in their hearts, READ it with their mouths, RECITE it from memory, UNDERSTAND what it means, and PRACTICE it the right way, keeping it in their minds with right remembrance.

"All of this gonna happen because of the BUDDHA'S POWER, the Buddha's authority and grace. So we asking you, World-Honored One: When you in other lands far away from here, WATCH OVER US from a distance. Guard us and protect us while we doing this work."

And then all these bodhisattvas together, with ONE VOICE, they broke out into verse. They sang out their vow, and this is what they sang:

## THE GREAT VOW IN VERSE

 World-Honored One, don't you worry,
 Don't let your mind be troubled none,
 After you done passed away,
 In that terrifying, evil age,
 We gonna spread this teaching WIDE.

 There gonna be ignorant folks,
 People with no wisdom in they heads,
 They gonna curse us out with evil mouths,
 They gonna revile us and mock us,
 Some of them gonna come at us with sticks and stones,
 Beat us and try to break us down—
 But we gonna ENDURE it all.
 We gonna bear it with patience.

 In that evil age, some of the monks—
 Supposed to be holy men, supposed to be our brothers—
 They gonna have TWISTED wisdom, deceitful hearts,
 Crooked minds and lying spirits.
 They gonna CLAIM they got what they ain't got,
 Say they achieved enlightenment when they ain't achieved nothing,
 And they hearts gonna be FULL UP with pride,
 Swollen up with arrogance like they somebody special.

 Some of them gonna call themselves forest-dwellers,
 Ascetics living out in the wilderness,
 Wearing patched-up rags like holy men,
 Staying in empty, quiet places,
 CLAIMING they walking the true path,
 Looking DOWN on everybody else,
 Acting like they better than regular folks.

 But you know what they REALLY after?
 They GREEDY for profit and offerings!
 They teach the Dharma to laypeople
 Just to get gain and respect,
 And the world gonna honor them,
 Treat them like they arhats with six spiritual powers,
 Like they got ALL the supernatural abilities.

 These people got EVIL in they hearts,
 Always thinking about worldly things, mundane concerns,
 But they PRETENDING to be forest-dwellers,
 Taking that holy name in vain.
 And they gonna LOVE to point out OUR faults,
 Always looking for something wrong with US,
 Always trying to catch us in some mistake.

 They gonna SAY things like this:
 "These monks here, they just in it for profit,
 They teaching for GAIN,
 They speaking HERETICAL doctrines,
 Non-Buddhist philosophy and lies!

 "They WROTE this sutra themselves!
 Made it up out of whole cloth!
 They doing it to TRICK the world,
 To DECEIVE people,
 'Cause they hunting for fame and reputation.
 They just trying to make a name for themselves!"

 They gonna stand up in BIG ASSEMBLIES,
 In front of CROWDS of people,
 And they gonna try to SHAME us,
 Try to HUMILIATE us and tear us down.
 They gonna go running to kings and ministers,
 To brahmins and householders,
 To other monks and the whole sangha,
 SLANDERING us, saying evil things about us,
 Calling us "wrong-view people,"
 Saying we teaching "outside-the-way doctrines,"
 Heretical teachings and false philosophy.

 But we gonna RESPECT the Buddha,
 We gonna honor what you taught us,
 So we gonna ENDURE all this evil,
 We gonna bear up under all this persecution.

 Even when they mock us and say—
 And they gonna say it with CONTEMPT in they voices—
 "Oh, Y'ALL gonna be Buddhas? YOU? Ha!"
 Even when they throw that in our faces like an insult,
 We gonna ACCEPT that contempt,
 We gonna RECEIVE that mockery,
 And we gonna keep on walking.

 In the defiled age, the corrupt times,
 The evil world that's coming,
 There gonna be MANY terrifying things,
 Fearful things that try to shake us.
 Evil spirits gonna POSSESS people's bodies,
 Take them over from the inside,
 And they gonna curse us, revile us, disrespect us,
 Say all manner of evil against us.

 But WE believe in the Buddha,
 WE trust in what you taught,
 So we gonna PUT ON the armor of patience,
 We gonna WEAR that armor like a soldier going to war.
 We teaching this sutra,
 And for the sake of teaching this sutra,
 We gonna ENDURE all these hard things,
 We gonna BEAR all these difficult trials.

 We don't love our own lives—
 Listen to me now, we don't love our own LIVES—
 But we CHERISH the unsurpassed way,
 We TREASURE this supreme Dharma
 More than we treasure breathing.

 In the world that's coming,
 We gonna PROTECT what the Buddha entrusted to us,
 We gonna GUARD what you asked us to hold.
 And the World-Honored One,
 You gonna KNOW our hearts,
 You gonna see what we doing,
 You gonna be a witness.

 Those corrupt monks in the defiled age,
 They don't UNDERSTAND the Buddha's loving tricks,
 Don't understand how you teach according to what's appropriate,
 How you speak different ways for different people.
 They gonna look at us with EVIL faces,
 Scowl at us and curse us with foul mouths,
 And they gonna kick us OUT again and again,
 Ban us from the assembly over and over,
 Keep us FAR AWAY from the temples and stupas,
 Won't let us near the holy places.

 All these kinds of persecution and trouble,
 All these varieties of evil—
 We gonna remember the Buddha's instruction,
 We gonna think about what you told us,
 And we gonna ENDURE all of it.
 We gonna bear every bit of it with patience.

 In all the villages and cities,
 All the towns and hamlets,
 Wherever there's people SEEKING the Dharma,
 Folks who WANT to hear the teaching,
 We gonna GO to those places.
 We gonna go wherever they at,
 And we gonna teach the Dharma the Buddha entrusted to us,
 The teaching you asked us to carry forward.

 We the WORLD-HONORED ONE'S MESSENGERS,
 We the BUDDHA'S ENVOYS sent out into the world.
 When we standing before the assembly,
 We AIN'T AFRAID of nothing,
 We got NO FEAR in our hearts,
 'Cause we know who sent us.

 We gonna teach this Dharma WELL,
 We gonna speak it CLEAR and TRUE.
 And we asking you, Buddha:
 Dwell in peace.
 Rest easy.
 Be at ease in your heart.

 Right here before the World-Honored One,
 Here in front of all the Buddhas
 Who done come from the ten directions
 To witness this moment,
 We making this solemn vow.
 We speaking this promise out loud.
 And the Buddha—
 The Buddha KNOWS our hearts.
 The Buddha see what's in us.
 The Buddha understand our commitment.
 This our TESTIMONY,
 And it's TRUE.

### END OF CHAPTER THIRTEEN: WHEN THE STORM COMES DOWN, YOU GOTTA KEEP STANDING

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

This chapter is pure testimony—a series of sacred vows made by different groups of disciples, each pledging to carry the Dharma forward after Buddha's passing. The structure mirrors the call-and-response pattern of gospel testimony meetings, where individuals and groups stand up to declare their commitment.

**Key Metaphorical Choices:**

1. **"Armor of patience"** - The Chinese 忍辱鎧 (rěnrù kǎi) becomes the central image of spiritual protection, rendered as something you "put on" like a soldier preparing for battle.

2. **"Lion's roar"** - The Sanskrit siṃhanāda becomes "speaking with power and authority, ain't scared of nothing" - the fearless proclamation of truth.

3. **"We don't love our own lives"** - This line echoes the martyr tradition in Christianity ("they loved not their lives unto death") while preserving the Buddhist teaching of non-attachment to the physical body.

4. **"Evil spirits gonna possess people's bodies"** - The Chinese 惡鬼入其身 speaks to the very real phenomenon of corruption from within communities, rendered in language that resonates with both Buddhist demon-possession imagery and African American spiritual warfare traditions.

5. **"We the Buddha's messengers"** - This directly parallels the Christian concept of apostleship (Greek: apostolos = "one sent forth") while maintaining fidelity to the Chinese 世尊使 (messenger/envoy of the World-Honored One).

The verse section maintains the repetitive, rhythmic structure of the Chinese original while using blues-style repetition ("we gonna... we gonna...") to create oral momentum. The confrontation with corrupt monks becomes a classic blues theme: the authentic versus the fake, the true preacher versus the charlatan, those who genuinely suffer for the cause versus those who profit from religion.

The closing testimony—"The Buddha knows our hearts"—echoes both the Buddhist understanding of Buddha's omniscience and the gospel singer's assurance that "God knows my heart," creating a bridge between traditions without violating either one.

This interpretation preserves the profound seriousness of these vows while making them accessible through vernacular language that carries its own tradition of suffering, endurance, and sacred commitment.

# CHAPTER FOURTEEN: HOW TO KEEP YOUR SOUL CLEAN WHEN THE WORLD'S GONE WRONG

*Blues Interpretation from Classical Chinese by Kumārajīva*
(安樂行品 - The Peaceful Practices)

---

## MAÑJUŚRĪ ASKS THE HARD QUESTION

Now at that time, Mañjuśrī—that prince of the Dharma, that great bodhisattva—he stood up and said to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, these bodhisattvas here, they're something rare, you know? They respect you so much they done made a vow—a big vow, a serious vow—that when the evil age comes rolling in, when everything's corrupt and wrong, they gonna protect this Lotus teaching. They gonna read it, study it, teach it out loud.

But World-Honored One, I got to ask: When that evil age comes—and it's coming—how can these bodhisattvas teach this sutra without losing themselves? How they gonna speak the truth when lies run the world?"

And the Buddha said to Mañjuśrī:

"Listen now. If a bodhisattva wants to teach this sutra in the evil age to come, they got to rest themselves in four practices—four ways of being that keep you safe, keep you clean, keep you whole. The first one's about where you stand. The second's about who you stand with. You got to know your ground, and you got to know your company."

## FIRST PRACTICE: THE GROUND YOU STAND ON

"Mañjuśrī, what do I mean by a bodhisattva's ground—their place to stand?
