## VI. THE POWER OF "EVEN THEN, EVEN THERE"

Throughout the interpretation, I emphasized the words "even" and "still":

- "Even when he seen the four kinds of people way off in the distance..."
- "Even then, he would lift up his voice high..."
- "Even from a distance..."
- "He just kept saying..." (kept = ongoing, persistent, against resistance)

This is blues structure. The blues is built on "even though" and "but still" and "anyway."

Even though you done me wrong, still I love you.
Even though the world is hard, still I'm gonna make it through.
Even though you beat me down, still I'm gonna lift up my voice.

The Chinese text has this through the repetition of 常 (chang - constantly, always), 猶 (you - still, nevertheless), and 不 (bu - not, negation maintained). I rendered this persistence through the blues architecture of "even/still/anyway."

## VII. "THAT WAS ME" - THE REVELATION MOMENT

When the Buddha reveals his identity (則我身是 - "that was my very self"), I interpreted it simply: "That was me. That was my own self."

This is testimony language. This is how you tell your story in Black churches. "I was lost, but now I'm found" - the personal testimony is direct, unadorned, powerful. "That was me" hits harder than "I was that very person" or other formal constructions.

The Buddha is testifying. He's saying: I'm telling you this story because I walked this walk. I know what it costs to prophesy to hostile folks. I know what it means to get stones thrown at you for speaking truth. I know because that was me.

This is how wisdom is transmitted in the blues/gospel tradition - through testimony, through "let me tell you what I been through," through personal witness that becomes universal truth.

## VIII. THE RADICAL RESPECT THAT SEES BUDDHA-NATURE IN ENEMIES

The core teaching of this chapter - that you bow down to people who are actively beating you, that you proclaim their Buddha-nature when they're denying your humanity - this is perhaps the most blues thing in the entire Lotus Sutra.

The blues tradition knows what it means to maintain your dignity when society denies your humanity. It knows what it means to see the image of God in people who treat you as less than human. It knows what it means to prophesy freedom to people who profit from your bondage.

"I wouldn't dare look down on you" - this phrase, repeated to hostile monks with sticks and stones, is the ultimate blues stance. It's the refusal to let their violence define your vision. It's the insistence on seeing what they cannot yet see in themselves.

This is what Fannie Lou Hamer meant when she said, "Nobody's free until everybody's free." It's what Martin Luther King Jr. meant when he said the arc of the moral universe is long but it bends toward justice. It's what the spirituals meant when they sang, "Free at last, free at last, thank God almighty we're free at last" - the "we" includes even the oppressor, who is also finally freed from the karma of oppression.

Never Looked Down prophesies the liberation of his oppressors. That's the ultimate blues wisdom - the recognition that the one with the stick is also trapped, is also suffering, will also need to be liberated. And when you can see that clearly enough to bow down to their Buddha-nature even while they're beating you - that's when you're free. That's when your eyes are purified. That's when you gain the power to extend life, to teach multitudes, to transform enemies into disciples.

## IX. "GONNA" AS ONTOLOGICAL COMMITMENT

The word "gonna" appears throughout this interpretation, and it deserves special attention as a theological term.

"You gonna become a Buddha" - this is not:
- "You might become a Buddha" (speculation)
- "You could become a Buddha" (possibility)
- "You should become a Buddha" (moral imperative)
- "You will become a Buddha" (formal prediction)

"Gonna" is certainty expressed in vernacular. It's the future tense that stakes everything on its truth. When Never Looked Down says "You gonna become a Buddha," he's not making a conditional prediction. He's declaring an ontological truth that exists independent of current appearances.

This is how "gonna" functions in the African American prophetic tradition:
- "We gonna overcome" - sung in the face of dogs and fire hoses
- "Truth gonna rise" - preached in the face of lies
- "You gonna reap what you sow" - declared in the face of injustice that seems to prosper

"Gonna" is prophetic faith made linguistic. It's the future that's so certain it's already being spoken into the present. It's the refusal to let current conditions determine future possibilities.

When Never Looked Down keeps saying "You gonna become a Buddha" while stones are flying at his head, he's doing what the blues and gospel traditions have always done - declaring a truth that transcends and transforms present suffering.

## X. CONCLUSION: THE BLUES LOTUS

This interpretation attempts to let the Lotus Sutra speak in the voice of a tradition that knows deeply about suffering, persistence, prophecy, and transformation. The blues and gospel traditions have their own vocabulary for enlightenment, their own understanding of how the word has power, their own practices of maintaining dignity under oppression.

Never Looked Down is a blues hero. His story is a blues story. And when interpreted into blues register, new dimensions of the Dharma become audible - dimensions about power and prophecy, about dignity and endurance, about seeing the Buddha-nature in your enemy and declaring it out loud until it becomes true.

The blues tradition says: you can take everything from me, but you can't take my song. Never Looked Down says: you can throw stones at me, but you can't stop me from bowing down to your Buddha-nature.

That's the same teaching, sung in different keys.

This interpretation is offered in the faith that the Dharma is big enough to hold every voice, that the Lotus opens in every language, that wisdom speaks in every tongue - including this one, the tongue that sang freedom in chains, that prophesied justice in Jim Crow, that proclaimed "We shall overcome" when overcoming seemed impossible.

May all beings hear the teaching in the voice that reaches their hearts.
May all beings see the Buddha-nature in themselves and others.
May all beings become Buddhas, just like Never Looked Down said.

You gonna become a Buddha. Every one of you.
I wouldn't dare look down.

─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Interpretation completed October 31, 2025
From the Classical Chinese text of Kumārajīva (406 CE)

Interpretation dedication: For all those who kept prophesying freedom when freedom seemed impossible. For all those who bowed down to the Buddha-nature in people who couldn't see it yet. For all those who lifted up their voices from a distance and declared the truth anyway. You was Never Looked Down. That was you.

# CHAPTER TWENTY-ONE: WHEN THE LORD SHOWED HIS HAND

(The Buddha's Supernatural Powers / 如來神力品第二十一)

A Blues/Gospel Interpretation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese

## THE VOW TO WITNESS

Now listen here—at that time, all them Bodhisattvas what come up from the earth, countless as dust motes in a thousand worlds, they all standing there before the Buddha, hands pressed together, gazing up at that holy face, and they speaking out their hearts:

"World-Honored One, after you done gone on from this world, wherever your presence still abiding—in all them lands where your manifested bodies been teaching, in every place where you entered into rest—we gonna spread this sutra far and wide.

Why we saying this? Because we ourselves want to get hold of this true and pure Great Law, want to keep it, read it, recite it, explain it, write it down, and make offerings to it."

## THE GREAT REVEALING

Now at that time the World-Honored One, right there before Mañjuśrī and all them countless hundreds of thousands of myriads of millions of Bodhisattvas what been dwelling in this Saha world from way back, and before all the monks and nuns, the laymen and laywomen, the devas and dragons and yakshas and gandharvas and asuras and garudas and kinnaras and mahoragas, before humans and non-humans alike—before EVERY LIVING THING THAT EVER WAS—the Buddha showed forth his great supernatural power.

And what he do?

He put out his broad long tongue, and it reached straight up to the Brahma heaven.

From every pore of his body he released immeasurable, numberless colored lights, and they shone everywhere throughout the ten directions.

And all them other Buddhas sitting on lion thrones under jeweled trees, they did the same thing—put out they broad long tongues and released immeasurable light.

When Śākyamuni Buddha and all them Buddhas under the jeweled trees was showing forth they supernatural power, it lasted for a hundred thousand years.

Then they drew back they tongue-signs.

And then—oh, then!—they coughed once, ALL TOGETHER, and snapped they fingers, ALL AT THE SAME TIME.

And them two sounds—that cough and that finger-snap—they traveled to all the Buddha-worlds in the ten directions.

And the earth, she shook six ways.

## THE UNIVERSAL WITNESS

Now all the living beings in them worlds—the devas and dragons and yakshas and gandharvas and asuras and garudas and kinnaras and mahoragas, humans and non-humans—through the Buddha's supernatural power, they ALL SAW this Saha world.

They saw immeasurable, boundless hundreds of thousands of myriads of millions of Buddhas sitting on lion thrones under jeweled trees.

They saw Śākyamuni Buddha sitting together with Many Treasures Tathāgata on the lion throne inside that jeweled stupa.

They saw immeasurable, boundless hundreds of thousands of myriads of millions of Bodhisattvas and the fourfold assembly respectfully surrounding Śākyamuni Buddha.

When they seen all this, they was filled with great joy—they got something they never had before!

## THE CALL AND THE RESPONSE

Right then and there, the devas in empty space, they raised up they voices high and made this proclamation:

"Beyond these immeasurable, boundless hundreds of thousands of myriads of millions of asamkhyeya worlds, there's a land called Saha. In that land there's a Buddha called Śākyamuni. Right now he teaching the great vehicle sutra to the Bodhisattvas—it called the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Flower, the teaching for Bodhisattvas, the one the Buddha guards and keeps in mind.

Y'all should rejoice from the bottom of your hearts! You should bow down and make offerings to Śākyamuni Buddha!"

When all them beings heard that voice from empty space, they pressed they palms together, turned toward the Saha world, and said:

"Glory to Śākyamuni Buddha! Glory to Śākyamuni Buddha!"

They took all kinds of flowers and incense and necklaces, banners and canopies, jewels and precious ornaments for adorning the body, and they scattered them from afar toward the Saha world.

All them scattered things coming from the ten directions, they gathered like clouds, transformed into jeweled canopies, and spread out covering all the Buddhas there.

And in that time, the worlds of the ten directions was completely open and unobstructed, like one Buddha land.

## THE ENTRUSTMENT

At that time the Buddha told Superior Practices and all them other Bodhisattvas:

"The supernatural powers of the Buddhas—they just like this: immeasurable, boundless, inconceivable. Even if I was to use these supernatural powers for immeasurable, boundless hundreds of thousands of myriads of millions of asamkhyeya kalpas, talking about the merit and virtue of this sutra for the sake of entrusting it to y'all, I still couldn't finish telling it.

Let me sum it up for you:

All the Dharmas the Tathāgata possesses,
All the sovereign supernatural powers of the Tathāgata,
All the storehouse of profound essentials of the Tathāgata,
All the most deep matters of the Tathāgata—

ALL OF IT is proclaimed and revealed and demonstrated in this sutra.

Therefore, after the Tathāgata done passed on into extinction, y'all should single-mindedly receive and keep, read and recite, explain and expound, transcribe and practice according to what it says.

In whatever land you find yourself, if someone receives and keeps, reads and recites, explains and expounds, transcribes and practices according to what this sutra says—or if there's a place where a sutra scroll dwells, whether in a garden, or in a grove, or under a tree, or in a monk's dwelling, or in a white-clothed person's house, or in a hall or palace, or in a mountain valley or wilderness—in that place you should build a stupa and make offerings.

Why is that? You should know: THAT PLACE IS THE SITE OF ENLIGHTENMENT.

The Buddhas attained anuttara-samyak-sambodhi at that place.
The Buddhas turned the Dharma wheel at that place.
The Buddhas entered nirvāṇa at that place."

## THE SUTRA IN VERSE

At that time the World-Honored One, wanting to restate this meaning, spoke in verse:

The Buddhas, saviors of the world,
Dwelling in great supernatural penetrations,
In order to gladden living beings,
Display immeasurable supernatural powers.

They tongues reaching to the Brahma heaven,
They bodies releasing numberless lights—
For them seeking the Buddha Way,
They manifest these rare occurrences.

The sound of the Buddhas coughing,
The sound of they fingers snapping,
Heard throughout the lands in ten directions,
The earth shaking six ways everywhere.

Because after the Buddha's extinction
Someone able to uphold this sutra,
All the Buddhas rejoicing,
Display immeasurable supernatural powers.

For the sake of entrusting this sutra,
Praising those who receive and keep it,
Even through immeasurable kalpas
They still can't come to the end of it.

The merit and virtue of such a person—
Boundless, without limit,
Like the empty space of ten directions,
You can't find no edge to it.

One who able to uphold this sutra,
That person already seen me,
Already seen Many Treasures Buddha
And all the manifested body Buddhas.

They also seen me today
Teaching and transforming the Bodhisattvas.
One who able to uphold this sutra
Makes me and my manifested bodies,
Makes Many Treasures Buddha now extinct,
Makes ALL OF THEM rejoice.

The Buddhas existing now in ten directions,
The Buddhas of past and future too—
They also gonna see them, gonna make offerings to them,
Gonna make them rejoice as well.

The secret essential Dharma
The Buddhas attained sitting at the place of enlightenment—
One who able to uphold this sutra
Gonna get it before too long.

One who able to uphold this sutra—
When it come to the meaning of all dharmas,
The names and the words,
They gonna preach with inexhaustible joy,
Like wind in empty space
That ain't got no obstruction nowhere.

After the Tathāgata's extinction,
They gonna know the sutras the Buddha taught,
The causes and conditions and the sequence,
And teach them according to the true meaning,
Like the light of sun and moon
That removes all darkness—

This person walking through the world
Able to destroy the darkness of living beings,
Teaching immeasurable Bodhisattvas,
Finally causing them to dwell in the One Vehicle.

Therefore, if you got wisdom,
Hearing these benefits and merits,
After my extinction
You should receive and uphold this sutra.

Such a person, on the Buddha Way,
Decidedly ain't got no doubt.

### END OF CHAPTER TWENTY-ONE: WHEN THE LORD SHOWED HIS HAND

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

On Cosmic Witness, Signs and Wonders, and the Universal "Amen"

## 1. THE COSMIC REGISTER

This chapter operates at the highest register in the entire Lotus Sutra—this is the moment when the Buddha "shows his hand," reveals the full scope of his power, and the entire universe bears witness. The challenge in a blues/gospel interpretation is to maintain both the vernacular authenticity AND the sense of cosmic scale.

The solution: Let the simple, concrete images do the heavy lifting. A tongue reaching to heaven. Light from every pore. A cough and finger-snap heard across universes. The earth shaking six ways. These images don't need academic language—they need direct, sensory language that lets us SEE and HEAR what's happening.

In African American religious tradition, there's a well-established vocabulary for cosmic theophany: "When the Lord showed up," "When glory came down," "When heaven touched earth." This chapter is precisely that moment—cosmic theophany, the showing-forth of divine presence that makes the whole creation respond.

## 2. CALL-AND-RESPONSE STRUCTURE

The architecture of this chapter is pure call-and-response:

CALL: The Buddha displays supernatural powers
RESPONSE: All beings witness and see

CALL: The Devas announce from heaven
RESPONSE: All beings bow and make offerings

CALL: The Buddha entrusts the sutra
RESPONSE: The Bodhisattvas vow to teach it

This is the fundamental structure of both Buddhist liturgy and African American sacred music. The proclamation goes out, and the whole assembly responds. Here, the "assembly" is every living thing in ten directions—the ultimate congregation.

The phrase "Glory to Śākyamuni Buddha!" (南無釋迦牟尼佛) works perfectly as a gospel response—it has the same function as "Glory hallelujah!" or "Praise the Lord!" It's the unanimous shout of recognition and affirmation.

## 3. THE EARTH SHAKING SIX WAYS

In Buddhist cosmology, the six-way earthquake (六種震動) is one of the standard signs that something of ultimate importance is happening. The earth shakes in six ways: trembling, roaring, crashing, quaking, bellowing, and rising/surging.

In blues/gospel terms, this is when "the whole world rocks." It's the physical world responding to spiritual truth—matter itself bearing witness to what's happening.

There's a deep correspondence here with the African American spiritual tradition where the Holy Spirit's presence is marked by physical response—shouting, dancing, falling, trembling. The earth shaking is the cosmos doing what the sanctified saints do when glory comes down—it can't keep still.

"The earth, she shook six ways" uses feminine personification (common in blues) to make the earth an active respondent, not a passive object. The earth is a witness, a participant in the cosmic celebration.

## 4. SIGNS AND WONDERS AS COMMUNICATION

The Buddha's supernatural display here is not arbitrary showing-off—it's maximum communication. He's using every available channel to broadcast the truth:

- VISUAL: Tongue extending, light radiating
- AUDITORY: Coughing, finger-snapping
- KINESTHETIC: Earth shaking

This is multimedia theophany. The Buddha is making sure NOBODY can miss this moment. Every sense is addressed. Every being, no matter what kind of perception they have, receives the message.

In gospel tradition, there's the understanding that when the Spirit is REALLY moving, it manifests in multiple ways simultaneously—in sound (music, shouting), in sight (people falling, dancing), in physical sensation (shaking, heat, tears). The Holy Spirit refuses to be contained in a single channel.

The Buddha's display here operates the same way—this is truth that can't be contained, that overflows every boundary, that demands every form of witness simultaneously.

## 5. "LIKE ONE BUDDHA LAND" — THE COLLAPSE OF DISTANCE

The line "於時十方世界，通達無礙，如一佛土" (At that time the worlds of the ten directions were completely open and unobstructed, like one Buddha land) is one of the most profound moments in the chapter.

All distance collapses. The "there" becomes "here." Infinite worlds become one world.

This is the Lotus Sutra's ultimate vision of non-duality—not abstract philosophical non-duality, but experiential, visible non-duality. Every being in every world SEES the same thing. There's no privileged viewpoint, no "you had to be there." Everyone IS there.

In blues/gospel terms, this is the moment when "heaven came down and glory filled the place"—when the distinction between sacred and mundane, far and near, heaven and earth, dissolves in the presence of the Spirit.

The practical implication: You don't have to be at Vulture Peak. You don't have to be in the Buddha's physical presence. Wherever the sutra is, THAT place becomes Vulture Peak. The "then" is available "now." The "there" is present "here."

## 6. ANY PLACE BECOMES HOLY GROUND

The Buddha's teaching that any place where the sutra dwells becomes a holy site—whether garden, grove, tree, monastery, layperson's house, hall, mountain, wilderness—is radical democratization of sacred space.

## "THAT PLACE IS THE SITE OF ENLIGHTENMENT" (是處即是道場).

Not "becomes like" the site of enlightenment. Not "symbolizes" the site. IS the site.

This connects to Moses at the burning bush: "Take off your shoes, for the place where you're standing is holy ground." The ground doesn't become holy through human consecration—it's revealed as ALREADY holy through the presence of the sacred.

In blues/gospel imagination: "Wherever two or three are gathered in my name, there I am in the midst of them." The sacred isn't far away, reserved for special temples and special people. The sacred is right here, right now, wherever the Word is present.

## 7. SUTRA-KEEPER BECOMES BUDDHA-SEER

The verse section makes an extraordinary claim: "One who able to uphold this sutra, / That person already seen me."

Not "will see" in some future. Already seen. Present tense.

To hold the sutra is to see the Buddha. Not metaphorically. The verse is clear: you see Śākyamuni, you see Many Treasures, you see all the emanation bodies, you see the Buddhas of past and future.

This is the Lotus Sutra's core soteriology in a nutshell: The Dharma and the Buddha are not separate. To encounter the teaching is to encounter the teacher. The Word and the Person are one.

In Christian terms (which deeply influenced African American religious consciousness): "In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God, and the Word was God." The Word is not ABOUT the divine—the Word IS divine presence.

For the sutra-keeper, reading/chanting/copying the text is not studying something absent. It's communing with someone present.

## 8. INFINITE GRATITUDE, INEXHAUSTIBLE PRAISE

"Even if I was to use these supernatural powers for immeasurable, boundless hundreds of thousands of myriads of millions of asamkhyeya kalpas, talking about the merit and virtue of this sutra for the sake of entrusting it to y'all, I still couldn't finish telling it."

The Buddha, with infinite time and infinite supernatural power, cannot exhaust his praise of those who keep the sutra.

This is a profound reversal: Usually we think WE can't sufficiently praise the Buddha. Here, the Buddha can't sufficiently praise US (the sutra-keepers).

In gospel terms, this is the moment when God says "Well done, good and faithful servant"—but extended infinitely. God's gratitude and joy in the faithful witness is itself infinite.

The implication: The act of keeping, teaching, copying, explaining this sutra is not a small thing. It's not "just" religious activity. It's the most cosmically significant act possible. The Buddha marshals all the powers of the universe to show this.

## 9. THE BURDEN OF WITNESS

The Bodhisattvas emerging from the earth make their vow: "We gonna spread this sutra far and wide."

This is accepting the burden of witness. Not just personal enlightenment, but transmission. Not just saving yourself, but becoming the vehicle for others' salvation.

In blues/gospel tradition, there's the understanding that once you've "been in the storm too long," once you've "come through hard trials," you have an obligation to "tell the story," to "give your testimony," to "let your light shine."

The Lotus Sutra operates with the same logic: If you've received this teaching, you're now responsible for passing it on. The gift demands regifting.

The chapter ends with the affirmation that those who do this work—who teach the sutra after the Buddha's extinction—"decidedly ain't got no doubt" on the Buddha Way. Their own enlightenment is assured precisely through the act of helping others to enlightenment.

## 10. ON INTERPRETATION CHOICES: VERNACULAR ELEVATION

The key challenge in this chapter was maintaining vernacular authenticity while reaching for cosmic scale. Some specific choices:

"When the Lord Showed His Hand" as alternate title - Drawing on the expression "showing your hand" (revealing your true power/resources), common in both card games and vernacular speech. Also resonates with "the hand of God" in biblical tradition.

"They put out they broad long tongues" - Keeping the multiple-subject "they" with the pronoun "they" (rather than "their") maintains vernacular grammar while describing an event that encompasses countless Buddhas acting in unison.

"The earth, she shook six ways" - Feminine personification of earth is common in blues and makes the earthquake an active response, not passive effect.

"Glory to Śākyamuni Buddha" for 南無 (namo) - While "namo" is usually interpreted "homage" or "refuge," in this context of cosmic call-and-response, "Glory to" captures the jubilant, proclamatory quality better.

"That place IS the site of enlightenment" - Emphasis added to capture the force of 即是 (ji shi), which means "precisely is," "exactly is," not merely "is like" or "represents."

"Decidedly ain't got no doubt" - The double negative ("ain't got no") is grammatically marked vernacular but semantically emphatic—it intensifies rather than negates, which matches the force of the Chinese 決定無有疑 (decidedly without doubt).

## CONCLUSION: WHEN EARTH AND HEAVEN SAY AMEN

This chapter is the Lotus Sutra's climax of cosmic affirmation. Everything that has been taught, everything that has been promised, receives the universe's "Amen."

The Buddha shows his power—not to dominate, but to communicate. The supernatural displays are not threats but invitations: "See! Hear! Know! Witness!"

All beings respond—not in fear, but in joy. They "got something they never had before."

The teaching goes forth—not to be hoarded, but to be spread "far and wide."

And any place where this happens becomes holy ground—the place where Buddhas awakened, the place where they taught, the place where they entered rest.

In blues/gospel terms, this is the moment when testimony becomes cosmic, when witness becomes universal, when the local congregation expands to include every living thing in ten directions, when "every knee shall bow and every tongue confess," when "the earth is full of his glory."

The blues has always known: When the truth is told truly, heaven and earth respond. The world itself bears witness. The ground shakes. Light breaks forth. And all creation says together:

"Glory to Śākyamuni Buddha! Glory to Śākyamuni Buddha!"

Amen, and amen.

---

Interpreted using Claude Code (Sonnet 4.5)
From Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese translation (406 CE)
Into Blues/Gospel Vernacular English
October 31, 2025

妙法蓮華經 / Saddharma-puṇḍarīka-sūtra
The Sutra of the Lotus Flower of the Wonderful Dharma

# CHAPTER TWENTY-TWO: THE PASSING ON

(Entrustment - 囑累品第二十二)

A Blues/Gospel Interpretation from the Classical Chinese

## THE LAYING ON OF HANDS

Now you got to see this right—
The Buddha, Śākyamuni himself,
He rise up from that teaching seat,
And he show forth great spiritual power.

He reach out with his right hand,
And he touch the heads
Of them countless Bodhisattvas,
Every blessed one,
And when his hand come down on them,
He speak these words:

"I been working at this
For more ages than you can count—
Hundred thousand million billion
Uncountable ages—
Working and struggling
To get this hard-to-get thing,
This supreme perfect enlightenment.

Now I'm putting it in your hands.
I'm entrusting it to you.
You got to take this teaching,
Spread it out with one mind,
Make it grow far and wide."

## THE SECOND TIME

And he do it again—
Three times he touch them heads,
Three times he lay his hand down,
Three times he speak the word:

"I been working at this
For more ages than you can count—
Hundred thousand million billion
Uncountable ages—
Working and struggling
To get this hard-to-get thing,
This supreme perfect enlightenment.

Now I'm putting it in your hands.
I'm entrusting it to you.

You got to receive it,
Hold it,
Read it,
Recite it,
Spread it abroad,
Make sure all living beings
Everywhere hear it and know it.

Why I'm telling you this?
Because the Tathāgata got great compassion—
He ain't stingy,
He ain't holding back,
He ain't afraid.

He give to living beings
The Buddha's wisdom,
The Tathāgata's wisdom,
The natural wisdom that come by itself.

The Tathāgata—
He the great gift-giver
To all living beings.

## YOUR CALLING

Now you got to follow his example,
Learn the Tathāgata's way,
Don't be holding back,
Don't be stingy with what you got.

In the world that's coming,
If there be good men and good women
Who believe in the Tathāgata's wisdom,
You got to preach this Lotus Teaching to them,
Make sure they hear it and know it,
So they can get the Buddha's wisdom.

But if there be living beings
Who don't believe it,
Who can't receive it—
Then you show them some other deep teaching
That the Tathāgata left,
Teach them,
Guide them,
Benefit them,
Make them glad.

You do this,
You be repaying
What all the Buddhas done for you.
You be answering grace with grace."

## THE TRIPLE AFFIRMATION

Now when them Bodhisattvas
Heard the Buddha say all this,
Great joy fill them up,
Joy running all through their bodies.

They bow down even deeper,
They bend low,
They drop their heads,
They put their palms together,
And they lift up their voice as one:

"As the World-Honored One command,
We will do it all—
Every bit of it.
Yes, World-Honored One!
Don't you worry none."

And them Bodhisattvas,
That great assembly,
They do it three times—
Three times they lift up their voice as one:

"As the World-Honored One command,
We will do it all—
Every bit of it.
Yes, World-Honored One!
Don't you worry none."

## THE SENDING HOME

Then Śākyamuni Buddha,
He tell all them Buddhas
Who come from the ten directions,
All them manifestation bodies:

"Go on back now,
Go to your own lands,
Go where you belong.

And the Stupa of Many Treasures—
Let it be as it was before."

And when he speak these words,
All them countless Buddhas
From the ten directions,
Sitting under them jewel trees
On them lion seats—

And the Many Treasures Buddha,

And Superior Conduct
And all them countless Bodhisattvas,

And Śāriputra
And all them voice-hearers,
The fourfold assembly—

And all the worlds,
The heavens,
The humans,
The asuras,
All them beings—

They hear what the Buddha said,
And great joy fill them up,
Every blessed one.

### END OF CHAPTER TWENTY-TWO: THE PASSING ON

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

This chapter contains the most physically intimate moment in the entire Lotus Sutra. After twenty-one chapters of cosmic visions, parables, prophecies, and supernatural displays, we arrive at a simple human gesture: the Buddha rises from his seat and puts his hand on people's heads. Three times.

This is transmission through touch. Not through doctrine, not through vision, but through the laying on of hands—a gesture that runs deep in both Buddhist and Christian ritual practice. When the Buddha's hand comes down on those Bodhisattvas' heads, something passes. Not just information, but authorization. Not just teaching, but succession. Not just wisdom, but responsibility.

## THE POWER OF THREE

The threefold repetition is crucial. In both Buddhist and gospel traditions, three is the number of confirmation. Peter denied Jesus three times; Jesus asked Peter "Do you love me?" three times. In Buddhist ritual, we take refuge in the Three Jewels three times. We prostrate three times. We circumambulate three times.

When the Buddha lays his hand on their heads three times and speaks the commission three times, he's doing more than emphasis. He's making it stick. He's sealing it in the body, in the memory, in the muscle. You don't forget what was said three times with a hand on your head.

And when the Bodhisattvas respond three times—"Yes, Lord! We will do it all! Don't you worry none!"—that's the gospel responsorial pattern. That's the congregation answering the preacher. That's call and response, where the answer is given back stronger each time, until it becomes a vow, a commitment, a binding oath.

## MEETING PEOPLE WHERE THEY ARE

The practical wisdom in this chapter is beautiful: "If they believe, teach them the Lotus. If they don't believe, teach them something else from the Buddha's deep teachings that they can receive."

This is the opposite of rigidity. This is the opposite of "my way or the highway." The Buddha's generosity is so complete that he doesn't need everyone to receive the highest teaching right now. He knows there are many doors, many paths, many ways to help beings. The Lotus Sutra is supreme, yes—but if someone can't hear it, don't beat them with it. Give them what they can receive. Meet them where they are.

This is the wisdom of a real teacher, not a dogmatist. This is someone who knows that the point is liberation, not orthodoxy.

## GRACE ANSWERED WITH GRACE

"If you do this, you'll be repaying what all the Buddhas have done for you." This is the theology of grace and response. The Buddha isn't saying "You owe me." He's saying "Pass it on." The way you honor what you've received is by giving it away.

This is the blues ethic: somebody helped me when I was down, so when I get up, I help somebody else. This is the gospel ethic: "Freely you have received, freely give." The debt is paid forward, not backward. You can't repay the Buddha directly—he doesn't need it. You repay him by doing for others what he did for you.

## THE GENTLE DISMISSAL

After all the cosmic drama—the Stupa rising from the earth, the Buddhas gathering from all worlds, the Bodhisattvas emerging from underground—the ending is quiet. "Go on home now. Go where you belong."

There's no drama in the dismissal. No thunder, no spectacle. Just the gentle sending away: "The assembly is over. You've received what you came for. Now go on back to your own place."

And that "great joy" at the end—that's not excitement, that's peace. That's the joy of knowing what you're supposed to do. That's the joy of having received a clear commission. That's the joy of being entrusted with something precious and knowing you can carry it.

The Stupa returns to its place. The Buddhas return to their worlds. The assembly departs in joy. Everything returns to where it belongs, but transformed. The teaching has been given. The responsibility has been accepted. The succession is complete.

This is how you end a gospel service: with a benediction, a sending forth, and joy.

---

Interpretation completed: October 31, 2025
From Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese (406 CE)
Interpretater: [William Altig]

# CHAPTER TWENTY-THREE: MEDICINE KING BODHISATTVA - THE STORY OF HOW HE GAVE IT ALL

藥王菩薩本事品第二十三

Interpreted from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese into Blues/Gospel Vernacular English

## THE QUESTION

Now at that time, Constellation King Flower Bodhisattva stood up and said to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, how does Medicine King Bodhisattva walk through this suffering world of ours? World-Honored One, this Medicine King Bodhisattva—he done how many hundred thousand million billion impossible hard practices? Tell us, World-Honored One! We want to know a little bit about this story."

And all the gods in heaven, all the dragons, all the spirits, all the night-wanderers, the music-makers, the jealous fighting gods, the great bird-kings, the half-human singers, the great serpent spirits, the humans and the not-quite-humans, and all the bodhisattvas come from other Buddha-lands, and all the disciples gathered there—they all wanted to hear it too, and they were glad in their hearts.

## THE OLD STORY - A LONG TIME BACK

Then the Buddha said to Constellation King Flower Bodhisattva:

"Listen now. A long time back—we talking about more ages past than there's grains of sand in the Ganges River—there was a Buddha called Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Thus Come One. He had all ten titles: Worthy of Offerings, Perfectly Enlightened in Right Knowledge, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.

"That Buddha, he had eighty billion great bodhisattvas—great beings, every one—and seventy-two times the sands of the Ganges River worth of great disciples who heard his teaching. That Buddha lived for forty-two thousand ages, and the bodhisattvas lived just as long.

"In that Buddha-land, there wasn't no women, no hell realms, no hungry ghost realms, no animal realms, no jealous fighting gods, none of that suffering business. The ground was flat and smooth as the palm of your hand, made of lapis lazuli stone—pure blue jewel-ground. Treasure trees stood everywhere for beauty, treasure canopies spread overhead, treasure flower banners hanging down. Treasure bottles and incense burners everywhere you looked, all through that land. Seven kinds of precious things made up the platforms, and every tree had its platform—each tree standing just one arrow-shot away from its platform.

"Under all them treasure trees, bodhisattvas and disciples sat in meditation. And up on all them treasure platforms, there was hundreds of billions of gods making heavenly music, singing praises to that Buddha, making offerings with their songs."

## THE BODHISATTVA WHO EVERYBODY LOVED TO SEE

"Now at that time, that Buddha was preaching the Lotus Sutra to a bodhisattva called Gladly Seen by All Living Beings—yeah, 'Gladly Seen by All Living Beings,' that's his name—and to all the other bodhisattvas and disciples gathered there.

"This Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva, he loved to practice hard things. In the Dharma of Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha, he practiced with intensity, walking meditation with his whole heart, seeking Buddhahood with everything he had. For twelve thousand full years he did this.

"Then he attained it—he got the samādhi called 'Manifestation of All Physical Forms.' Got that samādhi, and his heart was filled with great joy.

"He thought to himself: 'I got this Manifestation of All Physical Forms samādhi because I heard the Lotus Sutra. That's the power that did it. Now I got to make an offering to Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha and to the Lotus Sutra.'

"Right then and there he entered that samādhi, and up in the empty sky he rained down mandarava flowers and great mandarava flowers. He rained down fine powdered hard black sandalwood—filled up the whole sky like clouds coming down. And he rained down sandalwood from the shore of the ocean—this sandalwood so precious that six ounces of it worth a whole world like ours—making offerings to that Buddha."

## WHEN THINGS AIN'T ENOUGH

"After making that offering, he came out of his samādhi and thought to himself:

'Even though I made offerings to the Buddha with supernatural power, that ain't enough. I got to make an offering with my body.'

"So he started drinking fragrant oils—sandalwood oil, frankincense oil, turpentine oil, sweet-smelling oils, camphor oil, and all kinds of oils. He drank oils made from jasmine and all kinds of fragrant flower oils. He did this for one thousand two hundred years.

"After one thousand two hundred years, his whole body was saturated with fragrant oils. He rubbed more fragrant oils all over his body. Then, in front of Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha, he wrapped himself in heavenly treasure cloth, poured more fragrant oils all over himself, and by the power of his supernatural vow, he set his own body on fire.

"The light from his burning body lit up eighty billion times the sands of the Ganges River worth of worlds.

"In all those worlds, the Buddhas all praised him at the same time, saying:

'GOOD! GOOD! GOOD, good son! THIS is true intensity! THIS is what we call true Dharma-offering to the Thus Come One! Even if you make offerings with flowers, incense, necklaces, burning incense, powdered incense, anointing incense, heavenly silk banners and canopies, even sandalwood from the ocean shore—all kinds of things like that—can't match this. Even if you give away countries and cities, wives and children—can't match this. Good son, THIS is the first among all givings, the most honored, the most supreme among all offerings, because this is making Dharma-offerings to all the Thus Come Ones!'

"They said all that, then they fell silent.

"His body burned for one thousand two hundred years. After that time passed, his body was finally consumed."

## HE CAME BACK

"After Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva made that Dharma-offering, his life ended. But after his life ended, he was born again in the land of Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha, in the house of Pure Virtue King. Sitting in full lotus position, he suddenly appeared—born by transformation.

"Right away he spoke a verse to his father:

 Great King, you should know this now:
 I walked in practice in that place,
 And right away I got the samādhi
 Called Manifestation of All Physical Forms.
 I practiced with great intensity,
 Gave up my beloved body,
 Made offerings to the World-Honored One,
 Seeking that supreme wisdom.

"After speaking that verse, he said to his father:

'Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha is still present right now. Before, I made offerings to that Buddha, and I gained the dhāraṇī for understanding the languages of all living beings. Plus, I heard this Lotus Sutra—eight hundred thousand million billion nayuta koṭi vimbaras and aśaṅkhyeya verses of it.

'Great King, now I got to go back and make offerings to that Buddha again.'

"Soon as he said that, he sat himself on a seven-treasure platform and rose up into empty space—rose up high as seven tāla trees—and went to where that Buddha was. He bowed his head to the Buddha's feet, put his ten fingertips together, and praised the Buddha with a verse:

 Your face is wonderfully strange and fine,
 Your light shines through the ten directions,
 I made offerings once before,
 Now I come again to see you face to face.

## THE BUDDHA'S PASSING - THE TRUST HANDED DOWN

"After Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva spoke that verse, he said to the Buddha:

'World-Honored One, the World-Honored One is still present in the world!'

"Then Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha said to Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva:

'Good son, the time for my nirvāṇa has come. The time for extinction has arrived. You can prepare the bed and seat. Tonight, in the middle watch of the night, I will enter nirvāṇa.'

"And he charged Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva:

'Good son, I entrust the Buddha-Dharma to you—to you and to all the bodhisattvas and great disciples, and also the Dharma of supreme perfect enlightenment. I also entrust to you the three-thousand great thousandfold seven-treasure world—all the treasure trees, treasure platforms, and the serving gods—I hand all of it over to you. After I pass into extinction, my relics—I entrust them to you too. You should distribute them widely, set up offerings far and wide. You should build several thousand stupas.'

"After Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha charged Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva like that, in the last watch of the night, he entered nirvāṇa."

## GRIEF AND THE FUNERAL FIRE

"When Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva saw the Buddha pass into extinction, he was struck with grief, brokenhearted, filled with longing for the Buddha.

"So he took sandalwood from the ocean shore and made a funeral pyre to honor the Buddha's body, and burned it.

"After the fire went out, he gathered the relics and made eighty-four thousand treasure urns. With those, he built eighty-four thousand stupas—high as three worlds, decorated with banners and flags, adorned with hanging canopies, with all kinds of treasure bells hanging down."

## THE BURNING OF THE ARMS - WHEN THE BODY AIN'T COMPLETE NO MORE

"Then Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva thought to himself again:

'Even though I made these offerings, my heart still ain't satisfied. I got to make more offerings to the relics.'

"So he said to all the bodhisattvas and great disciples and to the gods, dragons, night-wanderers, and all the great assembly:

'Y'all should focus your hearts and minds. I'm about to make offerings now to the relics of Sun Moon Pure Bright Virtue Buddha.'

"After he said that, right there in front of those eighty-four thousand stupas, he burned his two arms—adorned with a hundred blessings, those arms were—and he burned them for seventy-two thousand years, making offerings.

"This caused countless people seeking to be disciples, and measureless uncountable people beyond measure, to bring forth the mind of supreme perfect enlightenment. And they all attained dwelling in the samādhi of Manifestation of All Physical Forms."

## THE WITNESSES CRY OUT - THE BODY AIN'T WHOLE NO MORE

"Now when all them bodhisattvas, gods, humans, jealous fighting gods, and the rest saw he didn't have no arms, they were distressed and sorrowful and grief-struck, and they said:

'This Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva is our teacher, the one who taught us and guided us. But now he burned his arms, and his body ain't complete no more!'

## THE VOW - IF THIS IS REAL, LET IT RESTORE

"Right then, Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva stood there in the midst of that great assembly and made this vow:

'I gave up both my arms. I WILL attain the Buddha's golden body. If that's real and not empty words, if that's true and not a lie, then let my two arms come back just like they were!'

"Soon as he made that vow, his arms naturally restored themselves—came back just like they were.

"This happened because of the deep merit, the thick virtue, the profound wisdom of that bodhisattva.

"Right at that time, the three-thousand great thousandfold world shook six ways. Heaven rained down treasure flowers. All the humans and gods experienced what they'd never experienced before."

## PART ELEVEN: WHO HE IS NOW

The Buddha said to Constellation King Flower Bodhisattva:

"What do you think? Was Gladly Seen by All Living Beings Bodhisattva some other person? No sir! Right now, it's Medicine King Bodhisattva. He gave up his body and made offerings like that—countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions beyond counting, that's how many times he gave.

"Constellation King Flower, if someone brings forth the mind seeking supreme perfect enlightenment, and they can burn even just one finger, or even just one toe, as an offering to a Buddha stupa—that's worth more than giving away countries, cities, wives, children, or even all the mountains, forests, rivers, and pools and precious treasures in a three-thousand great thousandfold world.

"And if someone fills up a three-thousand great thousandfold world with the seven treasures and makes offerings to the Buddha and to great bodhisattvas and to pratyekabuddhas and to arhats—the merit that person gets don't compare to someone who receives and keeps this Lotus Sutra, even just one four-line verse of it. THAT blessing is the greatest."

## PART TWELVE: THE PRAISES - THIS SUTRA IS SUPREME

"Constellation King Flower, listen:

"Just like among all the flowing streams and rivers, among all waters, the ocean is first—this Lotus Sutra is like that. Among all the sutras preached by the Thus Come Ones, it's the deepest and the greatest.

"And just like among earth mountains, black mountains, small iron-ring mountains, great iron-ring mountains, and the ten treasure mountains—among all mountains, Mount Sumeru is first—this Lotus Sutra is like that. Among all sutras, it's the highest.

"And just like among all the stars, the moon-child is first—this Lotus Sutra is like that. Among thousands of millions of billions of kinds of sutra-dharmas, it's the most illuminating.

"And just like the sun-child can drive out all darkness—this sutra is like that. It can break all the darkness of what ain't good.

"And just like among all the little kings, the Wheel-Turning Sacred King is first—this sutra is like that. Among all sutras, it's the most honored.

"And just like Indra is king among the thirty-three gods—this sutra is like that. It's king among all sutras.

"And just like Great Brahma Heaven King is the father of all living beings—this sutra is like that. It's the father of all worthy saints, all learners, all who completed learning, and all who brought forth the bodhisattva mind.

"And just like among all ordinary people, the stream-enterer, the once-returner, the non-returner, the arhat, and the pratyekabuddha are first—this sutra is like that. Among all sutras preached by Thus Come Ones, or preached by bodhisattvas, or preached by disciples—among all sutra-dharmas, it's the very first.

"If someone can receive and keep this sutra, they're like that too—among all living beings, they're the very first.

"Among all disciples and pratyekabuddhas, the bodhisattvas are first. This sutra is like that—among all sutra-dharmas, it's the very first.

"Just like the Buddha is king of all dharmas, this sutra is like that—it's king among all sutras."

## PART THIRTEEN: THE MEDICINE FOR EVERYTHING

"Constellation King Flower, this sutra can rescue all living beings. This sutra can cause all living beings to leave all suffering and distress. This sutra can greatly benefit all living beings, filling up their wishes.

"It's like a clear cool pool that can satisfy all those who thirsty and parched.

"Like someone cold getting fire.

"Like someone naked getting clothes.

"Like a merchant caravan finding a leader.

"Like a child finding its mother.

"Like someone crossing water finding a boat.

"Like someone sick finding a doctor.

"Like someone in darkness finding a lamp.

"Like someone poor finding treasure.

"Like people finding a king.

"Like a merchant ship reaching the sea.

"Like a torch driving out darkness.

"This Lotus Sutra is like that. It can cause living beings to leave all suffering, all sickness and pain. It can loosen all the bonds of birth and death.

"If a person gets to hear this Lotus Sutra—whether they copy it themselves or have someone else copy it—the merit they get, even if you measure it with the Buddha's wisdom, you can't find the edge of it.

"If someone copies this sutra scroll and makes offerings with flowers, incense, necklaces, burning incense, powdered incense, anointing incense, banners, canopies, robes, and all kinds of lamps—butter lamps, oil lamps, all kinds of fragrant oil lamps, jasmine oil lamps, sumana flower oil lamps, pāṭala oil lamps, vārṣika oil lamps, navamallikā oil lamps—making offerings like that, the merit they get is also measureless."

## PART FOURTEEN: THE PROMISE TO WOMEN - YOU WON'T BE WOMEN NO MORE

"Constellation King Flower, if someone hears this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva, they also get measureless, boundless merit.

"If a WOMAN hears this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva and can receive and keep it—when she finishes this female body, she won't receive a female body ever again.

"If, after the Thus Come One's extinction, in the last five hundred years, if a woman hears this sutra and practices according to what it says—when this life ends, she'll go straight to the Land of Peace and Joy, to where Amitābha Buddha and the great bodhisattvas surround and dwell. She'll be born in a lotus flower, on a jeweled seat.

"She won't be troubled no more by greed and desire.

"She won't be troubled no more by anger and stupidity.

"She won't be troubled no more by arrogance, jealousy, and all the other defilements.

"She'll get the bodhisattvas' supernatural powers and the Tolerance of Non-arising of Dharmas.

"Once she gets that tolerance, her eye-faculty will become pure. With that pure eye-faculty, she'll see seven hundred twenty billion nayuta times the sands of the Ganges River worth of Thus Come One Buddhas.

## PART FIFTEEN: ALL THE BUDDHAS PRAISE HER FROM FAR AWAY

"At that time, all those Buddhas will praise her from far away, saying:

'GOOD! GOOD! GOOD, good son! You were able to receive and keep and read and recite and think about this sutra in the Dharma of Śākyamuni Buddha, and you explained it to others. The merit you got—measureless, boundless. Fire can't burn it. Water can't wash it away. Your merit—a thousand Buddhas speaking together couldn't finish telling it.

'You already broke all the demon thieves. You destroyed the army of birth and death. All the other enemies—you smashed every one.

'Good son, hundreds of thousands of Buddhas, with their supernatural power, together guard and protect you. In all the worlds, among gods and humans, there's nobody like you. Except for the Thus Come One himself, among all the disciples, all the pratyekabuddhas, even among the bodhisattvas—in wisdom and meditation concentration, nobody equals you.'

"Constellation King Flower, this bodhisattva accomplished the power of merit and wisdom just like that."

## PART SIXTEEN: THE FRAGRANCE THEY CARRY

"If someone hears this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva and can rejoice and praise it—from that person's mouth will constantly come the fragrance of blue lotus flowers. From their body's pores will constantly come the fragrance of ox-head sandalwood. The merit they get is like what I already said.

"That's why, Constellation King Flower, I'm entrusting this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva to you. After my extinction, in the last five hundred years, you should spread it widely throughout this southern continent. Don't let it get cut off. Don't let evil demons, demon people, gods, dragons, night-wanderers, or kumbhāṇḍa spirits get their chance at it."

## PART SEVENTEEN: THIS SUTRA IS MEDICINE - DON'T GET OLD, DON'T DIE

"Constellation King Flower, you should use your supernatural powers to guard and protect this sutra. Why? Because this sutra is good medicine for the people of this southern continent when they sick.

"If a person has a disease and gets to hear this sutra, their disease will disappear and be gone. They won't get old. They won't die.

## PART EIGHTEEN: WHAT TO DO WHEN YOU SEE SOMEONE KEEPING THIS SUTRA

"Constellation King Flower, if you see someone who receives and keeps this sutra, you should take blue lotus flowers, fill them with powdered incense, and scatter them over that person. After you scatter them, you should think like this:

'This person before long—won't be long now—they gonna take the grass and sit down at the place of enlightenment. They gonna break all the demon armies. They gonna blow the Dharma conch. They gonna beat the great Dharma drum. They gonna ferry across all living beings from the ocean of old age, sickness, and death.'

"That's why—anyone seeking the Buddha Way, when they see someone receiving and keeping this sutra, they should bring forth a respectful mind just like that toward them."

## PART NINETEEN: WHAT HAPPENED WHEN HE TOLD THIS STORY

When the Buddha told this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva, eighty-four thousand bodhisattvas attained the dhāraṇī for understanding the languages of all living beings.

Many Treasures Thus Come One, there in his treasure stupa, praised Constellation King Flower Bodhisattva, saying:

"GOOD! GOOD! Constellation King Flower, you accomplished inconceivable merit. You were able to ask Śākyamuni Buddha about matters like this, benefiting measureless, all living beings."

## INTERPRETATION NOTES:

## **ON THE MEANING OF BURNING/SACRIFICE IN BUDDHIST AND BLUES/GOSPEL CONTEXTS**

This chapter contains some of the most extreme imagery in all of Buddhist literature: a bodhisattva who saturates his body with fragrant oils for 1,200 years, then sets himself on fire and burns for another 1,200 years. Later, he burns both his arms for 72,000 years.

In the Buddhist context, this represents *total self-offering* as the ultimate form of generosity (dāna-pāramitā). The text explicitly says that offering flowers, incense, treasures, even kingdoms and family members "cannot match" offering one's own body. This isn't about physical pain or destruction—it's about the willingness to give up everything, to hold nothing back, in service of the Dharma.

The burning body produces *light* that illuminates eighty billion worlds. This transforms the act from destruction into illumination. The bodhisattva becomes a lamp, a torch that lights up reality for countless beings. His sacrifice becomes revelation.

In blues and gospel traditions, fire carries similar meanings: *purification, intensity, transformation, and presence*. When the blues talks about "house on fire," when gospel singers cry "This little light of mine," when spiritual songs speak of "walking through the fire"—they're pointing to the same territory. Fire purifies but also reveals. It burns away what's false and shows what's true.

The blues knows about sacrifice that seems crazy to outsiders. It knows about loving something so much you'll give everything for it—your comfort, your safety, your very life. "I'd rather drink muddy water, sleep in a hollow log, than stay in this town, treated like a dirty dog"—that's the blues talking about what you'll do when truth demands it.

Gospel knows this even more directly: "If I perish, I perish." "Take up your cross and follow." The willingness to be consumed utterly if that's what love requires. This chapter takes that to the cosmic level—the bodhisattva burns not in despair but in offering, not in pain but in light.

What makes this Buddhist and not just passionate sacrifice: *the burning produces wisdom for others*. The light reaches eighty billion worlds. Countless beings attain enlightenment because they witness this. It's not about the individual's heroism—it's about the teaching that sacrifice makes visible.

## **ON THE VOW AND THE RESTORATION OF THE ARMS: FAITH HAS POWER**

The most dramatic moment in the chapter: Medicine King burns his arms as offerings for 72,000 years. When the assembly sees him armless, they cry out: "His body is incomplete!"

But Medicine King stands before them all and makes a vow: "I gave up both my arms. I WILL attain the Buddha's golden body. If that's real—if that's true and not a lie—then let my two arms come back just like they were!"

And they do. Instantly. Naturally. Perfectly restored.

The text says: "This happened because of the deep merit, the thick virtue, the profound wisdom of that bodhisattva."

This is about *the power of vow* (praṇidhāna). Not wishful thinking. Not bargaining with the universe. But the recognition that if you're truly aligned with the way things really are, if your commitment is total, if your realization is genuine—then the universe itself responds.

In gospel terms: "Faith can move mountains." "According to your faith, be it unto you." The idea that radical trust in truth has creative power. That when you stake everything on what's real, reality itself confirms it.

The blues knows this too, though it usually talks about it from the other side—when the vow is broken, when the promise turns false, when faith gets betrayed. But even in that negative witness, the blues testifies: *promises matter, vows have power, what you commit to shapes what happens*.

Medicine King's restoration of his arms isn't magic or miracle—it's demonstration. It shows that his sacrifice was genuine, his realization was real, his attainment of the samādhi was not empty. The complete body returns because *he already had it*. The Buddha-nature was never incomplete. The burning never destroyed anything essential.

At that moment, the three-thousand great thousandfold world shakes six ways. Heaven rains treasure flowers. Everyone experiences what they've never experienced before. Why? Because they witnessed something true: that total giving doesn't diminish you, that sacrifice for truth doesn't destroy you, that the Dharma-body is indestructible even when the physical body is completely offered.

## **ON THE PROMISE OF WOMEN'S LIBERATION: RADICAL INCLUSION THROUGH RADICAL TEACHING**

The Buddha makes an extraordinary statement near the end of this chapter:

"If a WOMAN hears this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva and can receive and keep it—when she finishes this female body, she won't receive a female body ever again."

And more: she'll be born in Amitābha's Pure Land, surrounded by great bodhisattvas, born in a lotus flower on a jeweled seat. She'll gain supernatural powers, attain the Tolerance of Non-arising of Dharmas, see 720 billion nayuta Ganges-sands-worth of Buddhas, and be praised by all of them.

This needs historical context: In the patriarchal societies of ancient India and China, women faced systemic religious, social, and spiritual barriers. Many Buddhist texts from that period contain problematic statements about women's spiritual capacity. The "five obstacles" doctrine claimed women couldn't become certain types of enlightened beings.

But the Lotus Sutra repeatedly challenges these limitations. Chapter 12 features the Dragon King's daughter attaining Buddhahood instantly. And here, Chapter 23 promises that *simply hearing this chapter and keeping it* liberates women from the female body permanently.

Why this chapter specifically? Because it's about *total commitment*. Medicine King doesn't hold anything back. He doesn't calculate or compromise. He gives everything—body, arms, everything—for the Dharma. The text is saying: If a woman hears this teaching about ultimate commitment and takes it into her heart, *that level of commitment itself transforms everything*.

The promise isn't "if you burn your own body, you'll escape being a woman." The promise is "if you hear this teaching and keep it"—meaning, if you receive it, hold it, let it work in you, practice according to it.

In blues/gospel terms, this is liberation theology. This is "There is neither Jew nor Greek, slave nor free, male nor female—for you are all one." This is "The last shall be first." This is the promise that radical truth-commitment breaks all the world's chains.

The text even emphasizes: she won't be troubled by greed, anger, stupidity, arrogance, jealousy, or defilements. These aren't gendered qualities—they're human afflictions. By escaping the "female body," the text means escaping the entire system of limitation, discrimination, and suffering that the patriarchal world imposed on women's bodies and women's possibilities.

When the 720 billion Buddhas praise her, they call her "good son" (善男子)—the standard term for a male practitioner. But they're praising a woman who practiced as a woman and was liberated as a woman. The term "good son" here reveals its own emptiness—it shows that these gender categories were always provisional, always empty, always just temporary designations for beings whose true nature is Buddha-nature.

This is the Lotus Sutra doing what it does: taking the conventional hierarchies and turning them inside out. Not by denying difference, but by showing that ultimate reality—Buddha-nature, Dharma-body, true self—transcends all these conventional distinctions while working right through them.

## **ON FRAGRANCE AS SIGN OF TRANSFORMATION**

Throughout this chapter, fragrance signals transformation:

1. Medicine King drinks fragrant oils for 1,200 years until his whole body is saturated
2. When he burns, the light that emerges carries fragrance—it's not just visual but olfactory illumination
3. Those who hear this chapter and rejoice: "from their mouth will constantly come the fragrance of blue lotus flowers; from their body's pores will constantly come the fragrance of ox-head sandalwood"

Fragrance in Buddhist symbolism represents *virtue that emanates naturally*. You can't fake fragrance. You can't will it into being. It arises from the substance itself—from the saturated body, from the pure practice, from the genuine realization.

The specific fragrances matter:
- **Sandalwood** (candana): cooling, calming, used in meditation and offerings. Represents the calming of afflictions.
- **Blue lotus** (utpala): blooms at night, represents enlightenment in the midst of darkness/samsara
- **Ox-head sandalwood**: the most precious type, from the Malaya mountains, used only for the most honored purposes

When someone's mouth emanates blue lotus fragrance and their pores emanate sandalwood fragrance, it means *their whole being has become an offering*. They don't just *make* offerings—they *are* an offering. Their very presence benefits others.

In blues/gospel terms, this is about authenticity so complete it changes your smell. "I know you been in the water, because your clothes smell wet." "I can tell you been with Jesus—you smell like mercy." The idea that transformation goes all the way down, all the way through, until even your scent testifies to where you've been and what you've touched.

Fragrance also travels. You can smell something before you see it, after it's gone. Fragrance pervades space, crosses boundaries, enters intimately. The fact that these practitioners emanate fragrance means *their merit spreads without effort*. They don't have to preach—their very presence communicates.

 internal transformation → spontaneous emanation → transmitted blessing.

## **ON THE PRAISES OF THE DHARMA: SUPREME, ALL-ENCOMPASSING, ILLUMINATING**

The middle section of the chapter contains a long series of analogies proclaiming the Lotus Sutra's supremacy:

- Like the ocean among all waters
- Like Mount Sumeru among all mountains
- Like the moon among all stars
- Like the sun dispelling darkness
- Like the Wheel-Turning Sacred King among rulers
- Like Indra among gods
- Like Brahma as father of all beings
- King of all sutras

This isn't mere boasting. It's pointing to the *all-encompassing, all-including* nature of this teaching. The Lotus Sutra claims to be supreme precisely because it *includes and fulfills* all other teachings. It's the ocean because all rivers flow into it. It's Mount Sumeru because it's the axis that holds all the worlds together.

Each analogy emphasizes a different quality:
- **Ocean**: deepest, receives all waters, contains all treasures
- **Sumeru**: highest, most stable, center of the cosmos
- **Moon**: illuminates darkness, waxes to fullness, reflects the sun
- **Sun**: dispels all darkness, life-giving, all-seeing
- **Wheel-Turning King**: rules through Dharma not force, brings peace
- **Father**: generates, nurtures, provides for all

The claim is that this sutra:
1. Receives all teachings (ocean)
2. Provides stable ground (Sumeru)
3. Illuminates gradually (moon)
4. Reveals completely (sun)
5. Unifies through truth (king)
6. Generates enlightenment (father)

In blues/gospel terms, this is like when a song says "This is the real thing—this ain't no imitation love, this ain't no substitute grace, this ain't no partial salvation—this is the whole thing, the complete thing, the truth that sets you free all the way."

The text then immediately pivots from these cosmic claims to practical promises: This sutra is like cool water to the thirsty, fire to the cold, clothes to the naked, a mother to a lost child, a boat to someone crossing water, medicine to the sick, light to those in darkness, treasure to the poor.

The grandest cosmic claims are also the most intimate practical aids. It's supreme in the ultimate sense AND it's medicine for right now. This is characteristic of the Lotus Sutra—it holds together the absolute and the relative, the cosmic and the personal, the eternal and the urgent.

## **ON THE MEDICINE METAPHOR: HEALING, LIFE, IMMORTALITY**

The chapter's title character is *Medicine King* (藥王, Bhaiṣajya-rāja). The Buddha says near the end: "This sutra is good medicine for the people of this southern continent when they sick."

Then: "If a person has a disease and gets to hear this sutra, their disease will disappear and be gone. They won't get old. They won't die."

This seems like extravagant metaphor until you realize what "disease," "old age," and "death" mean in Buddhist terms:

- Disease = all the afflictions (kleśa): greed, anger, ignorance, arrogance, jealousy, etc.
- Old age = samsaric existence, the aging of all conditioned things
- Death = the cycle of birth and death driven by karma and ignorance

"Not old, not die" = liberation from samsara, attainment of the deathless (amṛta), realization of the unborn and undying Buddha-nature.

So the sutra is medicine in a precise sense: it cures the disease of ignorance, stops the aging of samsaric existence, and ends the cycle of death and rebirth.

 to simple hearing as liberation.

This reveals the teaching's deep structure: What Medicine King demonstrated through literal body-offering, others can realize through faith and understanding. His burning body *showed* what it means to hold nothing back. His restored arms *proved* the indestructibility of what's real. His story *is the medicine*—not because it prescribes self-immolation, but because it reveals the nature of complete commitment and its fruition.

In blues/gospel terms: "There is a balm in Gilead, to make the wounded whole. There is a balm in Gilead, to heal the sin-sick soul." The idea that there's medicine for what really ails you—not just surface symptoms but the deep sickness. And that medicine is *truth-teaching plus commitment to it*.

The blues often talks about "bad blood," "mojo," "root work"—the idea that something's wrong deep down and needs real medicine, not superficial cures. This chapter says: the deepest sickness is not knowing who you really are, spending yourself on what doesn't last, holding back from what's real. The medicine is this teaching, received and kept, which reveals Buddha-nature and frees you into your true life.

## **ON COMMITMENT TO TRUTH EMBODIED IN THE BODY ITSELF**

The chapter's central paradox: Medicine King achieves the highest spiritual realization by destroying his physical body, yet his body is restored, and he's reborn in a king's house, and he continues practicing and teaching.

This isn't body-denial or body-hatred. This is body-transcendence-through-body-offering. The body is neither clung to nor rejected—it's *used*, *given*, *offered* as the vehicle for demonstrating truth.

When Medicine King drinks fragrant oils for 1,200 years, he's not torturing his body—he's transforming it. Making every cell into an offering. The body becomes saturated with practice, soaked through with intention, until it's ready to become pure light.

When he burns, he's not escaping the body—he's *using* it maximally. Turning it into illumination for eighty billion worlds. Making his flesh into teaching.

When his arms burn, it's not self-mutilation—it's ultimate generosity. Arms represent *action, grasping, making, doing*. To burn the arms is to offer all your doing, all your making, all your grasping and holding.

And when the arms restore, it shows: the offering was complete, so nothing was truly lost. What's given fully comes back fuller. The willing sacrifice of everything leads to perfect restoration.

This is profound body theology. Not spirit versus body. Not transcendence through body-denial. But *transcendence through complete body-offering*. The body as the place where truth is enacted, demonstrated, proven.

In blues/gospel terms: "This body ain't my home, I'm just passing through"—but also: "This body is the temple where the spirit lives." Both true at once. You're not your body, but your body is where you do the work. You'll leave this body behind, but while you're in it, it's holy ground.

When the blues singer says "Every nerve in my body hurting me"—that's the body as the place where truth registers. When the gospel shout makes people move, sway, dance, catch the spirit—that's the body as the instrument of realization.

Medicine King shows: your body is your first and last offering. Not because it's all you have, but because it's the most immediate expression of what you are. How you use this body, what you do with this flesh, what you're willing to give—that's where commitment becomes visible.

The chapter ends with the instruction: When you see someone keeping this sutra, scatter flowers over them and think, "This person will soon sit under the Bodhi tree, break the demon armies, blow the Dharma conch, beat the Dharma drum, and ferry all beings across the ocean of birth, old age, sickness, and death."

That's what Medicine King did. That's what his story teaches. That's what becomes possible when commitment to truth is complete—embodied, enacted, offered through this very body, right here, right now.

---
## FINAL WORD

---

This chapter asks: What would you give for the truth?

Medicine King's answer: Everything. My body. My arms. My comfort. My life. Everything.

And the teaching says: That total giving is what reveals the total truth. The complete offering is what manifests complete liberation.

But then—and this is the grace of it—the teaching says: You don't have to burn your body. If a woman even *hears* this story and keeps it, she's liberated. If someone *rejoices* in hearing it, fragrance comes from their mouth and pores.

The extreme example reveals the principle: Hold nothing back. The accessible practice applies the principle: Hear, keep, rejoice, practice.

Between Medicine King's burning and your hearing, the whole path is contained. The body on fire and the ear receiving teaching—both complete expressions of one truth.

This is the Lotus Sutra being the Lotus Sutra: Show the ultimate to reveal the immediate. Tell the impossible story to make the possible practice visible. Light up eighty billion worlds so you can see the ground beneath your feet.

Medicine King burned for 1,200 years. You can start right now. Same teaching. Same liberation. Same truth.

The fire's already burning. The question is: What are you gonna feed it?

---
### END OF CHAPTER TWENTY-THREE: MEDICINE KING BODHISATTVA - THE STORY OF HOW HE GAVE IT ALL

---

## INTERPRETATION NOTES ON CHAPTER 23

## **ON THE MEANING OF BURNING/SACRIFICE IN BUDDHIST AND BLUES/GOSPEL CONTEXTS**

This chapter contains some of the most extreme imagery in all of Buddhist literature: a bodhisattva who saturates his body with fragrant oils for 1,200 years, then sets himself on fire and burns for another 1,200 years. Later, he burns both his arms for 72,000 years.

In the Buddhist context, this represents *total self-offering* as the ultimate form of generosity (dāna-pāramitā). The burning body produces *light* that illuminates eighty billion worlds. This transforms the act from destruction into illumination. The bodhisattva becomes a lamp, a torch that lights up reality for countless beings. His sacrifice becomes revelation.

The blues knows about sacrifice that seems crazy to outsiders. It knows about loving something so much you'll give everything for it—your comfort, your safety, your very life. This chapter takes that to the cosmic level—the bodhisattva burns not in despair but in offering, not in pain but in light.

What makes this Buddhist and not just passionate sacrifice: *the burning produces wisdom for others*. The light reaches eighty billion worlds. Countless beings attain enlightenment because they witness this. It's not about the individual's heroism—it's about the teaching that sacrifice makes visible.

## **ON THE VOW AND THE RESTORATION OF THE ARMS: FAITH HAS POWER**

But Medicine King stands before them all and makes a vow: "I gave up both my arms. I WILL attain the Buddha's golden body. If that's real—if that's true and not a lie—then let my two arms come back just like they were!"

And they do. Instantly. Naturally. Perfectly restored.

This is about *the power of vow* (praṇidhāna). Not wishful thinking. Not bargaining with the universe. But the recognition that if you're truly aligned with the way things really are, if your commitment is total, if your realization is genuine—then the universe itself responds.

Medicine King's restoration of his arms isn't magic or miracle—it's demonstration. It shows that his sacrifice was genuine, his realization was real, his attainment of the samadhi was not empty. The complete body returns because *he already had it*. The Buddha-nature was never incomplete. The burning never destroyed anything essential.

## **ON THE PROMISE OF WOMEN'S LIBERATION: RADICAL INCLUSION THROUGH RADICAL TEACHING**

The Buddha makes an extraordinary statement near the end of this chapter:

"If a WOMAN hears this Chapter on the Past Deeds of Medicine King Bodhisattva and can receive and keep it—when she finishes this female body, she won't receive a female body ever again."

And more: she'll be born in Amitābha's Pure Land, surrounded by great bodhisattvas, born in a lotus flower on a jeweled seat.

This needs historical context: In the patriarchal societies of ancient India and China, women faced systemic religious, social, and spiritual barriers. But the Lotus Sutra repeatedly challenges these limitations.

Why this chapter specifically? Because it's about *total commitment*. Medicine King doesn't hold anything back. He doesn't calculate or compromise. He gives everything—body, arms, everything—for the Dharma. The text is saying: If a woman hears this teaching about ultimate commitment and takes it into her heart, *that level of commitment itself transforms everything*.

The promise isn't "if you burn your own body, you'll escape being a woman." The promise is "if you hear this teaching and keep it"—meaning, if you receive it, hold it, let it work in you, practice according to it.

In blues/gospel terms, this is liberation theology. This is the promise that radical truth-commitment breaks all the world's chains.

## **ON FRAGRANCE AS SIGN OF TRANSFORMATION**

Throughout this chapter, fragrance signals transformation: Medicine King drinks fragrant oils for 1,200 years until his whole body is saturated. When he burns, the light that emerges carries fragrance. Those who hear this chapter and rejoice emanate fragrance from their mouths and pores.

Fragrance in Buddhist symbolism represents *virtue that emanates naturally*. You can't fake fragrance. You can't will it into being. It arises from the substance itself—from the saturated body, from the pure practice, from the genuine realization.

When someone's mouth emanates blue lotus fragrance and their pores emanate sandalwood fragrance, it means *their whole being has become an offering*. They don't just *make* offerings—they *are* an offering. Their very presence benefits others.

In blues/gospel terms, this is about authenticity so complete it changes your smell. "I know you been in the water, because your clothes smell wet." The idea that transformation goes all the way down, all the way through, until even your scent testifies to where you've been and what you've touched.

## **ON THE MEDICINE METAPHOR: HEALING, LIFE, IMMORTALITY**

The chapter's title character is *Medicine King* (藥王). The Buddha says: "This sutra is good medicine for the people of this southern continent when they sick."

Then: "If a person has a disease and gets to hear this sutra, their disease will disappear and be gone. They won't get old. They won't die."

In Buddhist terms:
- Disease = all the afflictions (kleśa): greed, anger, ignorance, arrogance, jealousy, etc.
- Old age = samsaric existence, the aging of all conditioned things
- Death = the cycle of birth and death driven by karma and ignorance

"Not old, not die" = liberation from samsara, attainment of the deathless (amṛta), realization of the unborn and undying Buddha-nature.
